[٢] قوله : يكون إسقاطاً لوصف الحلول ؛ وصار كأنه أجل نفس الحق ، إذ لا يمكن جعله معاوضة ؛ لأنّ بيع الدراهم والدنانير بمثلها نسيئة لا يجوز.
Chapter on Settlement of Debt. And his settlement on some of the same genus of what is owed to him is taking some of his right and dropping the rest, not a exchange. So it is valid for a thousand due immediately in exchange for a hundred immediate, or for a thousand deferred, or for a thousand good coins in exchange for a hundred inferior coins. And it is not valid for dirhams in exchange for deferred dinars. [1] His statement: 'And his settlement...etc.' This is the beginning of the discussion on settlement of debt, which is what is established in the dhimmah. He delayed it after the settlement on general claims because specifics always come after generalities. The form of this issue: if the settlement amount is of the same genus as what the plaintiff is owed from the defendant through a prior loan contract, then the settlement is taking some of his right and dropping the rest, so it should not be treated as an exchange. Because the transaction of a rational person must be validated as much as possible, and it cannot be validated as an exchange because it would lead to riba, so it is considered a dropping of part, carrying the affairs of Muslims on the basis of validity as much as possible. The apparent situation indicates this because he seeks validity rather than corruption, since his intellect and religion prevent him from committing a prohibited act. He only said 'on some of the same genus of what he is owed' because if it is not of the same genus, it would be an exchange. In his statement 'dropping the rest' there is an indication that it is not permissible to settle for more than the genus of his right because that would be riba. [2] His statement: 'it becomes a dropping of the description of being due immediately' and it is as if he postponed the right itself, because it cannot be made as an exchange since selling dirhams and dinars for their like on credit is not permissible.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage explains the chapter on settlement of debt. The key principle is that when the settlement amount is of the same genus as the debt, it is not considered a sale but rather a partial remission of the debt. This classification avoids the prohibition of riba, since a sale of money for money on credit or with inequality is not allowed. Therefore, settling a debt of 1000 immediate for 100 immediate is valid, as it is a remission of 900. Similarly, settling for 1000 deferred is a remission of the right to immediate payment. Settling for 100 inferior coins instead of 1000 good coins is a remission of 900 and a downgrade of quality. However, settling dirhams for dinars on credit is invalid because it is a sale of one currency for another on credit, which is prohibited. The passage also notes that possession of the settlement amount is not required for validity in these cases. The underlying reasoning is based on istiḥsān (juristic preference) to avoid riba and to preserve the validity of transactions.

Key Terms
صلح (ṣulḥ): A transaction to settle a dispute or debt by mutual agreement.
إسقاط (isqāṭ): Remission or dropping of a right, in this case the remainder of the debt.
ربًا (ribā): Usury or illegal increase in exchange of certain items (gold, silver, foodstuffs).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Settling a debt of 1000 dirhams by accepting 1200 dirhams immediately.
D) Settling a debt of 1000 dirhams by accepting 900 dirhams deferred.

Key Takeaway
When a debt is settled with a portion of the same genus, it is a valid remission (isqat) of the balance, not a sale, thus avoiding riba.

Valid Compromise on Debt: Same Genus Forgiveness
Valid forms of compromise when substitute is same genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the conditions under which a compromise on a debt is valid as a partial forgiveness (isqat) rather than an exchange (mu'awada).

Core Idea
A compromise on a debt is valid as forgiveness when the substitute is of the same genus as the debt.

When a creditor and debtor agree to settle a debt for a lesser amount or different terms, the Hanafi school categorizes this as either forgiveness (isqat) or exchange (mu'awada). The determining factor is whether the substitute given is of the same genus (jins) as the original debt. If it is, the compromise is valid as a partial dropping of the debt; if not, it becomes an exchange and must comply with strict rules to avoid riba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debt and substitute
Determine the genus (e.g., dirhams, dinars, wheat) of the original debt and the substitute offered in compromise.
2
Check genus equality
If the substitute is of the same genus as the debt, the compromise can be construed as forgiveness. If not, it is an exchange.
Same genus means both are dirhams (even if different quality/deferred status) or both are dinars.
3
Verify no mutual benefit
If only the creditor benefits (by dropping part of the debt) and the debtor gives nothing extra, it is forgiveness. If both parties give up something (e.g., creditor drops part, debtor gives up deferment), it becomes an exchange.
4
Apply the ruling
If it is forgiveness, the compromise is valid and immediate possession of the substitute is not required. If it is an exchange, it must follow the rules of sarf (spot transaction) or sale, depending on genus.

Do you not see that riba al-nasi'ah is prohibited, and there is nothing in it except the exchange of wealth for the term by way of ambiguity; so that it is prohibited in its reality is prior. The principle in this: when beneficence occurs from both sides, it is construed as an exchange, like this issue; for the creditor dropped five hundred of his right, and the debtor dropped his right, which is the term, from the remaining five hundred, so it becomes an exchange. Contrary to what preceded of compromise on a thousand for five hundred; for it is construed as dropping part of the right without exchange, because beneficence only occurred from the side of the creditor alone. [3] His statement: 'or for a thousand black...' etc.; meaning if he had a thousand black dirhams and he compromised with him for five hundred white dirhams, this compromise is not valid. What is meant by black dirhams is those in which the silver is more than the alloy. He stated it explicitly in 'Jami' al-Mudmarat' and others. (1) i.e. there is no basis for its validity except exchange, and selling dirhams for dinars on credit is not allowed; it cannot be construed as a delay because dinars are not entitled by the debt contract. Commentary
This passage from the chapter on compromise in debt (sulh fi al-dayn) explains the distinction between valid and invalid forms of settling a debt through compromise. The key principle is that if the substitute in the compromise is of the same genus as the original debt, it is considered a partial forgiveness (isqat) and not an exchange (mu'awada). This is because taking part of a thing as substitute for the whole is not a valid exchange, but as forgiveness it is valid. Therefore, if someone owes 1000 current dirhams and agrees to accept 100 current dirhams in settlement, it is valid as forgiveness of 900. Similarly, if the debt is 1000 currently due and he accepts 1000 deferred, it is forgiveness of the attribute of being currently due, which is valid. Also, if the debt is 1000 high quality dirhams and he accepts 100 low quality, it is forgiveness of part and the quality attribute. In these cases, the compromise is valid and immediate possession of the substitute is not required.

However, if the substitute is not of the same genus (e.g., dirhams for dinars), it becomes an exchange (mu'awada) and is subject to the rules of currency exchange (sarf). Therefore, if the compromise is on dirhams for deferred dinars, it is invalid because it involves credit in currency exchange, which is prohibited. Similarly, if the compromise is on a deferred debt of 1000 for half of it currently due, the immediate payment is not part of the original debt (which was deferred), so the debtor gives up his right to the term in exchange for shedding part of the debt, making it an exchange for the term, which is considered riba. Likewise, if the debt is in black dirhams (low quality silver) and he accepts white dirhams (high quality), it is an exchange of dirhams with variation in quality, which is only valid spot, not deferred; thus the compromise is invalid unless immediate possession occurs.

The commentary explains the reasoning: the debtor's term (deferment) is a right that he can forgo, but if he gives it up in return for a reduction in debt, it becomes a sale of deferment, which is prohibited. The principle is that if the benefit (ihsan) is only from one side (the creditor forgiving part), it is forgiveness; if both sides give up something, it is an exchange.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): Compromise or settlement of a claim by mutual agreement.
حط (hatt): To drop or forgive part of the debt.
معاوضة (mu'awada): Exchange, a transaction where one party gives something in return for another.
حلول (halul): The attribute of being currently due or immediately payable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a compromise on a debt considered an exchange (mu'awada) rather than forgiveness (isqat)?
A) When the substitute is of the same genus as the original debt.
B) When the substitute is of a different genus than the original debt.
C) When the creditor forgives part of the debt without receiving anything in return.
D) When the debtor pays the full amount immediately.

Key Takeaway
A compromise on a debt is valid as forgiveness when the substitute is of the same genus, preserving validity and avoiding riba.

Invalid Compromise: Different Genus and Compensation for Time
Invalid forms of compromise when different genus or mutual benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why compromises involving a different genus or where both parties give up something (compensation for deferment) are invalid according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A compromise becomes invalid when it involves an exchange of a different genus on credit or when the debtor gives up the deferment term in exchange for a reduction in debt.

Not all compromises on debts are valid. The Hanafi school identifies two key situations that lead to invalidity: first, when the substitute is of a different genus and the transaction is deferred (e.g., dirhams for deferred dinars), which falls under prohibited riba al-nasi'ah; second, when both parties give up something, such as the creditor dropping part of the debt and the debtor giving up the right to deferment. The latter is considered a sale of time, which is also prohibited.

Guided Path
1
Identify whether the substitute is of a different genus
If the substitute is not of the same genus as the debt (e.g., dirhams for dinars), the compromise is an exchange (mu'awada). Then check if it is deferred.
2
Check for deferred payment in different genus exchanges
If the exchange of different genera is deferred (e.g., dirhams now for dinars later), it is invalid because it violates the rules of sarf (spot exchange).
Sarf requires immediate possession (qabd) before separation.
3
Check for mutual benefit (both parties give up something)
Even if the substitute is of the same genus, if the debtor gives up the attribute of deferment (ta'jil) in return for a reduction in debt, both parties benefit, making it an exchange for time, which is invalid.
The text's example: thousand deferred for half currently due – debtor gives up deferment, creditor drops half.
4
Apply the ruling
If either condition (different genus deferred or mutual benefit) is present, the compromise is invalid. If it is same genus and only creditor benefits, it is valid.

Contrary to what preceded of compromise on a thousand for five hundred; for it is construed as dropping part of the right without exchange, because beneficence only occurred from the side of the creditor alone. Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on compromise in debt (sulh fi al-dayn) explains the distinction between valid and invalid forms of settling a debt through compromise. The key principle is that if the substitute in the compromise is of the same genus as the original debt, it is considered a partial forgiveness (isqat) and not an exchange (mu'awada). This is because taking part of a thing as substitute for the whole is not a valid exchange, but as forgiveness it is valid. Therefore, if someone owes 1000 current dirhams and agrees to accept 100 current dirhams in settlement, it is valid as forgiveness of 900. Similarly, if the debt is 1000 currently due and he accepts 1000 deferred, it is forgiveness of the attribute of being currently due, which is valid. Also, if the debt is 1000 high quality dirhams and he accepts 100 low quality, it is forgiveness of part and the quality attribute. In these cases, the compromise is valid and immediate possession of the substitute is not required.

However, if the substitute is not of the same genus (e.g., dirhams for dinars), it becomes an exchange (mu'awada) and is subject to the rules of currency exchange (sarf). Therefore, if the compromise is on dirhams for deferred dinars, it is invalid because it involves credit in currency exchange, which is prohibited. Similarly, if the compromise is on a deferred debt of 1000 for half of it currently due, the immediate payment is not part of the original debt (which was deferred), so the debtor gives up his right to the term in exchange for shedding part of the debt, making it an exchange for the term, which is considered riba. Likewise, if the debt is in black dirhams (low quality silver) and he accepts white dirhams (high quality), it is an exchange of dirhams with variation in quality, which is only valid spot, not deferred; thus the compromise is invalid unless immediate possession occurs.

The commentary explains the reasoning: the debtor's term (deferment) is a right that he can forgo, but if he gives it up in return for a reduction in debt, it becomes a sale of deferment, which is prohibited. The principle is that if the benefit (ihsan) is only from one side (the creditor forgiving part), it is forgiveness; if both sides give up something, it is an exchange.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): Deferment period; the time until the debt is due.
صرف (sarf): Currency exchange transaction.
ربا النسيئة (riba al-nasi'ah): Interest due to deferment in exchange of ribawi items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is compromising a deferred debt of 1000 for 500 currently due invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the substitute (500) is of a different genus.
B) Because both parties give up something: the creditor drops 500, and the debtor drops the right to deferment, making it an exchange for time.
C) Because the debtor must pay the full amount.
D) Because the compromise must be notarized.

Key Takeaway
A compromise on a debt is invalid if it involves deferred exchange of different genera or mutual benefit where the debtor gives up a right (like deferment) in exchange for a reduction.

Dispute over 'على' in Debt Settlement: Condition vs Compensation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on 'على' in settlement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over the meaning of 'على' in a settlement and state the practical consequence of each interpretation.

Core Idea
A single ambiguous word in Arabic can change whether a debt is conditionally or absolutely discharged.

This passage presents a disagreement within the Hanafi school on how to interpret the word 'على' (on/upon) in a settlement agreement. The creditor says: 'I release you from 500 on condition that you pay me 500.' Does 'على' create a condition (so release is contingent on payment) or does it indicate compensation (so release is absolute)? Abu Yusuf argues for compensation; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue for condition. The resolution affects whether the original debt can be reclaimed if the debtor fails to pay.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute context
Recognize the scenario: a creditor releases half a debt with the phrase 'على أن تؤدي' – the core of the dispute is the meaning of 'على'.
2
Follow Abu Yusuf's argument
Abu Yusuf holds 'على' means compensation. Since the payment is already obligatory, it cannot be new compensation; thus the condition fails and the release is absolute.
3
Follow Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's argument
They hold 'على' can also mean condition. To give effect to the parties' intent, it should be interpreted as condition when compensation is impossible. They analogize to hawala (debt transfer).
4
Analyse the response to an objection
The commentator raises a linguistic objection: 'على' attaches to the release, not the payment. The answer is that despite wording, the intended meaning makes each conditional on the other.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Yusuf, if the debtor fails to pay, the release stands. Under Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the creditor can reclaim the full original debt. This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

If the condition is not fulfilled and the transferee dies insolvent, the debt returns to the transferor's liability. If this is possible, it must be interpreted accordingly because people have an interest in it to avoid total loss in case of insolvency, or to seek more profitable trade. Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the interpretation of the word 'على' (on/upon) in a settlement agreement. The scenario: a creditor releases the debtor from 500 out of 1000 dirhams 'على أن تؤدي إلى خمسمئة' (on condition that you pay me 500). The issue is whether the release is absolute (so debt does not revert if unpaid) or conditional (so debt reverts if condition not met).

Abu Yusuf argues that 'على' indicates compensation (عوض). Since the 500 payment is already obligatory before the settlement, it cannot serve as new compensation. Thus, the condition is void, the release is absolute, and the original debt does not revert. The analogy is if the creditor had initiated release without any condition.

Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue that 'على' can also indicate a condition (شرط). When using it as compensation is problematic, it should be interpreted as a condition to validate the parties' intent. They analogize to hawala (transfer of debt), where the transferor's release is conditional on payment by the transferee; if the transferee fails to pay, the debt returns. Here too, the release is conditional on payment; if unpaid, the original claim revives.

The commentator then addresses an objection: the word 'على' in the phrase applies to the release itself, not to the payment, so the proposed reasoning might not fit. The answer is that despite the wording, the intended meaning is that each part is conditional on the other, as the creditor did not intend absolute release.

Finally, the text discusses a sub-rule: if no time is specified for the payment (لم يؤقت), then the debt does not revert. The reasoning: 'على' is ambiguous; on one interpretation it is a condition (so release only upon payment), on another it is compensation (so absolute release). Since ambiguity means the debt is not dropped with certainty, it remains. This is a fundamental Hanafi principle: certainty is not removed by doubt.

Key Terms
على (ala): A preposition used in settlements; it can mean 'on condition that' (الشرط) or 'in exchange for' (العوض).
البراءة (al-bara'a): Release or discharge from a debt.
العوض (al-iwad): Compensation or consideration in a contract.
الشرط (al-shart): A condition that suspends or modifies a legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), if a creditor says 'I release you from 500 on condition that you pay me 500' and the debtor fails to pay, what is the legal effect?
A) The release stands absolute, and the creditor loses the 500.
B) The release is conditional; the creditor can reclaim the original 1000.
C) The release is void and the original debt remains unchanged.
D) The debtor must pay the 500 plus a penalty.

Key Takeaway
When a settlement uses the ambiguous word 'على', it should be interpreted as a condition when compensation is impossible, making the release contingent on performance; this is the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

Conditional vs Absolute Release in Debt Compromise
Explaining conditional vs absolute release with linguistic analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conditional and absolute release in debt compromise, apply the Hanafi rule that explicit conditions bind while absence of condition makes the release irrevocable, and identify the effect of the particle 'ala in determining the ruling.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a release is absolute unless an explicit condition is stated; ambiguity favors the debtor.

Debt compromise often involves a creditor releasing part of the debt in exchange for prompt payment. But what if the debtor fails to pay on time? Does the released amount return? The answer hinges on the wording: if the creditor explicitly ties the release to payment by a deadline, non-payment voids the release; if no deadline is set, the release is permanent. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position and the linguistic reasoning behind it, including the critical refinement by the super-commentator on the particle 'ala.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal classification
The act is a compromise (sulh) in debt, specifically a release (ibra') of part of the debt. Understand that the ruling varies based on the presence or absence of an explicit condition.
2
Analyze the particle 'ala
The word 'ala (على) can indicate either a condition (shart) or compensation ('iwad). In pure exchanges, it is treated as compensation; in releases, its interpretation depends on context.
3
Apply the rule to explicit conditional release
If the creditor says 'I release you from 500 on condition that you pay 500 tomorrow,' the release is conditional. Non-payment voids the release, and the entire original debt is due.
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Distinguish absolute release without time specification
If the creditor says 'Pay me 500, and you are released from the remainder' without a deadline, the release is absolute. The debt is extinguished permanently, even if the 500 is not paid.
The mention of payment without time is superfluous.
5
Recognize the critical refinement
The super-commentator notes that while the apparent wording may suggest a condition, the intended meaning is that release is contingent on payment. This nuance reinforces the Hanafi reliance on explicit wording.
This refinement addresses the objection that 'ala enters upon the release, not the payment.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah, with super-commentary by the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, discusses the crucial distinction between conditional and absolute release (ibra') in debt compromise (sulh). The base ruling states: 'If he did not specify a time, it does not return' — meaning if a creditor says to a debtor 'Pay me five hundred and you are released from the remainder' without fixing a deadline, the release is absolute and the debt for the remainder is extinguished permanently, even if the debtor fails to pay. The commentary then examines the linguistic particle 'ala (on/upon), which can indicate either a condition (shart) or compensation ('iwad). The author argues that in pure exchanges, 'ala is interpreted as compensation (like the letter ba) because construing it as a condition would introduce an element of risk (gharar) akin to gambling. However, in the context of release, the super-commentator refines this: while the apparent wording may suggest a condition, the intended meaning is that each part is bound to the other — the release is contingent on payment, and if payment fails, the right to the released portion returns. Three scenarios are then contrasted: (1) Absolute release without a time: the release stands regardless. (2) Explicit conditional compromise with a time: the whole debt returns if the condition fails. (3) Release using 'ala without explicit condition: the release holds, because the payment mentioned does not constitute a valid condition or compensation, so the release is considered absolute. The Hanafi position emphasizes the literal wording: explicit conditions are enforced; without explicit temporization, the release is final.

Key Terms
على ('ala): A preposition indicating either condition (shart) or compensation ('iwad) depending on context; in debt compromise, it is interpreted literally.
البراءة (al-bara'a): Release or discharge from a debt; can be absolute or conditional.
الصلح (al-sulh): Compromise or settlement of a dispute; here it refers to settling a debt by accepting a portion in full satisfaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a creditor says 'I release you from $500 on condition that you pay $500 tomorrow' and the debtor fails to pay, what is the ruling?
A) The release stands; the $500 is permanently forgiven.
B) The release is void; the entire original debt is due.
C) The release is void only for the $500; the other $500 remains due.
D) The release becomes absolute if the condition is not met.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, explicit conditions in debt compromise are binding; without explicit condition, the release is absolute and irrevocable.

Three Scenarios of Sulh in Debt and the Intra-Hanafi Ibra' Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional ibra' in debt settlement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three scenarios of sulh and ibra' in debt, state the ruling for each, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding conditional ibra'.

Core Idea
In settlement of debt, the wording order determines whether ibra' is absolute or conditional — ibra' first leaves it unconditional.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents three distinct scenarios involving settlement (sulh) and release (ibra') of debt. The key variable is the sequence of words: when the creditor mentions payment before ibra', it is a valid condition; when ibra' comes first, the payment mention becomes ineffective, leaving ibra' absolute. This difference triggers an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on whether ibra' can be made conditional on a future payment that is already owed. By mastering these scenarios, you will understand how subtle phrasing changes affect the legal outcome in debt settlement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and locate the three distinct fact patterns: (1) creditor says 'Pay me 500 and you are free from the rest' without a time; (2) explicit settlement with condition 'if not paid tomorrow, full debt returns'; (3) creditor says 'I release you from 500 on condition that you pay 500 tomorrow'. Note the wording order in each.
Scenario 1: no time specified. Scenario 2: explicit time and condition. Scenario 3: ibra' mentioned first, then condition.
2
Apply the ruling for each scenario
For scenario 1, the ibra' is absolute (debt does not return if unpaid). For scenario 2, the condition is explicit, so if payment fails, the full debt returns (consensus). For scenario 3, there is disagreement: Abu Hanifa says ibra' is conditional (debt returns if unpaid), Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say ibra' is absolute (remains effective even if unpaid).
The author calls the view of Abu Hanifa in scenario 3 'weak'.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind the ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa argues that the word 'ala indicates conditionality, so ibra' is tied to payment. The Sahibayn argue that ibra' is an immediate unilateral act; payment mentioned after ibra' cannot be a valid condition because it is future and the payment is already owed (not valid consideration). Thus the condition is ineffective and ibra' stands.
The difference hinges on whether future payment can be a valid condition for ibra'.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The author indicates that the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is relied upon, calling the opposing view 'weak'. Thus in fatwa, ibra' mentioned first is absolute regardless of non-payment.
This is consistent with the principle that ibra' is an immediate discharge.
5
Appreciate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, if the debtor fails to pay the conditionally released amount, the full original debt revives. Under the mufta bihi position, the debtor is permanently freed from the released portion and only the remaining amount is due.
This affects creditors' and debtors' strategies in settlement negotiations.

واستدل في هذه المسألة التي نحن فيها بقوله : لأنه أطلق الإبراء أولاً، وأداء الخمسمئة لا يصلح عوضاً مطلقاً، ولكنّه يصلح شرطاً، فوقع الشك في تقييده بالشرط، فلا يتقيد به بخلاف ما إذا بدأ بأداء خمسمئة؛ لأنَّ الإبراء حصل مقرونا به، فمن حيث أنه لا يصلح عوضاً تقع مطلقاً، ومن حيث أنه يصلح شرطاً لا يقع مطلقاً، فلا يثبتُ الإطلاق بالشك فافترقا. انتهى. فظن أن التعليل في هذه المسألة بما علله أبو يوسف له في المسألة الأولى بقرينة إطلاق الإبراء، وكون الأداء غير صالح للعوض، ويؤيد ذلك قولُ صاحب «الهداية» (۳) . في الاستدلال الأول : فبقي الإبراء مطلقاً، فلا يعود كما إذا بدأ بالإبراء.
And likewise if he settles with him regarding his debt for half to be paid to him tomorrow, he is free from the surplus, on condition that if he does not pay it tomorrow, the entire amount is due. Then if he releases him from half on condition that he give him the remainder tomorrow, he is free whether he pays the remainder or not. [Meaning: if he did not specify a time for payment, but said: Pay me five hundred on condition that you are free from the rest, and he did not say 'tomorrow', then in this scenario if he does not pay the debt, his debt does not return; because it is an absolute release. (And likewise if he settles with him regarding his debt for half to be paid to him tomorrow, he is free from the surplus on condition that if he does not pay it tomorrow, the entire amount is due. In this scenario, if he accepts, he is free from the rest; then if he does not pay tomorrow, the entire amount is due as in the first case, and this is by consensus. (Then if he releases him from half on condition that he give him what remains tomorrow, he is free whether he pays the remainder or not). A reference to its weakness, and it is apparent, for accountability is only by explicit words, not by interpreted phrases; for the door of interpretation and alteration is wide. (1) His saying: 'said: Pay me...' etc.; meaning he said: Pay me five hundred on condition that you are free from the excess, and he did not specify a time for payment, and did not say 'tomorrow' or otherwise, then the release is valid, and his debt does not return; because it is an absolute release. For paying five hundred is not valid as a consideration, nor is it a valid purpose since he did not tie it to a specific time, so its mention is void, unlike when he makes payment a time; for payment tomorrow is a valid purpose. [2] His saying: 'if he settles...' etc.; meaning if the creditor settles with the debtor regarding his debt for half, by saying: I settle with you regarding the thousand for five hundred that you pay me tomorrow, and you are free from the surplus on condition that if you do not pay it to me tomorrow, the thousand is due as it was, then the entire amount, i.e., the thousand, is due if he does not pay it tomorrow; because he came with explicit restriction, so it is acted upon, and if it is not found, it is nullified. [3] His saying: 'then if he releases him...' etc.; meaning if he says at the settlement: I release you from five hundred of the thousand on condition that you give me the five hundred tomorrow, then the release is effective whether he gives the five hundred or not; because the creditor uttered the release at the beginning of his speech, then mentioned the payment which is not valid as a consideration, so the possibility remains that the payment is a condition, and it is uncertain here because it is mentioned after the release, so it is not ascertained that it is a condition, so the release remains absolute. Thus the payment becomes a promise from him, not effective regarding the release, unlike the payment in the first scenario because it is effective in the release due to its mention at the beginning of the speech, and with this explanation the difference between the two scenarios is clear). (11) His saying: 'And he gave a reasoning in this scenario...' etc.; know that the author of Al-Hidayah inferred what Abu Yusuf went to in the first scenario by saying: because it is an absolute release. Do you not see that he made the payment of five hundred a consideration by mentioning it with the word 'ala which is for exchange, and payment is not valid as a consideration because it is already owed, so its existence is like non-existence, thus the release remains absolute, so it does not return, just as if he began with the release. End. And he inferred in this scenario which we are in by saying: because he uttered the release first, and payment of five hundred is not valid as a consideration absolutely, but it is valid as a condition, so doubt arises regarding its restriction by the condition, so it is not restricted, unlike when he begins with payment of five hundred; because the release occurs connected to it, so from the perspective that it is not valid as a consideration, it occurs absolutely, and from the perspective that it is valid as a condition, it does not occur absolutely, so absoluteness is not established by doubt, so they differ. End. So he thought that the reasoning in this scenario is what Abu Yusuf reasoned in the first scenario by the context of absolute release and that the payment is not valid as consideration, and this is supported by the saying of the author of Al-Hidayah in the first inference: 'Thus the release remains absolute, so it does not return, just as if he began with the release.'
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity and effect of settlement (sulh) and release (ibra') in debt. It presents three scenarios and the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, as commented upon by al-Haskafi in 'Umdat al-Ri'aya. The author explains the positions and the differences based on the timing and wording of the ibra' relative to the condition of payment. The first scenario is where the creditor says 'Pay me five hundred and you are free from the rest' without specifying a time. Here, the ibra' is absolute; if the debtor fails to pay, the debt does not revert because the payment is not a valid consideration (being already owed) and no time is fixed, so the mention of payment is void. The second scenario involves a settlement with explicit condition: 'I settle with you for five hundred to be paid tomorrow, and you are free from the rest on condition that if you do not pay tomorrow, the full thousand is due.' Here, the condition is explicit and binding; if the debtor fails to pay tomorrow, the full debt returns. This is by consensus. The third scenario: the creditor says 'I release you from five hundred on condition that you give me five hundred tomorrow.' According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the ibra' is effective whether the debtor pays or not, because the ibra' was uttered first and the payment is mentioned later, creating doubt about whether it is a condition. Since ibra' is an immediate act, it cannot be made conditional on a future payment, so the condition is ineffective and the ibra' stands. Abu Hanifa's position is that the ibra' is conditional and if payment is not made, the ibra' is void (the debt remains). The author notes the apparent weakness of the latter view. The commentary then details the reasoning from Al-Hidayah, distinguishing between the scenarios based on the word 'ala and the order of words. The key legal point is that ibra' (release) when made conditional on a future action that is not a valid consideration (because it is already owed) can either be absolute or conditional depending on the wording and order. The Hanafi school's relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in such cases is that when the ibra' is mentioned first, it is absolute and cannot be undone by non-payment.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra'): A unilateral release of debt, where the creditor declares the debtor free from obligation.
صلح (sulh): A settlement contract between creditor and debtor to reduce the debt in exchange for prompt payment or other consideration.
عوض ('iwad): Consideration or countervalue in a contract; something given in exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third scenario (paragraph beginning 'فإن أبرأه عن نصفه'), what is the ruling according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad if the debtor does not pay the agreed amount tomorrow?
A) The ibra' is void and the full debt is due.
B) The ibra' stands and the debtor is free from the entire debt.
C) The debtor must pay a penalty double the amount.
D) The settlement is invalid and the original debt remains.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when ibra' is mentioned before a condition of payment, the ibra' remains absolute and cannot be undone by non-payment.

Explicit Conditional Remission of Debt (Ibra') Not Valid
Commentary on the invalidity of explicit conditional remission and the distinction from implicit conditional remission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on explicitly conditional ibra', explain why it is invalid using the dual nature (tamleek and isqat) of ibra', and distinguish it from implicit conditional ibra'.

Core Idea
Explicit conditional ibra' is invalid because ibra' contains tamleek (gifting) which cannot be conditional, unlike pure isqat.

This lesson explores a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the invalidity of a remission of debt (ibra') that is explicitly conditional. The source text from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies that ibra' has a dual legal nature – it involves both a release (isqat) and a gifting (tamleek). While release can be conditional (like divorce), gifting cannot (like sale). Therefore, an explicitly conditional ibra' fails. We will unpack the reasoning, the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and compare with the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is ibra' (remission of debt). If the condition is explicit (e.g., using 'if', 'when'), the ruling is that the ibra' is invalid (fasid).
Explicit condition means clear conditional wording.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) based on the dual nature of ibra'. The tamleek aspect is analogous to a sale (conditional sale invalid), and the isqat aspect is analogous to divorce (conditional divorce valid).
No direct Quran or hadith is cited; the dalil is rational analogy.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Because ibra' contains tamleek, the explicit condition renders it invalid, as conditional tamleek is not permitted. The isqat aspect alone would allow conditionality, but the presence of tamleek overrides.
The condition affects the tamleek part, voiding the entire ibra'.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf differs on whether an implicit condition makes the ibra' conditional. Cross-madhhab: Shafi'is consider ibra' pure isqat, so explicit conditional ibra' is valid.
The focus here is on explicit conditions, where all Hanafis agree it is invalid.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position that explicit conditional ibra' is invalid is mufta bihi, supported by Al-Hidayah and 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah.
The ruling is relied upon for fatwa.

زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية ولو علق صريحاً كإن أديت إلى كذا، أو إذا ، أو متى لا يصح، لا يصح (ولو علق صريحاً كإن أديت إلي كذا ، أو إذا ، أو متى لا يصح) : أي إن قال : إن أديت إلي كذا، فأنت بريء من الباقي، لا يصح ) ؛ لأنَّ الأبراء" المعلق تعليقاً صريحاً لا يصح، فإنَّ الإبراء فيه معنى التمليك، ومعنى الإسقاط ، فالإسقاط لا يُنافي تعليقه بالشَّرط ، والتمليك يُنافيه، فراعينا المعنيين، وقلنا : إن كان التعليق صريحاً لا يصح، إن لم يكن صريحاً كما في الصورة المذكورة يصح فهذه التعليل عجيب، وكان المناسب أن يذكر هنا ما استدل به على ما ذهب إليه أبو حنيفة ومحمد الله في المسألة الأولى من أنّ هذا إبراء مقيّد بالشرط فيفوتُ بفواته ، فإنَّ كون الإبراء مقيداً بالشرط موجود هنا لا في المسألة الأولى، فانقلب الأمر.

وقالوا : في تقييد الإبراء بالشرط يحصل الإبراء في الحال بشرط وجود ما قيد به، حتى أنه لو لم يوجد المقيد يعودُ الدّين فيمكن الإبراء في الحال مقيداً بإعطاء الخمسمئة غداً كما لا يخفى ، فتأمل فيه فإنه بالتأمل حقيق.

He provided an explanation for this case with what Abu Yusuf explained for the first issue, and this is strange. Rather, the explanation mentioned from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is only valid for this issue, because remission (ibra') is conditional here, not in the first issue. It can be answered: This only came from the word 'tomorrow' because remission at the moment cannot be conditioned on payment of five hundred tomorrow... (continues) ... Explicit conditional remission is not valid, meaning if he says: 'If you pay me this, you are free from the rest,' it is not valid. Because remission with explicit suspension is not valid, for remission has the meaning of both gifting (tamleek) and release (isqat). Release is not incompatible with suspension by condition, but gifting is incompatible. So we consider both meanings and say: if the suspension is explicit, it is invalid; if not explicit, as in the mentioned case, it is valid. And it is known that the commentator's surprise is nothing considering the difference between the two issues as the author of Al-Hidayah sufficiently clarified...

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiyah) from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the commentary of Al-Hidayah regarding the chapter on settlement of debts (sulh). It deals with the validity of a remission of debt (ibra') that is made conditional on future payment. The text distinguishes two scenarios: (1) When the ibra' is explicitly conditioned (using 'if', 'when', etc.), the remission is invalid because ibra' has a dual nature: isqat (release) which can be conditional, and tamleek (gifting) which cannot (like a sale). (2) When the condition is implicit, such as in the earlier discussed cases where the ibra' is mentioned after payment, the ruling differs. The author of Al-Hidayah had reasoned for Abu Yusuf's position in the first case that it is an absolute remission, while for the second case, the remission is conditional but the condition is not explicit, so it is valid. The commentator here clarifies that this reasoning is correct, contrary to the initial impression of some critics.

Key Terms
إبراء (Ibra'): Remission of debt; legal release from a financial obligation.
تمليك (Tamleek): Gifting; making something owned by another.
إسقاط (Isqat): Legal dropping or release of a right or obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is an explicitly conditional remission of debt (ibra') invalid?
A) Because all conditional releases are invalid in Islamic law.
B) Because ibra' includes the meaning of tamleek (gifting), and conditional gifting is invalid.
C) Because the debtor must accept the remission explicitly.
D) Because the debt is considered repaid immediately.

Key Takeaway
Explicit conditional remission of debt is invalid in Hanafi fiqh because ibra' contains a gifting element (tamleek) that cannot be conditioned.

Secret vs Open Conditional Remission of Debt: Ruling, Secrecy, and Taqyīd/Taʿlīq
Ruling on debtor's secret conditional acknowledgment and its validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a debtor's secret conditional acknowledgment, explain why secrecy is necessary, and distinguish between taqyīd and taʿlīq in remissions.

Core Idea
A secret conditional remission is valid; an open one is an immediate acknowledgment. Taqyīd vs taʿlīq determines validity.

This passage from the Book of Reconciliation examines a nuanced scenario: a debtor who withholds acknowledgment until the creditor grants a concession. The Hanafi school draws a critical line between secret and open statements, and between restriction (taqyīd) and suspension (taʿlīq). Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying the rules of ṣulḥ and ibrāʾ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: a secret statement and an open statement. Note the different rulings: secret → valid; open → debt due immediately.
2
Understand the condition of secrecy
The commentary emphasizes that secrecy is essential; it is analogous to ṣulḥ maʿa al-inkār where the debtor is not coerced. If the debtor makes an open conditional statement, it is treated as an unconditional acknowledgment.
Secrecy was neglected by some earlier scholars but later affirmed by Mullā Miskīn and others.
3
Distinguish taqyīd and taʿlīq
Restriction (taqyīd) uses no explicit condition word; the remission takes effect immediately subject to the condition. Suspension (taʿlīq) uses explicit 'if/then' phrasing; the remission is delayed and invalid in ibrāʾ.
Verbally: taqyīd lacks 'sharṭ' word; taʿlīq includes it. Semantically: taqyīd → immediate effect; taʿlīq → suspended.
4
Apply the distinction to the debtor's statement
In the secret case, the debtor says 'I will not acknowledge until you postpone.' The word 'until' (ḥattā) indicates a restriction, not a suspension, so it is valid.
If he says 'If you postpone, then I acknowledge,' that would be suspension and invalid.
5
Trace the rationale and analogy
The commentary explains that the debtor is not coerced because he could produce evidence or demand an oath. This is analogous to selling food in famine and to ṣulḥ maʿa al-inkār. Thus the secret conditional remission is voluntary and binding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح / باب الصلح في الدين
وإن قال للآخر سراً : لا أقرّ لك حتّى تؤخِّرَه عنِّي ، أو تحطه ففعل، صح عليه، ولو أعلن أخذ للحال
[1] (وإن قال للآخر سراً : لا أقرّ لك حتّى تؤخِّرَه عنِّي ، أو تحطه ففعل، صح عليه، ولو أعلن أخذ للحال
قلنا : يصح إذا لم يصرح بالشرط ، ولا يصح إذا صرَّحَ به عملاً بالشبهين بخلافِ المسألة السابقة، فإنّها لم يؤت فيها بتصريح التعليق ، وإنما أتى فيها بالتقييد ، والفرقُ بين التقييد بالشرط والتعليق به ثابت لفظاً ومعنى. أما لفظاً فهو أنَّ التقييد لا يستعمل فيه لفظ الشرط صريحاً، وفي التعليق يستعمل. وأما معنى فهو أن تقييد الإبراء بالشرط يحصلُ به الإبراء في الحال، بشرط وجود ما قيد به، وفي التعليق لا يحصل في الحال ؛ لأنَّ المعلق بالشرط معدوم قبله، فكان التعليق بمنزلة الإضافة إلى وقتِ الشرط.
[١] قوله : وإن قال للآخر...الخ ؛ يعني وإن قال المديون للدائن سراً : لا أقر لك بمال ودين وجب منك علي حتى تؤخّر ذلك الدين عنّي، أو تحط عنِّي بعضه، ففعل الدائن التأخير أو الحط ، فذلك التأخير أو الحط جائز ولازم عليه، حتى أنه بعد التأخير لا يقدر على المطالبة في الحال. وبعد الحط لا يقدر على المطالبة لما حطّ به ؛ لأنه ليس بمكره عليه، فصار نظير الصلح مع الإنكار، ولو أعلن ما قاله سراً أخذ المال من المقر بلا تأخير وحط، والمراد بالإعلان هو التكلّم به أولاً بين الناس، وليس المراد به أنه بعدما اتفقا علـى الحـط والتأخير، فإنه لا ينقض الصلح. وقيد السر قد أهمله صاحب «الكنز» (۱) ولم ينبه عليه شارحه الزَّيْلَعِيُّ) ى (۳) ، ونبه عليه ملا مسكين (4) ، وصاحب «الدرر» و«الغــرر وصاحب والعيني «الهداية)، وغيرهم.
ولكن النظر إلى العلة التي ذكرها الزيلعي () ، وغيره من أنه ليس بمكره لتمكنه من إقامة البينة أو التحليف، فينكل ، وهو نظير الصلح مع الإنكار ؛ لأنَّ كلاً منهما لا ينافي الطوع والاختيار في تصرفه، أقصى ما في الباب أنه مضطر ، لكن الاضطرار لا يمنعُ من نفوذ تصرفه كبيع ماله بالطعام عند المخمصة يوجب التسوية بين الحالتين، اللهم إلا أن يقال : يؤخذ المقر بالمال في خبر الحال في صورة الإعلان ؛ لأنه إقرار منه بالحق بخلاف السر والكتمان. فتفكر فيه.
Book of Reconciliation / Chapter of Reconciliation regarding Debt. If he said to the other secretly: 'I will not acknowledge [the debt] for you until you postpone it from me, or reduce it,' and he did so, it is valid against him. But if he announced [it], he is taken immediately. [Commentary:] We say: It is valid if he does not explicitly state the condition, and it is not valid if he explicitly states it, acting upon both [probable] considerations, unlike the previous issue, for in that one explicit suspension was not used, rather restriction was used. The difference between restriction by condition and suspension by it is established verbally and semantically. As for verbally: restriction does not use the literal word of condition, while suspension does. As for semantically: restriction of remission by condition achieves remission immediately, subject to the existence of what it was restricted by, while in suspension it is not achieved immediately because what is suspended by condition is non-existent before it. Thus suspension is like addition to the time of the condition. [Commentary on commentary:] His saying: 'If he said to the other...' etc. means: If the debtor said secretly to the creditor: 'I will not acknowledge to you the property and debt due from me until you postpone that debt from me, or reduce some of it from me,' and the creditor performed the postponement or reduction, then that postponement or reduction is permissible and binding on him, so that after postponement he cannot demand payment immediately, and after reduction he cannot demand the reduced portion because he is not coerced. Thus it is similar to reconciliation with denial. If he announced what he said secretly, the money is taken from the acknowledging one without postponement or reduction. What is meant by 'announcement' is speaking about it first among the people; it does not mean that after they have agreed upon reduction and postponement it invalidates the reconciliation. The condition of secrecy was neglected by the author of al-Kanz, and his commentator al-Zayla'i did not point it out, but Mulla Miskin, the author of al-Durar, and al-Ghurar, and al-Ayni, and the author of al-Hidayah, and others pointed it out. However, consider the rationale mentioned by al-Zayla'i and others that he is not coerced because he is able to produce evidence or administer an oath, and then be disqualified. It is similar to reconciliation with denial because each does not contradict free will and choice in his action. The utmost in the matter is that he is in distress, but distress does not prevent the effectiveness of his action, like selling his property for food during famine necessitates equality between the two situations. Unless it is said: The acknowledger is taken for the money in the present case in the situation of announcement because it is an acknowledgment of the right, unlike secrecy and concealment. So reflect on it.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh regarding debt and reconciliation (ṣulḥ). The author begins by stating the ruling: If a debtor says secretly to his creditor, 'I will not acknowledge the debt until you postpone it or reduce it,' and the creditor agrees, then the postponement or reduction is valid and binding. The debtor thereafter cannot demand immediate payment or the reduced amount. However, if the debtor makes the same statement openly, the debt is payable immediately. The commentary then clarifies the underlying rationale. The key distinction is between taqyīd (restriction) and taʿlīq (suspension). If the debtor explicitly uses a conditional sentence (e.g., 'I will acknowledge if you postpone'), the remission is suspended and does not take effect immediately; it only becomes effective when the condition is met. This is invalid because it creates uncertainty. But if the debtor frames it as a restrictive statement (e.g., 'I acknowledge with the understanding that you postpone'), it is a valid immediate remission subject to the occurrence of the condition. The secrecy of the statement is crucial: when done secretly, it resembles a compromise (ṣulḥ) with the creditor's consent, and the debtor is not coerced because he could have chosen to litigate. If done openly, the public acknowledgment itself is proof of the debt, and the condition is disregarded. The commentator notes that some earlier scholars (like the author of Kanz al-Daqāʾiq) neglected the condition of secrecy, but later scholars like Mullā Miskīn and al-ʿAynī emphasized it. The final part discusses the rationale that the debtor is not coerced because he could have brought witnesses or demanded an oath, so his agreement is voluntary. This is analogous to selling goods during famine—distress does not invalidate the transaction. The passage concludes with an important nuance: in the open announcement case, the debtor is treated as having admitted the debt, so the money is taken immediately without any concession.

Key Terms
تعليق (taʿlīq): Suspending the effect of a legal act upon the occurrence of a future condition, using explicit conditional phrasing (e.g., 'if you do X, then I...').
تقييد (taqyīd): Restricting a legal act by a condition without using explicit conditional phrasing; the act takes effect immediately but is subject to the condition being fulfilled.
صلح مع الإنكار (ṣulḥ maʿa al-inkār): A compromise where the debtor denies the debt but agrees to pay something to avoid litigation; it is valid despite the denial.
إبراء (ibrāʾ): Remission or discharge of a debt; a unilateral act of forgiving a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between a debtor making a conditional statement secretly versus openly?
A) Both are valid if the condition is a restriction rather than a suspension.
B) If done openly, the debt is due immediately; if secretly, the condition (postponement or reduction) is valid.
C) If done secretly, the debtor is considered coerced, so the remission is invalid.
D) There is no difference; both are treated the same under Hanafi law.

Key Takeaway
A secret conditional remission phrased as a restriction (taqyīd) is valid; an open statement or explicit suspension (taʿlīq) is not.

The Right of Participation in a Shared Debt Settlement
Commentary on ruling for partner's option in settling shared debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one partner settles a shared debt and identify the option given to the other partner, using the principle of participation (musharaka).

Core Idea
Receipt from a shared debt creates a right for the non-receiving partner to share in what was received, akin to shared offspring.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi ruling on shared debts (al-dayn al-mushtarak). When one of two joint creditors settles his share of the debt with a specific property (e.g., a garment), what are the rights of the other creditor? The text explains the governing principle and provides a detailed ruling, grounded in qiyas (analogical reasoning).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the factual situation: two creditors, one debtor; one creditor settles his half of the shared debt with a garment. The other creditor has not received anything.
The debt is shared (mushtarak), meaning each creditor owns an undivided share in the single debt obligation.
2
State the Basic Principle
The commentary lays down an overarching rule: if one of two joint creditors receives something from the shared debt, the other has the right to participate in that receipt. This is the foundation.
The Arabic: 'إذا قبض أحد من الشريكين شيئاً من ذلك الدين شاركه الآخر فيه'.
3
Understand the Qiyas Proof
The principle is justified by analogy: the receipt (the property taken in settlement) is like offspring from a jointly owned slave-girl or fruit from a jointly owned tree. Such increase reverts to the original right and is shared among co-owners.
The increase is not the debt itself but a substitute (badal), yet it attaches to the original right.
4
Address the Objection
An objector might say: if the analogy is exact, the receiving partner should not be able to dispose of the received property before the other chooses to participate. The answer: the received property remains owned by the receiver until the other exercises his option; the receiver may dispose of it but then becomes guarantor for the partner's share.
This distinguishes the case from the original analogy: the physical object is not the debt itself, but a substitute.
5
Apply to the Specific Ruling
Finally, apply the principle to the case at hand: the non-settling partner has an option – he may either pursue the debtor for his half of the original debt (since his share remains in the debtor's liability) or take half of the garment from the settling partner (exercising his right of participation). An exception: if the settling partner guarantees a quarter of the debt, the right shifts to the debt, not the garment.
The exception shows that the option can be waived or modified by a guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الدين المشترك
ولو صالح أحد ربِّي دين عن نصفه على ثوب اتِّبع شريكه غريمه بنصفه، أو أخذ نصف الثّوبِ من شريكه
[1] قوله : ولو صالح ... الخ ؛ هذا شروع في بيان حكم الدين المشترك، وأخره عن بيان حكم الدين المفردِ لتلوه عنه طبعاً ، والأصل في هذا الباب أن الدين المشترك إذا قبض أحد من الشريكين شيئاً من ذلك الدين شاركه الآخر فيه.
ووجهه : إن الدين ازداد بالقبض، فإنّ ماليَّة الدين إنما تكون باعتبار عاقبة القبض، وهذه الزيادة راجعة إلى أصل الحق ، فيصير كزيادة الولد في الجارية المشتركة ، والثمرة في الشجرة المشتركة، ومثل هذه الزيادة يكون مشتركاً بين الشركاء، فهاهنا أيضاً يكون لصاحبه حق المشاركة. ولا يقال : إن زيادة الدين بالقبض لو كانت كزيادة الولد والثمر لما جازَ تصرُّفُ القابض في المقبوض قبل أن يختار الشريك مشاركته ، كما في الولد والثمر، فإنَّه لا يجوز تصرف أحد الشريكين بلا إذن الآخر.
لأنا نقول : إنّ المقبوض قبل أن يختار الشريك مشاركته فـيـه بـاق على ملك القابض ؛ لأن العين غير الدين حقيقة، وقد قبضه بدلاً عن حقه فيملكه وينفذ تصرفه فيه كالهبة وقضاء دين غريمه، ويضمن الشريكه حصته، فهذا أصل كلي يتفرع عليه فروع كثيرة فاحفظه. ومن تلك الفروع الكثيرة ما ذكره المصنف الله هاهنا بقوله : ولو صالح...الخ، يعني إذا كان الدين مشتركاً بين الشريكين، وصالح أحدهما عن نصفه وهو نصيبه على ثوب، فلشريكه الخيار إن شاء أن يتبع المديون بنصف الدين لبقاء حصته في ذمته أو يأخذ نصف الثوب من شريكه ؛ لأنّ له حق المشاركة فيه ؛ لأنه عوض عن دينه، إلا أن يضمن المصالح للشريك ربع الدين، فإنَّ حقه في الدين لا في الثوب.
Chapter on Shared Debt. If one of two creditors settles his half of a debt with a garment, his partner may pursue the debtor for his half, or take half of the garment from his partner. [Commentary:] His statement: 'And if he settles... etc.': This begins the explanation of the ruling of shared debt, and he delayed it after the explanation of the ruling of individual debt because it naturally follows it. The basic principle in this chapter is that if one of the two partners receives something from that shared debt, the other shares with him in it. Its reasoning: The debt increases by receipt, for the value of the debt is only realized in terms of the result of receipt, and this increase reverts to the original right, so it becomes like the increase of offspring in a jointly owned slave-girl, and the fruit on a jointly owned tree. Such increase is shared among the partners, so here too the partner has the right of participation. And do not say: If the increase of debt by receipt were like the increase of offspring and fruit, then the receiver would not be allowed to dispose of the received property before the partner chooses participation, as in the case of offspring and fruit, where one partner cannot dispose without the other's permission. We say: The received property before the partner chooses participation remains in the ownership of the receiver; because the physical object is not the debt in reality, and he received it in exchange for his right, so he owns it and his disposal of it is valid, such as gifting or paying off his own debt, and he guarantees his partner's share. This is a comprehensive principle from which many branches derive; memorize it. Among those many branches is what the author mentioned here with his statement: 'And if one settles... etc.', meaning that if the debt is shared between two partners, and one of them settles his half, which is his share, for a garment, then his partner has the option: if he wishes, he can pursue the debtor for half of the debt because his share remains in the debtor's liability, or he can take half of the garment from his partner, because he has the right of participation in it, since it is the substitute for his debt. However, if the settling partner guarantees to the partner a quarter of the debt, then his right is in the debt, not in the garment.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the ruling of shared debt (al-dayn al-mushtarak). The author explains the basic principle: when one of two joint creditors receives something from the common debtor (e.g., through a settlement), the other creditor has the right to share in what was received. The proof is analogical (qiyas): just as the offspring of a jointly owned slave-girl or the fruit of a jointly owned tree are shared among the owners, likewise the increase (the received property) from a debt returns to the original right and is shared. An objection is raised: if this analogy were perfect, the receiving partner should not be able to dispose of the received property before the other chooses to participate, as is the case with offspring and fruit. The author answers: the received property remains owned by the receiver until the other exercises his option; the receiver may dispose of it (e.g., give it as a gift) and is then liable to compensate the partner. This is the general principle. As for the specific ruling derived from the matn: if one of two partners settles his half of a joint debt with a garment, the other partner has an option: either pursue the debtor for his half of the debt (since it remains in the debtor's liability) or take half of the garment from the settling partner (since the garment is a substitute for the debt). An exception: if the settling partner guarantees (i.e., promises to pay) a quarter of the debt to the partner, then the partner's right remains in the debt, not in the garment.

Key Terms
الدين المشترك (al-dayn al-mushtarak): A debt owed by one debtor to two or more creditors jointly, where the debt is undivided (e.g., a loan from two people).
صلح (ṣulḥ): A settlement or compromise where one party relinquishes part of his claim in exchange for a specific property (here, a garment).
مشاركة (mushāraka): The right of a co-creditor to share in property received by another co-creditor from the common debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when one of two joint creditors settles his half of a shared debt with a garment, what option does the other creditor have?
A) He must accept half of the garment from his partner.
B) He may either pursue the debtor for his half of the debt or take half of the garment from his partner.
C) He has no option and must seek repayment from the debtor only.
D) He can void the settlement and demand the entire debt in cash.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of musharaka ensures that a settlement of a shared debt by one creditor does not extinguish the other creditor's claim; the other may either follow the debtor or share in the settlement property.

Shared Debt: Rights of Co-Creditors After Sulh
Explains ruling on shared debt when one creditor settles for a cloth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling when one co-creditor settles his share of a shared debt with a cloth, and identify the options available to the other creditor, including the exception when the settling creditor guarantees a quarter of the debt.

Core Idea
When one co-creditor receives payment from a shared debt, the other has a right to participate in it, similar to jointly-owned produce.

This lesson delves into a specific ruling from the Hanafi school on shared debts. When two creditors are owed a joint debt, and one of them settles his share by accepting a cloth instead, what are the rights of the other creditor? The passage establishes a key principle: any increase realized from the debt must be shared, just as offspring from a jointly-owned slave-girl or fruit from a jointly-owned tree are shared. You will learn the options available to the non-settling creditor and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base scenario and ruling
Read the matn: two creditors share a debt; one settles his half with a cloth. The ruling: the other creditor may either pursue the debtor for his half or take half the cloth from the settling creditor.
2
Understand the underlying principle
The commentator states a general principle: when one co-creditor receives any part of a shared debt, the other shares in it. The reasoning (talil) is that the value of the debt is realized upon receipt, making it an increase analogous to offspring/fruit in joint ownership.
Key analogy: increase in joint property is shared.
3
Analyze the objection and response
An objection: if the increase were truly like offspring, the receiver could not dispose of the cloth before the partner chooses participation. The response: the cloth remains the receiver's property until the partner opts in; the receiver guarantees the partner's share.
Distinction: physical item is owned by receiver, liability remains.
4
Examine the khiyar (option) and its exception
The non-settling creditor has two options: pursue the debtor or take half the cloth. Exception: if the settling creditor guarantees a quarter of the debt, the other creditor loses the option over the cloth and must take the quarter.
Reason: sulh is based on concession; imposing quarter protects both parties from hardship.
5
Note additional clarifications and the hila
The rule applies only to sulh (settlement), not purchase, and only when the settlement is with a different commodity than the debt. For same-type settlement, it's like receiving part of the debt. A legal trick (hila) allows the settling creditor to avoid sharing by using a gift or sale arrangement.
Understanding these details prevents misapplication.

فصل في الدين المشترك
ولو صالح أحد ربِّي دين عن نصفه على ثوب اتَّبِعَ شريكه غريمه بنصفه، أو أخذ نصف الثوب من شريكه
[1] قوله : ولو صالح ... الخ ؛ هذا شروع في بيان حكم الدين المشترك، وأخره عن بيان حكم الدين المفردِ لتلوه عنه طبعاً ، والأصل في هذا الباب أن الدين المشترك إذا قبض أحد من الشريكين شيئاً من ذلك الدين شاركه الآخر فيه.
ووجهه : إن الدين ازداد بالقبض، فإنّ ماليَّة الدين إنما تكون باعتبار عاقبة القبض، وهذه الزيادة راجعة إلى أصل الحق ، فيصير كزيادة الولد في الجارية المشتركة ، والثمرة في الشجرة المشتركة، ومثل هذه الزيادة يكون مشتركاً بين الشركاء، فهاهنا أيضاً يكون لصاحبه حق المشاركة. ولا يقال : إن زيادة الدين بالقبض لو كانت كزيادة الولد والثمر لما جازَ تصرُّفُ القابض في المقبوض قبل أن يختار الشريك مشاركته ، كما في الولد والثمر، فإنَّه لا يجوز تصرف أحد الشريكين بلا إذن الآخر.
لأنا نقول : إنّ المقبوض قبل أن يختار الشريك مشاركته فـيـه بـاق على ملك القابض ؛ لأن العين غير الدين حقيقة، وقد قبضه بدلاً عن حقه فيملكه وينفذ تصرفه فيه كالهبة وقضاء دين غريمه، ويضمن الشريكه حصته، فهذا أصل كلي يتفرع عليه فروع كثيرة فاحفظه. ومن تلك الفروع الكثيرة ما ذكره المصنف الله هاهنا بقوله : ولو صالح...الخ، يعني إذا كان الدين مشتركاً بين الشريكين، وصالح أحدهما عن نصفه وهو نصيبه على ثوب، فلشريكه الخيار إن شاء أن يتبع المديون بنصف الدين لبقاء حصته في ذمته أو يأخذ نصف الثوب من شريكه ؛ لأنّ له حق المشاركة فيه ؛ لأنه عوض عن دينه، إلا أن يضمن المصالح للشريك ربع الدين، فإنَّ حقه في الدين لا في الثوب.

[1] قوله : إلا أن يضمن.... الخ ؛ استثناء من قوله : أتبع شريكه غريمه أو أخذ نصف الثوب من شريكه : يعني إذا ضمن الشريك المصالح ربع الدين ليس للشريك الغير خيار، والسر في الخيار : أن أحد الشريكين لا يختص بالمقبوض من الدين، إذ لا يجوز قسمته قبل القبض، والمقبوض خير من الدين، فقضيّته أن يضمنه ربع الدين، ولا يكون له سبيل على الثوب ؛ لأنه أخذ عوضه.
لكن الصلح مبني على الحطّ ، فلو ألزمنا ربعَ الدِّينِ لتضرر المصالح ؛ لأنه قد لا يبلغ قيمة الثوب كله ربع الدين، فأثبتنا له الخيار بين أن يرجع على المدين بنصيبه، وبين أن يأخذ نصف ما وقع عليه الصلح أو ربع الدين، دفعاً للضرر عنهما بقدر الإمكان. ثم إن المصنف له إنما قال : لو صالح، فإنّه لو اشترى أحد الشريكين شيئاً بنصيبه لم يشارك الآخر فيه، كما سيصرح به، وإنّما قال أحد ربي دين، ووضع المسألة في الدين ؛ لأنه لو كان الصلح عن عين مشتركة يختص المصالح ببدل الصلح، وليس لشريكه أن يشاركه فيه لكونه معاوضةً من كل وجه؛ لأن المصالح عنه مال حقيقة ، بخلاف الدين فإنَّ ماليته باعتبار عاقبة القبض كما مرّ.
وإنما قال : على ثوب ؛ ومراده أن يكون على خلاف جنس الدين ؛ لأنه لو صالحه على جنسه فالآخر يشاركه فيه، أو يرجعُ على المديون، وليس للقابض فيه خيار ؛ لأنه بمنزلة قبض بعض الدين. ولا فرق في هذا الصلح بين أن يكون عن إقرار أو سكوت أو إنكار ؛ لأن المدعيين يتصادقان على أنّ لهما على المدعي عليه دينا، وما يأخذه بدلا عنه ، وزعمهما حجَّةً عليهما، ولو أراد القابض أن يختص به ولا يرجع عليه شريكه فيما قبض.
فالحيلة فيه أن يهبه الغريم قدر دينه ، وهو يبرئه عن دينه أو يبيع الطالب كفاً من زبیب ونحوه بقدر نصيبه من الدين، ثمّ يبرئه عن الدين ويأخذ ثمن الزبيب. كما سنذكره مفصلاً إن شاء الله (۱).
Chapter on shared debt. If one of two creditors settles for half of [the debt] with a cloth, his partner may pursue the debtor for his half, or take half of the cloth from his partner. [Comment] His statement: 'And if he settles...' etc.; this is a start in explaining the ruling of shared debt, and he delayed it after the ruling of sole debt due to natural sequence. The basic principle in this chapter is that if one of the two partners receives something from that shared debt, the other partner shares it with him. The reasoning: the debt increases by being received, for the wealth-value of a debt is only considered with regard to the consequence of receipt, and this increase goes back to the original right, thus it becomes like the increase of a child in a jointly-owned slave-girl, or fruit in a jointly-owned tree; such increase is shared among the partners, so here too the partner has a right of participation. It cannot be said: if the increase of debt by receipt were like the increase of a child and fruit, then the receiver's disposal of the received item before the partner chooses participation would not be permissible, as in the child and fruit, for disposal by one partner without the other's permission is not allowed. We respond: the received item before the partner chooses participation remains in the ownership of the receiver, because the physical object is not the debt in reality, and he received it in exchange for his right, so he owns it and his disposal therein (like gifting or paying off his own debt) is valid, and he guarantees the partner's share. So this is a comprehensive principle from which many branches derive; memorize it. Among those many branches is what the author mentioned here: 'And if he settles...' etc., meaning if the debt is shared between two partners, and one of them settles for his half—his share—with a cloth, then his partner has the option: if he wishes, he may pursue the debtor for half the debt, since his share remains in the debtor's liability; or he may take half the cloth from his partner, because he has the right of participation in it, as it is the substitute for his debt. Except if the settling partner guarantees to the partner a quarter of the debt, for then his right is in the debt, not in the cloth. [1] His statement: 'Except if he guarantees...' etc.; an exception from his statement: 'He may pursue his partner's debtor or take half the cloth from his partner' – meaning if the settling partner guarantees a quarter of the debt to the partner, then the other partner has no option. The secret behind the option: one of the partners does not exclusively own the received item from the debt, since division is not permissible before receipt, and the received item is better than the debt, so the consequence would be that he guarantees a quarter of the debt, and he has no claim on the cloth because he took its substitute. But sulh is based on reduction; if we were to impose [payment of] a quarter of the debt, the settling party would be harmed, because the value of the entire cloth may not equal a quarter of the debt. So we established the option for him between returning to the debtor for his share, and between taking half of what was settled upon or a quarter of the debt, to repel harm from both as much as possible. Moreover, the author only said 'if he settles' because if one of the partners buys something with his share, the other does not participate in it, as will be clarified. He only said 'one of two creditors' and posed the scenario in debt because if the settlement were over a jointly-owned physical asset, the settling party exclusively owns the substitute of the sulh, and his partner has no right to participate in it, because it is a complete exchange; because the subject of settlement is actual wealth, unlike debt whose wealth-value is considered based on the consequence of receipt as mentioned. He only said 'with a cloth' intending that it be of a different type than the debt; because if he settled with the same type, the other partner participates in it or returns to the debtor, and the receiver has no option in it, because it is like receiving part of the debt. There is no difference in this sulh whether it is based on admission, silence, or denial; because the two claimants agree that the debtor owes them a debt, and what one takes is a substitute for it, and their claim is a proof against them. If the receiver wants to exclusively keep it and his partner not to have recourse on what he received, then the legal trick (hila) is for the debtor to gift him the amount of his debt, and he discharges him from the debt; or the claimant sells a handful of raisins or similar for the amount of his share of the debt, then discharges the debtor and takes the price of the raisins, as will be mentioned in detail if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition (sharh) expanding on the ruling of shared debt (al-dayn al-mushtarak) as introduced in the matn. The commentator begins by stating that this section treats the ruling of shared debt, placed after the ruling of sole debt for natural sequence. He then lays down a fundamental principle: when one of two co-creditors receives any part of a debt owed to them jointly, the other creditor has the right to share in that received property. The reasoning is based on the idea that the value of a debt is realized only upon receipt; thus receiving the debt is effectively an increase that goes back to the original right, analogous to the offspring of a jointly-owned slave-girl or the fruit of a jointly-owned tree—both of which are common property. The commentator anticipates an objection: if this increase were truly like offspring or fruit, then the receiving creditor should not be able to dispose of the received item before the other partner elects to participate, just as one joint owner cannot dispose of offspring without the other's permission. He answers this by explaining that the received item (the cloth) remains the property of the receiver until the other partner chooses to participate, because the physical object is distinct from the debt. The receiver owns it in exchange for his share and may dispose of it (e.g., give it as a gift or pay off a debt), but he is liable to compensate his partner for his share (i.e., he guarantees the partner's portion). This is a general principle with many applications. The specific application mentioned in the matn is the case of sulh (settlement): if one of two co-creditors settles his half of the debt with a cloth (of a different type than the debt), the other creditor has an option: either to pursue the debtor for his half (since his share remains in the debtor's liability), or to take half of the cloth from the settling creditor (because he has a right to participate in it as it represents the substitute for the debt). However, there is an exception: if the settling creditor guarantees (i.e., undertakes to pay) the other creditor a quarter of the debt, then the other creditor's right is confined to the debt—he cannot claim the cloth. The commentator explains the wisdom behind this option: since division of the debt before receipt is invalid, and the cloth is better than the debt, the strict rule would require the settling creditor to give his partner a quarter of the debt (since the cloth is half the debt? Actually, text says: the consequence would be that he guarantees a quarter of the debt). But because sulh is based on mutual concession (hatt), enforcing a quarter of the debt could harm the settling creditor if the cloth's value is less. So the law grants the partner the alternative of either returning to the debtor or taking half the cloth or a quarter of the debt, to prevent hardship. The commentator also clarifies that the ruling is specific to sulh (compromise) not purchase, and to debt not a physical asset, because a physical asset's value is immediate. Additionally, if the settlement is with the same type of currency as the debt, it is considered like receiving part of the debt, and the other creditor may participate or return to the debtor without an option for the receiver. The sulh is valid whether it is based on admission, silence, or denial, since both claimants affirm that the debtor owes them a debt. Finally, the commentator provides a legal trick (hila) for a creditor who wishes to avoid sharing: either the debtor gifts the creditor an amount equal to the debt and the creditor discharges him, or the creditor sells a handful of raisins or similar to the debtor for his share and then discharges the debtor, effectively receiving the price without sharing. This hila will be explained later in detail.

Key Terms
الدين المشترك (al-dayn al-mushtarak): A debt owed to two or more persons jointly, where each is entitled to a share.
الصّلح (al-sulh): A compromise or settlement where one party accepts something in lieu of a claim.
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option given to the non-settling creditor to either pursue the debtor or take a share of the settlement item.
الحيلة (al-hila): A legally permissible stratagem to achieve a legitimate goal while avoiding a legal consequence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of shared debt, if one creditor settles his half with a cloth (different type), what options does the other creditor have?
A) Only to pursue the debtor for his half of the debt
B) Either to pursue the debtor for his half or take half the cloth from the settling creditor
C) Only to take half the cloth from the settling creditor
D) To force the settling creditor to give him a quarter of the debt

Key Takeaway
When a co-creditor settles his share of a shared debt with a property of a different type, the other co-creditor has the option to either pursue the debtor or take a share of that property, unless the settling creditor guarantees a portion of the debt.
