Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
When a Partner Collects Part of a Shared Debt: Sharing and Recourse Options
Commentary on partner's rights when one collects part of a shared debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options a partner has when the other partner collects part of a shared debt and explain the conditional reversion if the debtor fails.

Core Idea
Collecting a shared debt does not give exclusive ownership; your partner shares automatically and may later revert if the debtor fails.

In partnerships where a debt is owed jointly, if one partner collects part of it, the other partner automatically shares in what was collected. The debtor cannot claim he has fully paid the collector. Moreover, the non-collecting partner may instead pursue the debtor, but if the debtor becomes insolvent, he can revert and claim half from the collector. This lesson examines these rules and their underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Basic Ruling
One partner collects part of a shared debt. What is the default ruling for the collected amount? The commentator states it is shared between the partners.
Refer to the opening sentence: 'If one of them collects something of the debt, his partner shares with him in it.'
2
Understand the Debtor's Position
Why can't the debtor claim he has discharged the debt to the collector alone? Because the debt is jointly owned, payment to one partner is payment to both in proportion.
3
Examine the Non-Collecting Partner's Options
The partner may either share in the collected amount (since it is jointly owned) or pursue the debtor for his share. The collector cannot force the partner to accept only the debtor's later payment.
Derived from: 'He may share with him in it if he wishes... and if he wishes he may return against the debtor.'
4
Analyze the Conditional Reversion
If the partner chooses to pursue the debtor and the debtor later fails to pay (e.g., dies insolvent), the partner can revert and claim half of what the collector had collected. This is analogous to hawala (debt transfer).
The commentator explicitly analogizes to hawala: 'as in the case of hawala.'
5
Differentiate from the Case of Compromise (Sulh)
In the compromise case, the partner who compromises his share for a good (e.g., cloth) has the option to give half the cloth or a quarter of the debt, to avoid harm. This contrasts with the strict sharing in the collection case.
The commentator notes that here harm is absent, while in sulh harm would occur if quarter liability were imposed.

ولو قبض أحد شيئاً من الدين شاركه شريكه فيه، ورجعا على الغريم بما بقي (ولو قبض أحد شيئاً من الدين شاركه شريكه فيه، ورجعا على الغريم بما ) : أي لا يكون للغريم أن يقول للذي أعطاه نصف الدين : إِنِّي قد أعطيتُك حقك، فليس لك علي شيء، فإنَّ ما أعطاه إياه مشترك بينه وبين شريكه. (ولو اشترى بنصفه شيئاً ضمنه شريكه ربع الدين أو اتبع غريمه ) : أي اشترى أحد الشريكين بنصفه من الغريم شيئاً فللشريك الآخر أن يضمنه ربع الدين ؛ لأنه صار قابضا نصف الدين [ ١ ] قوله : ولو قبض...الخ ؛ يعني ولو قبض أحد الشريكين شيئاً من الدين شاركه شريكه فيه، لما تقدَّمَ من أنَّ الدِّينَ المشترك بين اثنين إذا قبض أحدهما شيئاً منه فلصاحبه أن يشاركه في المقبوض. وقال العيني في «شرح الكنز (۱) : فله أن يشاركه فيه إن شاء ؛ لأنه عين حقه من وجه، وإن شاء رجع على الغريم وهو المديون ؛ لأنّ حقه عليه في الحقيقة، وإنما كان له أن يشاركه في فصل الثوب في فصل الصلح ؛ كيلا يلزم المصالح الضرر، وهاهنا انتفى الضرر. انتهى. [٢]قوله : ورجعا على الغريم بما بقي ؛ لأنَّ هذين الشريكين لما اشتركا في المقبوض فلا بد أن يبقى الباقي على الشركة، ولو سلم له المقبوض واختار متابعةً الغريم ، ثمَّ تَوى نصيبه بأن مات الغريم مفلساً . ا رجع على القابض بنصف ما قبض ؛ لأنَّ التسليم مقيد بشرط سلامة الباقي له ، فإذا لم يُسلم له رجع عليه كما في الحوالة، صرَّحَ بها العيني(٢) ، والزَّيْلَعِي (۳)، وغيرهما. [۳] قوله : أو اتبع غريمه ؛ لأن القابض قبض حقه إلا أن له حق المشاركة، فكان له أن يشاركه. [٤] قوله : لأنه صار ... الخ ؛ يعني لأنّ المشتري هاهنا صار قابضاً بالمقاصة من غير (۱) رمز الحقائق» (۲) : ١٦٩). (۲) في «رمز الحقائق»(٢ : ١٦٩). (۳) في تبيين الحقائق»(٥ : ٤٦). كتاب الصلح باب الصلح في الدين وفي الإبراء عن حظه والمقاصة بدين سبق لم يرجع الشريك بالمقاصة"، فيضمنه شريكه الربع بخلاف مسألة الصلح، فإنَّه إذا أخذَ الثّوبَ بطريقِ الصلح عن النصف، ومبنى الصلح على الحطّ، فالظاهر أن قيمةَ الثّوبِ أقل من نصف الدين، فلو ضمنه ربعَ الدّين يتضرّر آخذ التّوب، فلاخذ الثّوبَ أن يقول : إني ما أخذت إلا النوب، فإن شئت خذ نصفه بخلاف مسألة الشراء إذ مبناه على المعاكسة ، فلا يتضرر المشتري بضمان ربع الدين. (وفي الإبراء عن حظه والمقاصة بدين سبق لم يرجع الشريك) : أي إذا أبرأ أحد الشريكين الغريم عن نصيبه لا يرجع الشريك الآخر على ذلك الشريك ؛ لأن الأبراء إتلاف لا قبض، وكذا إن وقعتِ المقاصة بدينه السابق، صورته : لزيد على عمرو خمسون درهماً ، فباع عمرو وبكر عبداً مشتركاً بينهما من زيد بمئة درهم حتَّى وَجَبَ لكل منهما على زيد خمسون درهماً، وقعت المقاصة بين الخمسين التي وجبت لعمرو على زيد، وبين الخمسين التي كانت لزيد على عمرو حطيطة ولا ضرر عليه، فإنّ مبنى البيع على المماكسة، بخلاف ما إذا صالح بنصيبه على الثوب، حيث يكون المصالح بالخيار إن شاء دفع إليه نصف الثوب، وإن شاء دفع إليه ربع الدين. فإن مبنى الصلح على الإغماض والحطيطة، فكأن المصالح بالصلح أبرأه عن بعض نصيبه، وقبض البعض، فلو ألزمنا المصالح دفع ربع الدين يتضرر به ؛ لأنه لم يستوف الدين كاملاً، فيتخيرُ القابض بين دفع نصف المصالح عليه أو ربع الدين كما تقدم. ] قوله : بالمقاصة ؛ فإن قيل : قسمة الدين قبل القبض لا تتصور، فكيف تتصور المقاصة فيه ؟ أجيب : بأن قسمة الدين قبل القبض يجوز ضمناً، وإنما لا تجوز قصداً، وهنا وقعت القسمة في ضمن صحة الشراء، وصحة المصالحة، وكم من شيء يصح ضمناً ولا يصح قصداً. (1) ينظر: «الهداية»(۳: ۱۹۹).
And if one of them collects something of the debt, his partner shares with him in it, and they both return against the debtor for what remains. (And if one collects something of the debt, his partner shares with him in it, and they both return against the debtor for what remains): i.e., the debtor cannot say to the one who gave him half the debt: 'I have given you your right, so you have no claim against me,' because what he gave him is shared between him and his partner. (And if he buys something with his half, his partner guarantees him for a quarter of the debt or follows his debtor): i.e., if one of the two partners buys something from the debtor with his half, the other partner may hold him liable for a quarter of the debt, because he has become a receiver of half the debt. [1] His saying: 'And if he collects... etc.'; meaning, if one of the two partners collects something of the debt, his partner shares with him in it, due to what preceded that when a debt shared between two is collected partially by one, the other may share in what was collected. Al-'Ayni said in Sharh al-Kanz (1): He may share with him in it if he wishes, because it is essentially his right in one respect, and if he wishes he may return against the debtor, because his right is actually against him. He only had the option to share in the case of the cloth in the chapter of sulh (compromise) to avoid harm to the compromiser, but here harm is absent. End. [2] His saying: 'and they both return against the debtor for what remains'; because when the two partners share in what was collected, the remainder must remain shared. If he (the partner who collected) had been allowed to keep what he collected and had chosen to follow the debtor, then his share was lost (i.e., the debtor died insolvent), he would revert against the collector for half of what he collected, because the concession was conditional on the safety of the remainder for him; so if it is not safe, he reverts against him as in the case of hawala (transfer of debt). This was explicitly stated by al-'Ayni (2), al-Zayla'i (3), and others. [3] His saying: 'or he follows his debtor'; because the collector collected his own right, but the partner has a right of participation, so he may participate. [4] His saying: 'because he became... etc.'; meaning because the buyer here became a receiver by muqassa (set-off) without... And in the case of remission from his share and muqassa with a prior debt, the partner does not revert by muqassa; so the partner holds him liable for a quarter, unlike the case of sulh, because if he takes the cloth by way of sulh for half, and the basis of sulh is reduction, then it is apparent that the value of the cloth is less than half the debt; so if he were held liable for a quarter of the debt, the taker of the cloth would be harmed. Therefore the taker of the cloth may say: 'I only took the cloth; if you wish, take half of it.' Unlike the case of purchase, because its basis is bargaining, so the buyer is not harmed by guaranteeing a quarter of the debt. (And in the case of remission from his share and muqassa with a prior debt, the partner does not revert): i.e., if one of the two partners remits the debtor from his share, the other partner does not revert against that partner, because remission is destruction (itlaf), not collection. Similarly, if muqassa occurs with his prior debt. Its example: Zayd owes 'Amr fifty dirhams. Then 'Amr and Bakr sell a slave jointly owned between them to Zayd for one hundred dirhams, so each of them becomes owed fifty dirhams by Zayd. Then muqassa occurs between the fifty that 'Amr is owed by Zayd and the fifty that Zayd owed 'Amr. This is a reduction (hatita) and no harm is upon him, because the basis of sale is bargaining, unlike if he made a compromise (sulh) with his share for a cloth, where the compromiser has the option: if he wishes he gives him half the cloth, and if he wishes he gives him a quarter of the debt. Because the basis of sulh is leniency and reduction, so it is as if the compromiser by sulh remitted part of his right and collected the part; so if we obligated the compromiser to pay a quarter of the debt, he would be harmed, because he did not recover the debt fully. Therefore the receiver (of the cloth) has the choice between handing over half of the compromised item or a quarter of the debt as mentioned earlier. [His saying: 'by muqassa'; for it may be asked: division of a debt before collection is inconceivable, so how is muqassa conceivable in it? The answer: division of debt before collection is permissible implicitly, though not permissible intentionally. Here the division occurs within the validity of the purchase and the validity of the compromise. Many things are valid implicitly but not intentionally. (1) See al-Hidayah (3:199).
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of a ruling from al-Wiqaya regarding the rights of two partners when one of them collects part of a shared debt or transacts with his share. The commentator explains that if one partner collects something from the debtor, the other partner automatically shares in what was collected; the debtor cannot claim he has discharged the collector alone. The collecting partner does not have exclusive right. If the partner wishes, he may instead pursue the debtor for his share. But if he chooses to pursue the debtor and the debtor becomes insolvent, he can revert to the collector and claim half of what was collected, under an implicit condition that the debtor would pay the rest. This is analogous to hawala (transfer of debt). If the collecting partner buys something with his half (i.e., uses the collected amount to purchase an item from the debtor), the other partner has two options: either hold the collector liable for a quarter of the total debt (since the collector received half the debt in effect) or pursue the debtor for his share. The reason for quarter liability is that the collector becomes a receiver by set-off (muqassa). This differs from the case of compromise (sulh), where if a partner compromises his half for a cloth (which is likely worth less than half), harming the partner by imposing quarter liability is avoided, so the partner can demand half the cloth instead. The text then discusses remission (ibra) and set-off (muqassa) with a prior debt. If one partner remits the debtor from his share, the other partner has no recourse against the remitting partner because remission is destruction, not collection. Similarly, if set-off occurs due to a prior debt (e.g., offsetting mutual debts), the partner cannot revert. An example is given involving a joint sale and mutual debts. The passage concludes by addressing an objection: how can set-off occur before division of a debt? The answer is that division is implicitly valid within the sale or compromise, even though direct division before collection is prohibited. This reasoning uses istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent harm.

Key Terms
المقاصة (al-muqassa): Set-off of mutual debts without physical transfer; implicitly valid in contracts like sale.
الإبراء (al-ibra): Remission or discharge of a debtor from a debt; considered itlaf (destruction) of the right, not qabd (collection).
الصلح (al-sulh): Compromise, especially for a consideration less than the claimed amount; its basis is reduction (hatt) and leniency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A debtor pays half the debt to one of two partners. Which statement is correct?
A) The debtor is fully discharged.
B) The debt is reduced by half, and the collected half is shared between the partners.
C) The debtor must pay the other half directly to the other partner.
D) The collecting partner owns the entire payment.

Key Takeaway
When one partner collects a shared debt, the other partner automatically shares in the collected amount and has the option to either share or pursue the debtor, with a conditional right of reversion.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Purchase with Collected Share: Quarter Liability vs. Pursuing Debtor
Commentary on partner's options when one partner buys with collected shared debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the options available to a partner when the other partner uses collected shared debt to purchase goods, and distinguish the ruling from the case of compromise (sulh).

Core Idea
When a partner buys goods with collected shared debt, the other partner can either hold him liable for a quarter or pursue the debtor, due to implicit set-off and no harm.

If a partner collects part of a shared debt and then uses that amount to buy something from the debtor, the other partner has a choice: either hold the collecting partner liable for a quarter of the debt (since he effectively received half the debt through set-off) or pursue the debtor for his share. This differs from the compromise case (sulh) where the partner has different options to prevent harm. This lesson analyzes the rationale behind these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Transaction
One partner uses his collected half of the shared debt to purchase an item from the debtor. What is the legal effect? The buyer becomes a receiver of half the debt by set-off (muqassa).
Text: 'if one of the two partners buys something from the debtor with his half, the other partner may hold him liable for a quarter of the debt, because he has become a receiver of half the debt.'
2
Understand the Quarter Liability
Why quarter? The buyer received half the debt (his share) via the purchase, so the partner can claim a quarter of the total debt from the buyer. This is because the buyer now holds the item, which is equivalent to half the debt, and the partner's share is half of that half (i.e., quarter).
This is the default remedy: the partner can demand quarter from the buyer.
3
Alternative Option: Pursue the Debtor
Instead of holding the buyer liable, the partner may choose to pursue the debtor for his share directly. The buyer's purchase does not extinguish the debtor's obligation to the partner.
The commentator says: 'or he follows his debtor.'
4
Distinguish from Sulh (Compromise)
In the sulh case, if a partner compromises his share for a cloth (worth less than half the debt), the other partner cannot impose quarter liability because that would harm the compromiser. Instead, the partner can demand either half the cloth or a quarter of the debt. The basis of sulh is reduction (hatt), while purchase is based on bargaining.
The commentator explicitly contrasts: 'unlike the case of sulh, because its basis is reduction...'
5
Reason for the Distinction
In purchase, the buyer's price is assumed to be fair market value, so no harm occurs. In sulh, the compromised item is typically worth less, so imposing quarter liability would cause loss. The rule protects the compromiser from such loss.
This demonstrates the Hanafi principle of preventing harm (darar).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if one of them collects something of the debt, his partner shares with him in it, and they both return against the debtor for what remains. (And if one collects something of the debt, his partner shares with him in it, and they both return against the debtor for what remains): i.e., the debtor cannot say to the one who gave him half the debt: 'I have given you your right, so you have no claim against me,' because what he gave him is shared between him and his partner. (And if he buys something with his half, his partner guarantees him for a quarter of the debt or follows his debtor): i.e., if one of the two partners buys something from the debtor with his half, the other partner may hold him liable for a quarter of the debt, because he has become a receiver of half the debt. [1] His saying: 'And if he collects... etc.'; meaning, if one of the two partners collects something of the debt, his partner shares with him in it, due to what preceded that when a debt shared between two is collected partially by one, the other may share in what was collected. Al-'Ayni said in Sharh al-Kanz (1): He may share with him in it if he wishes, because it is essentially his right in one respect, and if he wishes he may return against the debtor, because his right is actually against him. He only had the option to share in the case of the cloth in the chapter of sulh (compromise) to avoid harm to the compromiser, but here harm is absent. End. [2] His saying: 'and they both return against the debtor for what remains'; because when the two partners share in what was collected, the remainder must remain shared. If he (the partner who collected) had been allowed to keep what he collected and had chosen to follow the debtor, then his share was lost (i.e., the debtor died insolvent), he would revert against the collector for half of what he collected, because the concession was conditional on the safety of the remainder for him; so if it is not safe, he reverts against him as in the case of hawala (transfer of debt). This was explicitly stated by al-'Ayni (2), al-Zayla'i (3), and others. [3] His saying: 'or he follows his debtor'; because the collector collected his own right, but the partner has a right of participation, so he may participate. [4] His saying: 'because he became... etc.'; meaning because the buyer here became a receiver by muqassa (set-off) without... And in the case of remission from his share and muqassa with a prior debt, the partner does not revert by muqassa; so the partner holds him liable for a quarter, unlike the case of sulh, because if he takes the cloth by way of sulh for half, and the basis of sulh is reduction, then it is apparent that the value of the cloth is less than half the debt; so if he were held liable for a quarter of the debt, the taker of the cloth would be harmed. Therefore the taker of the cloth may say: 'I only took the cloth; if you wish, take half of it.' Unlike the case of purchase, because its basis is bargaining, so the buyer is not harmed by guaranteeing a quarter of the debt. (And in the case of remission from his share and muqassa with a prior debt, the partner does not revert): i.e., if one of the two partners remits the debtor from his share, the other partner does not revert against that partner, because remission is destruction (itlaf), not collection. Similarly, if muqassa occurs with his prior debt. Its example: Zayd owes 'Amr fifty dirhams. Then 'Amr and Bakr sell a slave jointly owned between them to Zayd for one hundred dirhams, so each of them becomes owed fifty dirhams by Zayd. Then muqassa occurs between the fifty that 'Amr is owed by Zayd and the fifty that Zayd owed 'Amr. This is a reduction (hatita) and no harm is upon him, because the basis of sale is bargaining, unlike if he made a compromise (sulh) with his share for a cloth, where the compromiser has the option: if he wishes he gives him half the cloth, and if he wishes he gives him a quarter of the debt. Because the basis of sulh is leniency and reduction, so it is as if the compromiser by sulh remitted part of his right and collected the part; so if we obligated the compromiser to pay a quarter of the debt, he would be harmed, because he did not recover the debt fully. Therefore the receiver (of the cloth) has the choice between handing over half of the compromised item or a quarter of the debt as mentioned earlier. The answer: division of debt before collection is permissible implicitly, though not permissible intentionally. Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of a ruling from al-Wiqaya regarding the rights of two partners when one of them collects part of a shared debt or transacts with his share. The commentator explains that if one partner collects something from the debtor, the other partner automatically shares in what was collected; the debtor cannot claim he has discharged the collector alone. The collecting partner does not have exclusive right. If the partner wishes, he may instead pursue the debtor for his share. But if he chooses to pursue the debtor and the debtor becomes insolvent, he can revert to the collector and claim half of what was collected, under an implicit condition that the debtor would pay the rest. This is analogous to hawala (transfer of debt). If the collecting partner buys something with his half (i.e., uses the collected amount to purchase an item from the debtor), the other partner has two options: either hold the collector liable for a quarter of the total debt (since the collector received half the debt in effect) or pursue the debtor for his share. The reason for quarter liability is that the collector becomes a receiver by set-off (muqassa). This differs from the case of compromise (sulh), where if a partner compromises his half for a cloth (which is likely worth less than half), harming the partner by imposing quarter liability is avoided, so the partner can demand half the cloth instead. The text then discusses remission (ibra) and set-off (muqassa) with a prior debt. If one partner remits the debtor from his share, the other partner has no recourse against the remitting partner because remission is destruction, not collection. Similarly, if set-off occurs due to a prior debt (e.g., offsetting mutual debts), the partner cannot revert. An example is given involving a joint sale and mutual debts. The passage concludes by addressing an objection: how can set-off occur before division of a debt? The answer is that division is implicitly valid within the sale or compromise, even though direct division before collection is prohibited. This reasoning uses istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent harm.

Key Terms
المقاصة (al-muqassa): Set-off of mutual debts without physical transfer; implicitly valid in contracts like sale.
الصلح (al-sulh): Compromise, especially for a consideration less than the claimed amount; its basis is reduction (hatt) and leniency.

A) Half

Key Takeaway
When a partner buys goods with collected shared debt, the other partner can claim a quarter of the debt from him or pursue the debtor, a rule that differs from compromise cases due to the risk of harm.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Remission and Set-Off: When a Partner Cannot Reclaim
Commentary on remission and set-off in shared debts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a partner cannot reclaim from another partner who remits the debtor or engages in set-off with a prior debt.

Core Idea
Remission (ibra) is destruction, not collection; set-off (muqassa) is implicit division; neither gives the non-acting partner a right to reclaim from the acting partner.

In shared debt scenarios, if one partner remits (forgives) the debtor his share or engages in a set-off with a prior debt, the other partner has no recourse against the remitting partner. This is because remission is considered destruction (itlaf) of the right, not collection, and set-off is treated as an implicit division that is valid as part of a larger transaction. This lesson clarifies these principles and addresses the objection about dividing a debt before actual collection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Actions
What are the two actions that do not allow the partner to revert? (1) Remission (ibra) of the debtor from the partner's share. (2) Set-off (muqassa) of the partner's share against a prior debt owed by that partner to the debtor.
Text: 'If one of the two partners remits the debtor from his share, the other partner does not revert... Similarly, if muqassa occurs with his prior debt.'
2
Understand Why Remission Is Not Collection
Remission is considered itlaf (destruction) of the right, not qabd (collection). The remitting partner has not taken possession of anything; he has simply waived his right. Thus, the partner cannot claim a share of something that was never collected.
The commentator says: 'because remission is destruction, not collection.'
3
Analyze Set-Off (Muqassa) and Implicit Division
Set-off occurs when the partner offsets his share against a debt he owes the debtor. This is an implicit division of the debt. The division of a debt before actual collection is generally invalid if done intentionally, but it is valid implicitly as part of a valid transaction (e.g., sale). Since the set-off is valid, the partner cannot revert.
The commentator provides an example involving a joint sale and mutual debts.
4
Address the Objection about Division Before Collection
An objection is raised: division of a debt before collection is not permissible, so how can set-off be valid? The answer is that such division is permissible implicitly (within a valid contract), though not intentionally. Many things are valid as a consequence but not as a primary act.
Text: 'Division of debt before collection is permissible implicitly, though not permissible intentionally.' This is a key usul principle.
5
Contrast with Collection Cases
Unlike actual collection, in remission and set-off, there is no physical receipt of funds or goods. Therefore, the partner cannot claim a share. The ruling protects the remitting partner from further liability and upholds the validity of implicit divisions.
This contrasts with the earlier cases where actual collection occurred, giving the partner options.

Source Text

(۲) في «رمز الحقائق»(٢ : ١٦٩). (۳) في تبيين الحقائق»(٥ : ٤٦). كتاب الصلح باب الصلح في الدين وفي الإبراء عن حظه والمقاصة بدين سبق لم يرجع الشريك بالمقاصة"، فيضمنه شريكه الربع بخلاف مسألة الصلح، فإنَّه إذا أخذَ الثّوبَ بطريقِ الصلح عن النصف، ومبنى الصلح على الحطّ، فالظاهر أن قيمةَ الثّوبِ أقل من نصف الدين، فلو ضمنه ربعَ الدّين يتضرّر آخذ التّوب، فلاخذ الثّوبَ أن يقول : إني ما أخذت إلا النوب، فإن شئت خذ نصفه بخلاف مسألة الشراء إذ مبناه على المعاكسة ، فلا يتضرر المشتري بضمان ربع الدين. (وفي الإبراء عن حظه والمقاصة بدين سبق لم يرجع الشريك) : أي إذا أبرأ أحد الشريكين الغريم عن نصيبه لا يرجع الشريك الآخر على ذلك الشريك ؛ لأن الأبراء إتلاف لا قبض، وكذا إن وقعتِ المقاصة بدينه السابق، صورته : لزيد على عمرو خمسون درهماً ، فباع عمرو وبكر عبداً مشتركاً بينهما من زيد بمئة درهم حتَّى وَجَبَ لكل منهما على زيد خمسون درهماً، وقعت المقاصة بين الخمسين التي وجبت لعمرو على زيد، وبين الخمسين التي كانت لزيد على عمرو حطيطة ولا ضرر عليه، فإنّ مبنى البيع على المماكسة، بخلاف ما إذا صالح بنصيبه على الثوب، حيث يكون المصالح بالخيار إن شاء دفع إليه نصف الثوب، وإن شاء دفع إليه ربع الدين. فإن مبنى الصلح على الإغماض والحطيطة، فكأن المصالح بالصلح أبرأه عن بعض نصيبه، وقبض البعض، فلو ألزمنا المصالح دفع ربع الدين يتضرر به ؛ لأنه لم يستوف الدين كاملاً، فيتخيرُ القابض بين دفع نصف المصالح عليه أو ربع الدين كما تقدم. ] قوله : بالمقاصة ؛ فإن قيل : قسمة الدين قبل القبض لا تتصور، فكيف تتصور المقاصة فيه ؟ أجيب : بأن قسمة الدين قبل القبض يجوز ضمناً، وإنما لا تجوز قصداً، وهنا وقعت القسمة في ضمن صحة الشراء، وصحة المصالحة، وكم من شيء يصح ضمناً ولا يصح قصداً. (1) ينظر: «الهداية»(۳: ۱۹۹).
And if one of them collects something of the debt, his partner shares with him in it, and they both return against the debtor for what remains. (And if one collects something of the debt, his partner shares with him in it, and they both return against the debtor for what remains): i.e., the debtor cannot say to the one who gave him half the debt: 'I have given you your right, so you have no claim against me,' because what he gave him is shared between him and his partner. (And if he buys something with his half, his partner guarantees him for a quarter of the debt or follows his debtor): i.e., if one of the two partners buys something from the debtor with his half, the other partner may hold him liable for a quarter of the debt, because he has become a receiver of half the debt. [1] His saying: 'And if he collects... etc.'; meaning, if one of the two partners collects something of the debt, his partner shares with him in it, due to what preceded that when a debt shared between two is collected partially by one, the other may share in what was collected. Al-'Ayni said in Sharh al-Kanz (1): He may share with him in it if he wishes, because it is essentially his right in one respect, and if he wishes he may return against the debtor, because his right is actually against him. He only had the option to share in the case of the cloth in the chapter of sulh (compromise) to avoid harm to the compromiser, but here harm is absent. End. [2] His saying: 'and they both return against the debtor for what remains'; because when the two partners share in what was collected, the remainder must remain shared. If he (the partner who collected) had been allowed to keep what he collected and had chosen to follow the debtor, then his share was lost (i.e., the debtor died insolvent), he would revert against the collector for half of what he collected, because the concession was conditional on the safety of the remainder for him; so if it is not safe, he reverts against him as in the case of hawala (transfer of debt). This was explicitly stated by al-'Ayni (2), al-Zayla'i (3), and others. [3] His saying: 'or he follows his debtor'; because the collector collected his own right, but the partner has a right of participation, so he may participate. [4] His saying: 'because he became... etc.'; meaning because the buyer here became a receiver by muqassa (set-off) without... And in the case of remission from his share and muqassa with a prior debt, the partner does not revert by muqassa; so the partner holds him liable for a quarter, unlike the case of sulh, because if he takes the cloth by way of sulh for half, and the basis of sulh is reduction, then it is apparent that the value of the cloth is less than half the debt; so if he were held liable for a quarter of the debt, the taker of the cloth would be harmed. Therefore the taker of the cloth may say: 'I only took the cloth; if you wish, take half of it.' Unlike the case of purchase, because its basis is bargaining, so the buyer is not harmed by guaranteeing a quarter of the debt. (And in the case of remission from his share and muqassa with a prior debt, the partner does not revert): i.e., if one of the two partners remits the debtor from his share, the other partner does not revert against that partner, because remission is destruction (itlaf), not collection. Similarly, if muqassa occurs with his prior debt. Its example: Zayd owes 'Amr fifty dirhams. Then 'Amr and Bakr sell a slave jointly owned between them to Zayd for one hundred dirhams, so each of them becomes owed fifty dirhams by Zayd. Then muqassa occurs between the fifty that 'Amr is owed by Zayd and the fifty that Zayd owed 'Amr. This is a reduction (hatita) and no harm is upon him, because the basis of sale is bargaining, unlike if he made a compromise (sulh) with his share for a cloth, where the compromiser has the option: if he wishes he gives him half the cloth, and if he wishes he gives him a quarter of the debt. Because the basis of sulh is leniency and reduction, so it is as if the compromiser by sulh remitted part of his right and collected the part; so if we obligated the compromiser to pay a quarter of the debt, he would be harmed, because he did not recover the debt fully. Therefore the receiver (of the cloth) has the choice between handing over half of the compromised item or a quarter of the debt as mentioned earlier. [His saying: 'by muqassa'; for it may be asked: division of a debt before collection is inconceivable, so how is muqassa conceivable in it? The answer: division of debt before collection is permissible implicitly, though not permissible intentionally. Here the division occurs within the validity of the purchase and the validity of the compromise. Many things are valid implicitly but not intentionally. (1) See al-Hidayah (3:199).
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of a ruling from al-Wiqaya regarding the rights of two partners when one of them collects part of a shared debt or transacts with his share. The commentator explains that if one partner collects something from the debtor, the other partner automatically shares in what was collected; the debtor cannot claim he has discharged the collector alone. The collecting partner does not have exclusive right. If the partner wishes, he may instead pursue the debtor for his share. But if he chooses to pursue the debtor and the debtor becomes insolvent, he can revert to the collector and claim half of what was collected, under an implicit condition that the debtor would pay the rest. This is analogous to hawala (transfer of debt). If the collecting partner buys something with his half (i.e., uses the collected amount to purchase an item from the debtor), the other partner has two options: either hold the collector liable for a quarter of the total debt (since the collector received half the debt in effect) or pursue the debtor for his share. The reason for quarter liability is that the collector becomes a receiver by set-off (muqassa). This differs from the case of compromise (sulh), where if a partner compromises his half for a cloth (which is likely worth less than half), harming the partner by imposing quarter liability is avoided, so the partner can demand half the cloth instead. The text then discusses remission (ibra) and set-off (muqassa) with a prior debt. If one partner remits the debtor from his share, the other partner has no recourse against the remitting partner because remission is destruction, not collection. Similarly, if set-off occurs due to a prior debt (e.g., offsetting mutual debts), the partner cannot revert. An example is given involving a joint sale and mutual debts. The passage concludes by addressing an objection: how can set-off occur before division of a debt? The answer is that division is implicitly valid within the sale or compromise, even though direct division before collection is prohibited. This reasoning uses istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent harm.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibra): Remission or discharge of a debtor from a debt; considered itlaf (destruction) of the right, not qabd (collection).
المقاصة (al-muqassa): Set-off of mutual debts without physical transfer; implicitly valid in contracts like sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a partner not have recourse against the other partner who remits the debtor?
A) Because remission is a form of collection
B) Because remission is destruction of one's own right, not collection
C) Because the remitting partner gains possession
D) Because the debtor must still pay the remitting partner

Key Takeaway
Remission and set-off are not considered collection; they are destruction and implicit division respectively, so the partner cannot reclaim from the acting partner.

Dividing the Remaining Debt After Partial Release by a Co-Creditor
Ruling on proportional division of remaining debt after partial release

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for dividing the remaining debt when one co-creditor partially releases the debtor.

Core Idea
A co-creditor's partial release affects only his own share; the remaining debt is divided proportionally.

When two people jointly own a debt, and one of them forgives part of his share, the distribution of the remaining amount changes. This lesson explains how to recalculate each creditor's entitlement based on their original shares, using a clear numerical example from the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
Understand the scenario: two partners jointly own a debt, and one of them releases part of his own share.
2
Determine the original shares
Establish each partner's share in the total debt (e.g., half each).
The shares must be known before the release.
3
Calculate the amount released
The release applies only to the releasing partner's share, not to the other's.
For example, if the debt is 100 and each has 50, releasing half of his share means releasing 25.
4
Recalculate the remaining debt
Subtract the released amount from the total, then divide the remainder proportionally according to the original shares.
The ratio of remaining shares determines the division.
5
Verify the result
Check that the releasing partner's share is reduced by the amount he forgave, while the other's share remains unchanged.
The sum of new shares must equal the remaining debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أبرأ أحدهما عن البعض قُسِّمَ الباقي على سهامه : أي إذا كان الدين بين الشريكين نصفين، فأبرأ أحدهما عن نصف نصيبه، وهو الربع ، قسم الباقي أثلاثاً ؛ لأنه بقي له ربع ، وللآخر نصف
And if one of them releases (the debtor) from part (of the debt), the rest is divided according to their shares. That is, if the debt between the two partners is half each, and one releases half of his share, which is one quarter, then the remaining is divided into thirds, because there remains for him one quarter and for the other half.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling concerning two co-creditors who have a debt owed to them jointly. If one creditor voluntarily releases the debtor from part of his own share, the remaining debt is not simply left as is; rather, it is divided between the two creditors in proportion to their respective shares. The example given: if two partners each own half of a debt, and one partner forgives half of his share (i.e., one quarter of the total debt), then the remaining debt is three-quarters of the original amount. This remaining amount is divided into three equal parts: one part (one quarter of the original) belongs to the first partner (who forgave half his share), and two parts (the original half) belong to the other partner. So the division is a simple proportional recalculation. The underlying principle is that the release only affects the share of the releaser; the other partner's share remains intact, and the remaining debt is shared accordingly.

Key Terms
أبرأ (abra'a): To release or forgive a debt, extinguishing the debtor's liability for that portion.
قُسِّمَ الباقي على سهامه (qussima al-baqi 'ala sihamihi): The remaining debt is divided proportionally according to each creditor's original share.
شريكين (sharīkayn): Two partners who jointly own a debt receivable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two partners are jointly owed 100 dirhams, each having a 50 dirham share. One partner releases the debtor from half of his share (25 dirhams). How is the remaining debt distributed between them?
A) The non-releasing partner gets 50 dirhams, the releasing partner gets 25 dirhams.
B) Each gets 37.5 dirhams.
C) The non-releasing partner gets 75 dirhams, the releasing partner gets 0.
D) The remaining 75 dirhams are split equally: 37.5 each.

Key Takeaway
A co-creditor's partial release only reduces his own share; the remaining debt is divided proportionally, preserving the other creditor's original entitlement.

Settlement by One Party in a Salam Contract: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sulh in salam contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the settlement (sulh) of one salam buyer, explain the qiyas argument, and identify the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Core Idea
In salam, a settlement by one buyer without the other's consent is invalid; the subject matter is a debt, not a physical asset.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents a dispute among the three main Hanafi imams. Two people jointly enter a salam contract (forward purchase) with a seller. One of them later settles his share with the seller, taking back his capital. Is this settlement valid without the consent of the other buyer? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say no; Abu Yusuf says yes. Let's examine the reasoning and see why the Hanafi school ultimately requires the partner's consent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the salam scenario
Identify the basic facts: two buyers each pay 50 dirhams for a joint salam of 1 kurr. One buyer later settles his share for his 50 dirhams, effectively canceling his portion. Recognize the subject matter is a debt (grain in the seller's Dhimma) not yet received.
Salam requires immediate payment for future delivery of fungible goods.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
They rule the settlement invalid without the other buyer's consent. Their proof: if valid for his share alone, it would require dividing a debt before receipt (impermissible); if valid for both shares, the other's consent is needed but absent.
This is a decisive argument (dalil) based on the nature of debt.
3
State Abu Yusuf's opposing position and his analogy
Abu Yusuf considers the settlement immediately valid because it is a disposal of one's own right. He analogizes to a jointly purchased slave where one partner cancels his share (iqala) without the other's consent.
In iqala of a slave, each has independent ownership of a physical asset.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the analogy
The Hanafi response: the analogy is flawed (qiyas ma'a al-fariq). In the slave case, ownership and authority pre-exist the contract; in salam, the subject matter does not yet exist, so authority arises only from the contract, making it joint. Hence the partner's consent is essential.
The key difference (farq) is physical existence vs. debt in Dhimma.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and its practical effect
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as adopted in al-Tanwir: the settlement is suspended on the other's consent. If consent is given, it is effective; if refused, it is void.
Under Abu Yusuf, the settlement would be immediately valid, and the other could choose to share or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطل صلح أحد ربِّي سَلَم من نصفِهِ على ما دَفَعَ : أي إذا أسلم رجلان في كُرّ، ورأس مالهما مئة، وسَلّم كُلُّ واحدٍ خمسين درهماً، ثُمَّ صالح أحدهما عن نصف كره بالخمسين التي دَفَعَها إلى المُسَلّم إليه، وأخذ الخمسين، فهذا الصلح لا يجوز عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد. وعند أبي يوسف له يجوز كما إذا اشتريا عبداً فأقال أحدهما في نصيبه. لهما: أنه لو صح في نصيبه خاصةً لَزِمَهُ قسمةُ الدِّينِ في الذمة، ولو جازَ في نصيبهما لا بُدَّ من إجازة الآخر، ولم توجد. فالأولى ما قاله في «التنوير : صالح أحد ربِّي سَلَم عن نصيبه على ما دفع فإن أجازه الآخر نفذ عليهما، وإن ردّه رد. انتهى. فإنك عرفت أنه ليس بباطل، بل هو صحيح موقوف على الإجازة، وظهر من قول المصنف له بطلانه مطلقاً، اللهم إلا أن يراد به أنه سيبطل على تقدير عدم الإجازة. قوله : يجوز؛ لأنه تصرُّف في خالص حقه فيجوز كما في سائر الديون، والسر فيه : إنَّ الصلح هاهنا على رأس المال، كالصلح في غيره على ما أراد من المال. ألا ترى أنه يجوز إذا أجازه شريكه، ويشاركه في المقبوض ، ويرجعان على الغريم ، فإذا كان كذلك وجب أن ينفذ في حقه، ثمّ يكون شريكه بالخيار إن شاءَ شاركه فيما قبض ، ويكون الدين بينهما، وإن شاءَ رجع على المسلم إليه كسائر الديون، وكإقالة نصيبه المعين في العبد المبيع المشترك. قوله : لهما أنه...الخ ; تقريره : إنّ هذا الصلح على تقدير صحتِه لا يخلو : إما أن يصح في نصيبه خاصة، أو في نصيبهما جميعاً، وعلى الشق الأول لزمه قسمةُ الدِّينِ في الذمة قبل القبض، وهذا غير جائز. ووجه اللزوم : إنّ خصوصية نصيبه لا تظهر إلا بالتمييز، وهو لا يحصل إلا بالقسمة، وعلى الشق الثاني فلا بُدَّ من إجازة الآخر، وهي غير موجودة. وأما القياس على الإقالة قياس مع الفارق، فإنّ العين هاهنا أصل لوجود العقد، حتى لا يجوز العقد إلا لوجودها ، فكذا يكون أصلاً لصحة الرفع ; لأنّ العين موجودة حساً ومعاينة قبل العقد، وكان لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما ولاية التصرف فيها، ولا يستفيده بالعقد، فكذا رفعه لتمام العلة فيها. وفي المسلّم فيه لا يثبت الملك إلا بالعقد، ولا ولايـة للتصرف فيه إلا به ، فكان فعلهما علة لثبوت الشركة والولاية، فافهم
And the settlement of one of the two parties of salam from his half for what he paid becomes void. That is, if two men enter into a salam for a kurr, and their capital is one hundred, each having paid fifty dirhams, then one of them settles for half of the kurr with the fifty dirhams he paid to the seller, and takes the fifty, this settlement is not valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, it is valid, just as if they two bought a slave and one of them cancels his share. They (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) said: If it were valid in his share specifically, it would necessitate division of the debt while it is still in the liability, and if it were valid in both their shares, the other's consent would be necessary, and it is not present. The best view is what was said in al-Tanwir: one of the two salam parties settles for his share for what he paid; if the other consents, it is effective on both, and if he rejects it, it is rejected. So you know that it is not void, but rather it is valid suspended on consent. And it appears from the author's statement that it is absolutely void, except if it is meant that it becomes void upon failure of consent. He said: It is valid because it is a disposal of his pure right, so it is valid as in other debts. The secret is that the settlement here is for the capital, like settlement for other things for whatever money he wishes. Do you not see that it is valid if his partner consents, and he shares in what is received, and they both have recourse against the debtor? So if that is the case, it must be effective in his right, then his partner has a choice: if he wishes, he shares with him in what is received and the debt is between them, and if he wishes, he resorts to the seller like other debts. And it is like the cancellation of his specified share in a jointly purchased slave. He said: Their argument that... its summary is: This settlement, assuming its validity, cannot be except that it either is valid in his share specifically, or in both their shares. On the first possibility, it would necessitate division of the debt before receipt, which is not permissible. The reason: his share's specificity does not appear except by segregation, and that is not achieved except by division. On the second possibility, the other's consent is necessary, and it is not present. As for the analogy to cancellation, it is an analogy with a difference, because the physical object here is the basis for the contract's existence, such that the contract is not valid except for its existence, so it is likewise a basis for the validity of cancellation; because the physical object exists tangibly and visibly before the contract, and each of them has authority to dispose over it, and he does not acquire that authority through the contract, so likewise its cancellation completes the effective cause in it. In the salam subject matter, ownership is not established except through the contract, and there is no authority to dispose over it except through it, so their act is the cause for establishing joint ownership and authority. So understand.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute among the three main Hanafi jurists regarding the validity of a settlement (sulh) made by one of two parties to a salam (forward purchase) contract. The scenario: two people together advance 100 dirhams (50 each) to a seller for a specified quantity of grain (a kurr). One of them then settles with the seller for his half of the grain, taking back his 50 dirhams. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, this settlement is not valid as an absolute transaction; rather, it is suspended on the consent of the other buyer. If the other consents, it is effective; if not, it is void. Their reasoning: if the settlement were valid only for the settling party's share, it would require dividing the debt (the entitlement to the grain) while it is still a liability in the seller's Dhimma, which is impermissible. If it were valid for both shares, the other's consent is necessary and missing. Abu Yusuf, however, considers it immediately valid based on analogy (qiyas) to the case of a jointly purchased slave: if one partner cancels his share of the purchase (iqala), it is valid without the other's consent. The commentator explains that this analogy is flawed because in a slave purchase, each buyer owns a share of a physical object and has authority over it before any cancellation, whereas in salam, the subject matter (the grain) does not exist yet; ownership and authority to dispose arise only from the contract itself. Therefore, the settlement requires the partner's consent. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, namely that the settlement is valid only if the other partner consents; otherwise it is void. This resolution is adopted in al-Tanwir.

Key Terms
ربي سلم (rabbay salam): Two parties who give capital in a salam contract (forward purchase).
صلح (sulh): A settlement or compromise where one party forgoes part of his claim in exchange for something.
إقالة (iqala): Recission or cancellation of a sale by mutual agreement or by one party's offer.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; a contract whose validity depends on a condition (here, the partner's consent).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the ruling when one of two salam buyers settles his share with the seller for the capital he paid, without the other buyer's consent?
A) The settlement is valid only if the other buyer consents; otherwise it is void.
B) The settlement is immediately valid regardless of the other buyer.
C) The settlement is valid for the buyer's share only if he takes possession.
D) The settlement requires the seller's consent.

Key Takeaway
In a salam contract, the subject matter is a debt in the Dhimma, so a settlement by one buyer without the other's consent is invalid; consent of all partners is required.

Settlement of Debt in Salam by a Partner: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Suspended Validity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on settlement by one partner in Salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions (Abu Yusuf, the two, and the mufta bihi) on the validity of a settlement by one partner in a Salam contract, and explain the qiyas and counter-arguments.

Core Idea
A partner's settlement in Salam is valid but suspended on the other's ratification — reconciling Abu Yusuf's permissiveness and the two's caution.

In a joint Salam contract, one partner may seek to settle with the seller for his share of the advanced capital. Is this settlement immediately effective, or does it require the other partner's consent? The Hanafi school contains a nuanced internal dispute: Abu Yusuf permits it absolutely, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require ratification. The later relied-upon view, cited from al-Tanwir, considers the settlement valid but suspended (mawqūf) upon the other partner's approval. This lesson dissects the reasoning and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The act is sulh (settlement) by one partner in a Salam contract with the seller, concerning his share of the capital (ra's al-mal).
The partners are joint owners of the Salam capital; the seller owes the commodity.
2
State the three Hanafi positions
Abu Yusuf: valid immediately. The two (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad): invalid without other's ratification. Mufta bihi (al-Tanwir): valid but suspended on ratification.
The author of al-Wiqayah may have implied absolute invalidity, but the commentator refines this.
3
Analyse the proofs of each position
Abu Yusuf analogizes to iqalah in a jointly bought slave (each partner has independent authority). The two argue: if valid only in his share, debt division in dhimmah is required (impermissible); if valid in both, permission is needed.
The key issue is whether the subject matter is a specific chattel ('ayn) or a debt in dhimmah.
4
Apply the tarjih (preponderance)
The later relied-upon view (al-Tanwir) adopts suspended validity, reconciling both concerns: it is not void, but effective only upon ratification.
This protects the other partner's rights while not declaring the settlement futile.

فالأولى ما قاله في «التنوير (۱) : صالح أحد ربِّي سَلَم عن نصيبه على ما دفع فإن أجازه الآخر نفذ عليهما، وإن ردّه ردّ. انتهى. فإنك عرفت أنه ليس بباطل، بل هو صحيح موقوف على الإجازة، وظهر من قول المصنف له بطلانه مطلقاً، اللهم إلا أن يراد به أنه سيبطل على تقدير عدم الإجازة.

Chapter of Settlement / Section on Settlement Regarding Debt. And according to Abu Yusuf, it is permissible for him, just as if they jointly bought a slave and one of them annulled [the sale] with respect to his share. According to the two, if it were valid specifically in his share, it would necessitate division of the debt while it is in the dhimmah; and if it were valid in both their shares, the other's permission would be necessary, and it is not present. The better [view] is what was stated in al-Tanwir: 'If one of the two parties to a Salam contract settles with the seller regarding his share for what he paid, then if the other ratifies it, it is effective on both; and if he rejects it, it is rejected.' End. For you have come to know that it is not invalid; rather it is valid but suspended on ratification. And it is apparent from the author's statement that it is absolutely invalid, unless it is intended that it will be invalid on the assumption of non-ratification. [1] His statement: 'it is permissible' because it is a disposition in his exclusive right, so it is permissible just as in other debts. And the secret in it is that the settlement here is upon the capital, like settlement in other [debts] on whatever wealth one desires. Do you not see that it is permissible if his partner ratifies it, and he shares with him in what is received, and they both have recourse against the debtor? So if this is the case, then it must be effective in his [right], then his partner has the option: if he wishes he shares with him in what he received, and the debt is between them; and if he wishes he has recourse against the seller (muslam ilayh) like other debts, and like the cancellation (iqālah) of his specific share in a jointly purchased slave that is sold. [2] His statement: 'according to the two that...etc.'; its exposition: This settlement, assuming its validity, is not free from either being valid specifically in his share, or being valid in both their shares. On the first branch, it necessitates division of the debt in the dhimmah before receipt, and that is not permissible. And the face of necessity is that the specificity of his share does not appear except by distinction, and that is not achieved except by division. And on the second branch, the other's permission is necessary, and it is not present. As for the analogy upon cancellation (iqālah), it is an analogy with a difference. For the specific chattel ('ayn) here is the basis for the existence of the contract, such that the contract is not valid except for its existence; likewise it is a basis for the validity of revocation, because the chattel exists sensibly and visually before the contract, and each of them has authority of disposal over it, and he does not acquire that [authority] by the contract; therefore revocation is likewise for the completion of the effective cause. In the object of Salam (muslam fīh), ownership is not established except by the contract, and there is no authority to dispose over it except by it; so their action was the cause for establishing joint ownership and authority. So understand. (1) Tanwir al-Absar (p. 176). (2) See al-'Ināyah (8:438). (3) See Tabyīn al-Haqā'iq (5:49).
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a Salam contract (forward sale) where two parties, say A and B, jointly place a Salam order with a seller (muslam ilayh). The capital (ra's al-mal) is paid at the time of contract, and the commodity is to be delivered later. The question arises when one of the two, say A, settles with the seller for his share of the capital due — i.e., A releases the seller from his share of debt in exchange for something (usually the capital). Is this settlement (sulh) valid? Abu Yusuf holds it is valid, analogizing it to a case where two people jointly buy a slave and one cancels the sale regarding his share (iqalah). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two) argue that it is invalid unless the other partner B ratifies it. Their reasoning: if the settlement is valid only in A's share, that would require division of the debt while still in the dhimmah (before receipt), which is impermissible because the debt is indivisible until receipt. If it is valid in both shares, then B's permission is required, and it is lacking. The commentator then cites al-Tanwir, which states the correct view: the settlement is not absolutely invalid, but valid and suspended (mawqūf) upon B's ratification — if B ratifies, it becomes effective for both; if not, it is void. This refines the author's (of al-Wiqāyah) apparent statement of absolute invalidity. The commentary then clarifies the qiyas on iqalah: analogizing Salam to a joint purchase of a slave is flawed because in the slave case, each partner has independent disposal authority over a specific physical thing before the contract, whereas in Salam, rights arise only from the contract and belong jointly. Thus, the effective cause is different, supporting the two's position that permission is necessary.

Key Terms
صلح (ṣulḥ): A contract of settlement or compromise, where one party relinquishes a claim or debt in exchange for something.
سلم (salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance for a specified commodity to be delivered later.
إقالة (iqālah): Cancellation or revocation of a sale by mutual agreement, returning the goods and price.
دين في الذمة (dayn fī al-dhimmah): A debt that is a liability in the abstract, not yet attached to a specific asset.
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification or permission, especially by a co-owner or partner to validate a disposition made without their prior consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two), why is the settlement by one partner in a Salam contract invalid without the other partner's ratification?
A) Because the settling partner lacks legal capacity.
B) Because if valid in his share only, it would require division of the debt in the dhimmah before receipt, which is impermissible.
C) Because the seller (muslam ilayh) did not consent.
D) Because the settlement contract requires a specific commodity which is not yet delivered.

Key Takeaway
The settlement by one partner in Salam is not invalid outright but valid and suspended on the other's ratification, reconciling the protective and permissive positions within the Hanafi school.

Settlement (Sulh) with Two Currencies and Other Goods
Commentary on sulh with two currencies and other goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sulh involving gold, silver, and other goods, identify the condition for validity when using one currency, and explain the rationale based on sale vs. ibra.

Core Idea
Sulh involving ribawi goods must follow sale rules; using one currency requires giving more than the share to avoid riba.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāya explains the Hanafi ruling on settlements (sulh) when an estate contains gold, silver, and other assets. The commentator shows that such settlements are treated as sales because ibra is invalid for specific property. The key condition to avoid riba is that if only one currency is given, the amount must exceed the recipient's share of that currency. We will unpack the legal reasoning and the requirements of possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage first discusses sulh with both currencies (naqdayn) and then sulh with one currency plus other goods. Identify each scenario clearly.
2
State the ruling for each
Sulh with both currencies is valid regardless of amount because it is an exchange of different types. With one currency plus other goods, it is valid only if the given amount exceeds the recipient's share of that currency; otherwise it is invalid due to riba.
3
Trace the dalil
The commentary uses qiyas: sulh for specific property is a sale (mubadala) because ibra is invalid for non-debts. Sale of gold for silver is allowed due to different types, but equality is required for same type. Thus, if only one currency is given, the portion equal to the share would be an exchange of like for like, which requires equality and immediate possession, and the excess must cover the other assets.
The dalil is aqli (rational).
4
Apply the possession rules
For the part involving sarf (exchange of gold/silver), immediate possession in the session is required. However, if the heir who holds the remainder is in denial (juhud), the previous possession suffices because it becomes a guaranteed possession (qabd daman). If he acknowledges (iqrar), fresh possession must be taken.
The principle: guaranteed possession substitutes for non-guaranteed.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is disagreement on whether sulh is invalid in both agreement and denial or only in agreement. The mufta bihi is invalidity in both, as reported in Al-Kifayah.
This shows the school's cautious approach to riba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نقدين وغيرهما بأحد النقدين لا إلا أن يكون المعطى أكثر من قسطه من ذلك الجنس
إنما يصح عن النقدين : أي الدراهم والدنانير بهما سواء قل البدل أو كثر ؛ لأنه يصرف الجنس إلى خلافِ الجنس على ما عُرِف في «كتاب الصرف». (وفي نقدين وغيرهما بأحدِ النقدين لا إلا أن يكون المعطى أكثر من قسطِهِ من ذلك الجنس)
[1] قوله : لأنه يصرف...الخ ؛ هذا استدلال على صحة الصلح عن نقدين بها. وجملة الاستدلال في هذا المسألة : إن هذا الصلح يحمل على المبادلة ؛ لكونه صلحاً عن عين، ولا يمكن حمله على الإبراء عمّا زاد من نصيبه ؛ لأنَّ الإبراء عن الأعيان الغير المضمونة لا يصح، فتعيَّن البيع وبيع العروض والعقار جائز، سواء كان بالقليل أو بالكثير، وكذا بيع الذهب بالفضة، والفضة بالذهب ؛ لعدم الربا لكون الجنس مختلفاً. لا يقال : إنَّ هذا الصلح لو كان محولاً على البيع فلا بد أن يشترط فيه معرفة مقدار حصته من التركة، فإنَّ جهالته مفسدة للبيع ؛ لأنا نقول : الجهالة مطلقة لا تفسد البيع، بل إذا كانت مفضية إلى المنازعة وإلا فلا.
[۲] قوله : إلى خلاف الجنس ؛ فلا يعتبر التساوي ، لكن يعتبر التقابض في المجلس ؛ لكونه صرفاً إلا أن الوارث الذي في يده بقيّة التركة إن كان جاحداً لكون التركة في يده ، فيكتفى بالقبض السابق، ولا يحتاجُ إلى تجديد القبض ؛ لأنه قبض ضمان، فإنّ الأمانة بالجحود مضمونة، فينوب عن قبض الصلح. وإن كان مضراً له فلا بد من تجديد القبض، بأن يرجع إلى موضع فيه العين ويمضي وقت يتمكن فيه من قبضه ؛ لأنه قبض أمانة، فلا ينوب عن قبض الصلح، والأصل فيه : إنه متى تجانس القبضانِ ناب أحدهما عن الآخر، وإن اختلفا ناب المضمون عن غير المضمون، وغير المضمون عن المضمون. ذكره في «الكفاية)، وغيرها.
[٣] قوله : وفي نقدين وغيرهما ...الخ ؛ يعني إذا كانت التركةُ ذهباً وفضة وغير ذلك من هذه الأجناس فأخرج الورثة أحدهم بأحد النقدين، وأعطوه إما فضة أو ذهباً ، فلا يصح الصلحُ إلا إذا كان ما أعطوه أكثر ذلك الجنس ؛ ليكون نصيبه بمثله، والزيادة بمقابلة حقه من بقية التركة. ولا بد من التقابض فيما يقابل نصيبه من الذهب والفضة؛ لأنه صرف في هذا القدر، أو كان مقدار نصيبه مجهولاً بطل الصلح صرّح به في «العناية»، وغيرها. وذكر في «الكفاية» : قال الحاكم أبو الفضل الله : إنما يبطل الصلح على مثل نصيبه أو أقل من مال الربا في حال التصادق، وأما في حال المناكرة فالصلح جائز ؛ لأنه يعطي المال لدفع الخصومة، فلا يتمكن فيه الربا ، وقيل : إنه باطل في الوجهين؛ لأنه معاوضة في حق المدعي، فيتمكن فيه الربا. انتهى.
And regarding two currencies and other goods with one of the two currencies, it is not valid unless what is given is more than his share of that type. It is only valid with the two currencies, meaning dirhams and dinars, with both whether the substitute is little or much, because it converts the type to a different type as known in the Book of Sarf. (And regarding two currencies and other goods with one of the two currencies, it is not valid unless the given is more than his share of that type.) [1] He said: because it converts... etc.; this is evidence for the validity of sulh for two currencies with them. The summary of evidence in this issue: This sulh is carried on exchange (mubadala) because it is sulh for a specific thing, and it cannot be carried on discharge (ibra) for the excess of his share because ibra for non-guaranteed properties is invalid, so sale is determined, and sale of movable and immovable is permissible, whether little or much, and similarly sale of gold for silver and silver for gold due to absence of riba because the type is different. It cannot be said: If this sulh were converted to sale, then it must require knowledge of the measure of his share from the estate, for ignorance of it is corruptive of sale; for we say: Absolute ignorance does not corrupt sale, but only if it leads to dispute, and if not. [2] He said: to a different type; thus equivalence is not considered, but mutual possession in the session is considered because it is sarf, unless the heir who has the remainder of the estate in his hand is denying that the estate is in his hand, then the previous possession suffices and there is no need for renewal of possession because it is a possession of guarantee (qabd daman), for the trust by denial becomes guaranteed, so it substitutes for possession of sulh. And if he acknowledges (muqirr), then renewal of possession is necessary, so that he returns to a place where the thing is and a time passes in which he can possess it, because it is a possession of trust, so it does not substitute for possession of sulh. The principle is: when the two possessions are of the same type, one substitutes for the other; and if they differ, the guaranteed substitutes for the non-guaranteed, and the non-guaranteed for the guaranteed. This is mentioned in Al-Kifayah and others. [3] He said: And regarding two currencies and other goods... etc.; meaning if the estate includes gold, silver, and other such types, and the heirs exclude one of them with one of the two currencies, giving him either silver or gold, then the sulh is not valid unless what they give is more than that type, so that his share is with its equivalent, and the increase corresponds to his right from the rest of the estate. And mutual possession is necessary concerning what corresponds to his share of gold and silver because it is sarf in this amount, or if the measure of his share is unknown, the sulh becomes invalid, as stated in Al-Inayah and others. And in Al-Kifayah: Al-Hakim Abu al-Fadl said: The sulh becomes invalid for the equivalent of his share or less regarding ribawi wealth in the case of agreement (tasadiq), but in the case of denial (manakara), the sulh is permissible because he gives money to avoid dispute, so riba does not apply. And it is said: It is invalid in both situations because it is a compensation in the right of the claimant, so riba applies. End.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding sulh (settlement) when the estate contains both gold and silver (the two currencies) along with other goods. The commentator explains that if the settlement is made using both currencies, it is valid regardless of the amount, because it is considered a sale (mubadala) of one type for another, which is not subject to riba. However, if the settlement is made using only one currency along with other goods, it is not valid unless the amount given exceeds the recipient's share of that currency. This excess ensures that the portion equal to the share is exchanged for itself (which would be invalid due to riba if equal or less), while the excess covers the other types in the estate. The commentary also discusses the requirement of mutual possession (taqabud) in the session, noting exceptions when the heir is in denial or acknowledgment. It clarifies that ignorance of the exact share does not necessarily invalidate the contract unless it leads to dispute. The passage concludes with a reference to Al-Kifayah, which presents two opinions on the invalidity of sulh when the amount is equal to or less than the share in ribawi goods: one view invalidates it only if the parties agree on the value, while another invalidates it in all cases because it is a compensation.

Key Terms
نقدين (naqdayn): The two currencies: gold and silver (dinar and dirham).
صرف (sarf): The exchange of gold for silver or vice versa; subject to specific rules including immediate possession.
إبراء (ibra): A discharge or release from a debt; in this context, it is invalid for non-guaranteed property (specific assets not in the form of debts).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a settlement where the estate contains gold, silver, and other goods, and the heirs give the departing heir only silver, what condition must be met for the sulh to be valid?
A) The silver given must be exactly equal to his share of silver in the estate.
B) The silver given must be more than his share of silver in the estate.
C) The silver given must be less than his share of silver in the estate.
D) The silver given can be any amount as long as the parties agree.

Key Takeaway
Sulh involving ribawi goods must be treated as sale; when using one currency only, the amount given must exceed the recipient's share to avoid riba.

Invalidity of Sulh When Debts Are Stipulated for Remaining Heirs
Ruling on the invalidity of sulh if debts are conditionally assigned to other heirs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the invalidity of sulh when debts are conditionally assigned to remaining heirs and explain the reasoning based on the prohibition of riba and improper debt transfer.

Core Idea
A sulh that stipulates debts for remaining heirs is invalid because it constitutes improper debt transfer and leads to riba.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in inheritance settlements: when one heir is bought out and the remaining heirs stipulate that debts from the estate belong to them. The Hanafi school rules that such a condition invalidates the entire settlement. Understanding this ruling requires analyzing the nature of the settlement as either a sale or a discharge, and how riba considerations apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? (Sulh where a condition is made that debts go to remaining heirs) Ruling: batil.
2
Locate the dalil
The reasoning is based on the principle that ibra for specific property is invalid, and the settlement must be treated as a sale. The text also uses analogy to riba.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition of riba applies because part of the estate remains unpriced.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is a dispute: some say it is only invalid if the parties agree on the value, but valid if there is denial. The preferred view is invalid in both cases.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The mufta bihi is that the entire settlement is invalid.

وبطل الصلح إن شرط فيه لهم الدين من التركة) : يعني إن أخرج أحد الورثة ، وفي التركة ديون ، فشرط أن يكون الديون لبقية الورثة ، بطل الصلح من العقار والعروض، وكانت التركةُ مشتملة على هذه الأجناس فأخرج الورثة أحدهم بأحد النقدين، وأعطوه إما فضة أو ذهباً ، فلا يصح الصلحُ إلا إذا كان ما أعطوه أكثر ذلك الجنس ؛ ليكون نصيبه بمثله، والزيادة بمقابلة حقه من بقية التركة. ولا بد من التقابض فيما يقابل نصيبه من الذهب والفضة؛ لأنه صرف في هذا القدر، أو كان مقدار نصيبه مجهولاً بطل الصلح صرّح به في «العناية»، وغيرها. وذكر في «الكفاية» : قال الحاكم أبو الفضل الله : إنما يبطل الصلح على مثل نصيبه أو أقل من مال الربا في حال التصادق، وأما في حال المناكرة فالصلح جائز ؛ لأنه يعطي المال لدفع الخصومة، فلا يتمكن فيه الربا ، وقيل : إنه باطل في الوجهين؛ لأنه معاوضة في حق المدعي، فيتمكن فيه الربا. انتهى.
[1] قوله : وذلك لأن الصلح ... الخ ؛ يعني اشتراط كون المعطى أكثر من : نصيبه من ذلك الجنس في صحة ذلك الصلح أنّ الصلح لا يجوز بطريق الإبراء عن الأعيان، فإنّ الإبراء عن الأعيان باطل ؛ لأنَّ الإسقاط إنما يستعمل في الديون لا في الأعيان. وما نحن فيه من الأعيان لا من الديون، فتعين تجويزه بطريق المعاوضة، ولا يمكن هاهنا ؛ لأنه يبقى من التركة بلا ثمن في ضمن المعاوضة، فصار ربا، فلا جرم أن يزيد على نصيبه ؛ لئلا يلزم الربا.
(٢) قوله : بطل الصلحُ في الكلّ ؛ أي في الدين والعين جميعاً، أما في حصة الدين ؛ فلان فيه تمليك الدين الذي هو حصة المصالح من غير من عليه الدين وهم
And the sulh becomes invalid if it is stipulated therein that the debts from the estate are for them (the remaining heirs). That is, if one heir is excluded and in the estate there are debts, and it is stipulated that the debts are for the remaining heirs, the sulh becomes invalid for the real estate and movables. And if the estate consists of these types and the heirs exclude one of them with one of the two currencies, giving him either silver or gold, then the sulh is not valid unless what they give is more than that type, so that his share is with its equivalent, and the increase corresponds to his right from the rest of the estate. And mutual possession is necessary concerning what corresponds to his share of gold and silver because it is sarf in this amount, or if the measure of his share is unknown, the sulh becomes invalid, as stated in Al-Inayah and others. And in Al-Kifayah: Al-Hakim Abu al-Fadl said: The sulh becomes invalid for the equivalent of his share or less regarding ribawi wealth in the case of agreement, but in the case of denial, the sulh is permissible because he gives money to avoid dispute, so riba does not apply. And it is said: It is invalid in both situations because it is a compensation in the right of the claimant, so riba applies. End. [1] He said: that is because the sulh... etc.; meaning the condition that the given is more than his share of that type for the validity of that sulh is that the sulh is not allowed by way of ibra for specific property, for ibra for specific property is invalid because cancellation (isqaat) is only used for debts, not for specific property. And what we are dealing with is specific property, not debts, so it must be permitted by way of compensation, and it is not possible here because there remains from the estate without price in the compensation, so it becomes riba; therefore, it is necessary that he increase over his share to avoid riba. (2) He said: The sulh becomes invalid in entirety; i.e., in both debt and property. As for the share of debt, because in it there is transferring ownership of the debt which is the share of the settling party from other than the debtor, and they... (incomplete).
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage begins with the ruling that if a settlement stipulates that the debts in the estate go to the remaining heirs, the entire settlement becomes invalid. The commentator explains the reasoning: ibra (discharge) for specific property is invalid, so the settlement must be treated as a sale. However, if the debts are given to the other heirs, it constitutes an improper transfer of debt without the debtor's consent, which is invalid. The commentary then discusses the condition that the amount given to the excluded heir must exceed his share to avoid riba. It also references a difference of opinion from Al-Kifayah: according to one view, the settlement is only invalid if the parties agree on the value, but if there is denial, it is permissible to settle for peace. The preferred view is that it is invalid in both cases. The passage ends with a portion of commentary stating that the invalidity applies to both debt and property, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra): Discharge or release from a debt; here, it is argued that ibra for specific property (ayn) is invalid.
تمليك الدين (tamlik al-dayn): Transferring ownership of a debt; requires the debtor's consent or conditions, otherwise invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If in a sulh, the remaining heirs stipulate that debts belong to them, what is the ruling on the entire settlement according to the Hanafi mufta bihi?
A) Valid
B) Invalid only for debts
C) Entirely invalid
D) Valid if the excluded heir agrees

Key Takeaway
A sulh that improperly assigns debts to remaining heirs is invalid in its entirety due to the prohibition of riba and improper debt transfer.
