Three Hanafi Stratagems for Valid Sulh of Debt
Explaining three stratagems for valid sulh when debtor is not present

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi stratagems for making a valid sulh involving debt, explain the ruling, and apply the preferred method.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits three stratagems to make a sulh involving debt valid; the lending method is the best.

When an heir wishes to settle the estate's debts by exchanging his share of the debt for tangible assets from other heirs, a direct exchange is problematic because the debt is not owed by them. The Hanafi school permits three legal stratagems (hiyal) to achieve a valid sulh. This lesson explains each stratagem, their legal basis, and which is preferred, including an intra-Hanafi dispute on combining debt and tangible assets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core problem
Understand why a simple sulh of debt with a non-debtor is invalid: transferring ownership of debt requires the debtor's involvement or a clear legal mechanism.
The muslih (settling heir) wants to give up his share of the estate's debt in exchange for something from other heirs, but those heirs are not the debtors.
2
List the three stratagems
From the passage, extract: (1) stipulating release of debtors, (2) heirs paying the muslih's share in cash voluntarily, (3) lending the muslih his share and then settling on the rest.
Each stratagem bypasses the direct exchange of debt with non-debtors.
3
Analyze the legal basis
Each stratagem relies on a valid transfer mechanism: release (ibra) of debt, gifting cash, or lending followed by assignment (hawala) of debt.
The underlying principle is that debt can be transferred to the debtor via hawala or released via ibra, making the sulh valid.
4
Compare the stratagems
Weigh benefits and harms: first stratagem benefits heirs by speeding debtor payment; second harms heirs by using cash; third avoids harm and is considered the best.
The text explicitly states the third (lending) is 'the best of methods'.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
If debt and tangible assets are combined in one sulh without apportioning the price, Abu Hanifa rules the whole void; the Sahibayn allow it for the tangible part if specified.
This dispute affects how the stratagems are applied when mixed assets are involved.

والأوجه : أن يقرضوا المصالح مقدار نصيبه، ويصالحوا عما وراء الدين، ويحيلهم على استيفاء نصيبه من الغرماء. انتهى. وقال الزيلعي في «شرحه على الكنز» : وهذا في الوجهين ظاهر ؛ لأنهم إذا أعطوا المصالح شيئاً بمقابلة الدين أو قدرِ الدِّينِ ولم يحصل لهم الدين بمقابله، فانتفى الضرر عنهم إلا ضرر القديم، فإن العين خير من الدين.
الحيلة الأولى : أن يشترطوا أن يُبْرِئَ المصالح الغرماء عن حصته من الدين، ويصالح عن أعيان التركة بمال وفي هذا الوجهِ فائدة لبقية الورثة ؛ لأن المصالح لا يبقى له على الغرماء حق لا أن حصته من الدين تصير لهم.
والثانية : إن بقية الورثة يؤدون إلى المصالح نصيبه نقداً، ويحيل لهم حصته من الدين على الغرماء، وفي هذا الوجه يتضرر بقيَّةُ الورثة ؛ لأنَّ النقد خيرٌ من الدين.
والثالثة : وهي أحسنُ الطُّرق، وهي الإقراض، فلنفرض أن حصة المصالح من الدين مئة درهم ومن العين مئة أيضاً ، وهم يصالحون على الدراهم فلا بُدَّ أن يكون بدل الصلح أكثر من مئة ، وهو مئة وعشرة دراهم، فيقرضونه مئة ، وهو يحيلهم بالمئة على الغرماء ، وهم يقبلون الحوالة ، ثُمَّ يصالحون عن غير الدين على عشرة، فإن كان غير الدين بحيث يجوز الصلح عنه بعشرة فظاهر، وإن لم يكن يزاد على العشرة شيء آخر، كسكين مثلاً ؛ ليكون العشرة في مقابل العشرة، والباقي في مقابلة السكين. والأوجه منه أن يبيعوه كفّاً من تمر أو نحوه بقدر الدين، ثمّ يحيلهم على الغرماء أو يحيلهم ابتداء من غير بيع شيء ليقضوه له، ثم يأخذوه لأنفسهم. انتهى. فتفكر فيه واحفظه.
قوله : وأحالهم ...الخ ؛ أي أحال المصالح الورثة فدفع عنهم ضرر إقراضه ، وانتفعوا بمصالحته عما وراء الدين.
قوله : وفي هذا الوجه فائدة لبقية الورثة ؛ أي في اشتراط إبراء المصالح الغرماء من حصته من الدين، والمصالحة عن أعيان التركة بمال فائدة لبقية الورثة ؛ لأن المصالح لا يبقى له على الغرماء حق، فالغرماء يتعجّلون في الأداء ؛ لأنّ أداء البعض أسهل من أداء الكل، والتعجيل فائدته للورثة. وفي بعض النسخ: لا فائدة لبقية الورثة لكن قوله : فإنَّ المصالح ... الخ ؛ ينافيه نعم وجه عدم فائدتهم أنهم لا يتمكنهم الرجوع على الغرماء بقدر نصيب المصالح، كما
If they stipulate the release of the debtors from it, or they pay the share of the settling person from it voluntarily, or they lend him the amount of his installment from it, and they settle for the rest with the heirs. And as for the share of the tangible asset: because when the share of the debt was void, it becomes void in the tangible asset as well; because the transaction is one, whether the share of the debt is made known or not. This is the doctrine of the Imam, and it should be permissible according to them (the two) in other than debt if he makes known its share. The origin of the dispute is regarding when he combines a slave and a free person, or a slaughtered sheep and a carrion, and sells them in one transaction, and makes known the share of each in the price; it is void in all according to him, and valid regarding the slave and the slaughtered sheep according to them, and its mention has preceded.
His saying: 'because it is transferring ownership of debt from someone other than the debtor...' etc.; for the settling person becomes the owner of his share of the debt from the rest of the heirs by what he takes from them of the tangible asset. Thus in al-Kafi.
His saying: 'stratagems'; i.e., three stratagems: the first is what he mentioned by his saying: 'if they stipulate the release of the debtors'; the second: what he mentioned by his saying: 'or they pay the share of the settling person from it voluntarily'; the third: what he mentioned by his saying: 'or they lend him the amount of his installment from it...' etc.
His saying: 'if they stipulate...' etc.; He said in al-Hidayah: And if they stipulate that the debtors are released from it, and they do not have recourse against them for the share of the settling person, then the compromise is valid; because it is a waiver or transfer of debt from the one upon whom it is due, and it is valid. This is a stratagem for permissibility. Another is that they hasten to pay his share voluntarily. In both aspects there is harm to the rest of the heirs.
The most appropriate is that they lend the settling person the amount of his share, and make a compromise regarding what is beyond the debt, and assign them (the heirs) to collect his share from the debtors. End. Al-Zayla'i said in his commentary on al-Kanz: This is apparent in the two aspects; because when they give the settling person something in exchange for the debt or the amount of the debt, and they do not obtain the debt in exchange for it, then the harm is removed from them except the old harm, for tangible assets are better than debt.
The first stratagem: that they stipulate that the settling person releases the debtors from his share of the debt, and makes a compromise regarding the assets of the estate for a sum. In this aspect there is benefit for the rest of the heirs; because the settling person has no right left against the debtors, not that his share of the debt becomes theirs.
The second: that the rest of the heirs pay to the settling person his share in cash, and assign him his share of the debt against the debtors. In this aspect the rest of the heirs are harmed; because cash is better than debt.
The third: and it is the best of the methods: lending. Let us suppose that the share of the settling person from the debt is a hundred dirhams and from the tangible assets also a hundred, and they are making a compromise on dirhams, so the consideration for the compromise must be more than a hundred, and it is a hundred and ten dirhams. So they lend him a hundred, and he assigns them (the heirs) the hundred against the debtors, and they accept the assignment. Then they make a compromise regarding other than the debt for ten. If what is other than the debt is such that compromise upon it for ten is allowed, then it is apparent. And if not, something else is added to the ten, like a knife, so that the ten is in exchange for the ten, and the remainder is in exchange for the knife. The more appropriate method is that they sell him a handful of dates or the like for the amount of the debt, then assign them against the debtors, or assign them initially without selling anything for them to pay him, then they take it for themselves. End. So ponder it and memorize it.
His saying: 'and assigned them...' etc.; i.e., the settling person assigned the heirs (to the debtors), so he removed from them the harm of his lending, and they benefited from his compromise regarding what is beyond the debt.
His saying: 'In this aspect there is benefit for the rest of the heirs'; i.e., in stipulating the release of the settling person of the debtors from his share of the debt, and compromising on the assets of the estate for a sum, there is benefit for the rest of the heirs; because the settling person has no right left against the debtors, so the debtors hasten to pay; because paying part is easier than paying all, and acceleration benefits the heirs. In some copies: 'there is no benefit for the rest of the heirs,' but his saying: 'for the settling person...' etc. contradicts it. Indeed, the aspect of their lack of benefit is that they are not able to have recourse against the debtors for the amount of the settling person's share, as
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi fiqh commentary explaining the legal stratagems (hiyal) for making a compromise (sulh) valid when one of the heirs wants to settle with the debtors of the estate. The core issue is that the settling heir (muslih) wants to exchange his share of the debt (which is a receivable) for some tangible asset from the other heirs, but the debt is not owed by those heirs. The stratagems allow this to be valid.

First, the author explains the three stratagems mentioned in the matn: (1) the heirs stipulate that the debtors are released from the settling heir's share; (2) the heirs pay the settling heir's share of the debt in cash voluntarily; (3) the heirs lend the settling heir the amount of his share, and then they settle on the rest (non-debt assets).

Then there is a discussion of an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding when the transaction combines both debt and tangible assets (ayn) in one sulh. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if the price is not apportioned (i.e., the share of the debt is not clearly separated in the consideration), the entire transaction is void. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), it is valid for the tangible asset part if the share is specified. This is analogous to the ruling on selling a slave together with a free person or a slaughtered sheep with carrion.

The passage then details each stratagem with commentary from al-Hidayah and al-Zayla'i. The first stratagem (stipulating release) benefits the other heirs because the debtors will pay faster. The second stratagem (cash payment) harms the other heirs because they give up cash. The third stratagem (lending) is the best as it avoids harm: the heirs lend the settling heir his share, he assigns them the debt, and then they settle for a small amount on the remaining assets. The commentary explains that if there are no suitable non-debt assets, they can add a small item like a knife to make the sulh valid.

The overall ruling is that these stratagems are permissible and valid in the Hanafi school, and they are taught for practical application.

Key Terms
حيلة (hila): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible result by indirect means.
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement between parties, often involving a transaction.
مصالح (muslih): The person who makes the compromise (the settling heir in this context).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which stratagem is considered the best method for valid sulh of debt?
A) Stipulating release of debtors
B) Paying the settling heir's share in cash
C) Lending the settling heir his share and then settling on the rest
D) Combining debt and tangible assets without apportioning

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school provides three stratagems to make a sulh involving debt valid; the lending method is the most practical because it avoids hardship for the other heirs.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Settlement Over Unknown Estate
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of sulh with makil/mawzun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on sulh over an unknown estate with measurable/weighable items, explain the reasoning behind each, and apply the principle that a remote suspicion (shubhat al-shubha) does not invalidate a transaction.

Core Idea
A remote doubt (shubhat al-shubha) does not invalidate a settlement, even if riba is theoretically possible.

The validity of a settlement (sulh) when the estate's contents are unknown can be contentious, especially when the settlement involves items of the same type as what might be in the estate. This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: some scholars invalidate it due to a mere suspicion of riba, while others uphold it because that suspicion is too remote. Understanding this distinction is key to applying Hanafi rules on permissible transactions and avoiding unnecessary prohibition.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the two positions
Identify that the dispute concerns a settlement (sulh) over an unknown estate when the settlement is made on a measurable (makil) or weighable (mawzun) item. Note that some scholars say it is invalid, others say it is valid.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
2
Trace the argument for invalidity
Explain Imam al-Marghinani's view: the settlement may involve riba if the estate contains the same type of item as the settlement substitute and the settling party's share is less. This possibility makes it impermissible.
The fear is that the excess over the actual share constitutes riba.
3
Trace the argument for validity
Explain Faqih Abu Ja'far al-Hindwani's view: the suspicion of riba is only a 'suspicion of a suspicion' (shubhat al-shubha) because it is equally possible that the estate contains no such item or that the share is not less. Such remote conjecture is not legally considered.
Key term: shubhat al-shubha.
4
Examine the tarjih (preference)
Note that Qadi Khan and al-Zayla'i prefer the valid view, as the remote suspicion does not outweigh the permissibility of the settlement.
The mufta bihi is validity.
5
Apply the principle to similar cases
Consider a scenario where only part of the estate is unknown; the same reasoning applies if the suspicion remains remote.
The principle is not limited to estates but applies to any transaction with a remote suspicion of riba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي صحة الصلح عن تركة جهلت على مكيل أو موزون اختلاف. فعند بعض المشايخ: لا يجوز لشبهة الربا، وعند البعض: يجوز؛ لأنه هاهنا شبهة شبهة الربا، ولا اعتبار لها؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يكون في التركة من جنس بدل الصلح. ذكره في «الكافي» وغيره.
[١] قوله: على مكيل أو موزون؛ متعلق بالصلح، يعني إذا لم يكن في التركة دين وأعيانها غير معلومة، وأريد الصلح على مكيل أو موزون، قيل: يصح، وقيل: لا يصح؛ أما عدم الصحة فلاحتمال أن يكون في التركة مكيل أو موزون من نصيبه، من ذلك مثل بدل الصلح فيكون ربا. وأما الصحة فلاحتمال أن لا يكون في التركة مكيل أو موزون، وإن كان فيحتمل أن يكون نصيبه أقل من بدل الصلح، فكان القول بعدم الجواز مؤدياً إلى اعتبار شبهة الشبهة، ولا اعتبار لها، هذا هو الصحيح، صرح به الزيلعي وغيره.
[٢] قوله: لا يجوز؛ وهو قول الإمام ظهير الدين المرغيناني. كذا في «الكفاية».
[٣] قوله: لشبهة؛ فإن التركة يحتمل أن يكون فيها كيلي أو وزني، وبدل الصلح مثل نصيب المصالح من ذلك أو أقل، فإن ما زاد على بدل الصلح من نصيب المصالح يكون رباً لا محالة.
[٤] قوله: يجوز؛ وهو قول الفقيه أبي جعفر الهندواني. كذا في الكفاية.
[٥] قوله: لأن هاهنا ... الخ؛ حاصله: إن في التركة يحتمل أن لا يكون فيها كيلي أو وزني، وبدل الصلح مثل نصيب المصالح في ذلك، وعلى تقدير أن يكون كذلك يحتمل أن يكون نصيبه أقل من بدل الصلح، فكان القول بعدم الجواز مؤدياً إلى اعتبار شبهة الشبهة، وهي ساقطة الاعتبار.
وقال قاضي خان في «فتاواه»: الصحيح ما قاله الفقيه أبو جعفر؛ لأن الثابت هاهنا شبهة الشبهة، وذلك لا يعتبر.
And there is disagreement regarding the validity of a settlement (sulh) for an unknown estate when it is upon a measurable or weighable item. Some scholars say: it is not valid due to the suspicion of riba. Others say: it is valid, because here there is a suspicion of a suspicion of riba, and it is not considered, because it is possible that the estate contains the same type as the settlement substitute. Mentioned in al-Kafi and others. [1] His saying: 'upon a measurable or weighable' is attached to the settlement, meaning if there is no debt in the estate and its specific items are unknown, and a settlement is desired upon a measurable or weighable item, it is said: it is valid, and it is said: it is not valid. As for invalidity, it is because it is possible that in the estate there is a measurable or weighable item from his share, like the settlement substitute, so it would be riba. As for validity, it is because it is possible that there is no measurable or weighable item in the estate, and if there is, it is possible that his share is less than the settlement substitute. So the view of impermissibility leads to considering a suspicion of a suspicion, which is not considered. This is the correct view, stated by al-Zayla'i and others. [2] His saying: 'it is not valid' is the view of Imam Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani, as in al-Kifaya. [3] His saying: 'due to the suspicion' — the estate may contain a measurable or weighable item, and the settlement substitute is like the share of the settling party from that or less, for whatever exceeds the settlement substitute from the share of the settling party is necessarily riba. [4] His saying: 'it is valid' is the view of Faqih Abu Ja'far al-Hindwani, as in al-Kifaya. [5] His saying: 'because here...' — its summary: it is possible that the estate contains no measurable or weighable item, and the settlement substitute is like the share of the settling party in that, and if it is so, it is possible that his share is less than the settlement substitute. So the view of impermissibility leads to considering a suspicion of a suspicion, which is disregarded. And Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: the correct view is what Faqih Abu Ja'far said, because what is established here is a suspicion of a suspicion, and that is not considered.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a compromise (sulh) over an unknown estate when the settlement is made on a measurable (makil) or weighable (mawzun) item. The core concern is the potential for riba. The matn states that there is ikhtilaf. Some scholars, including Imam al-Marghinani, hold that the settlement is invalid due to the suspicion of riba: since the estate may contain items of the same type as the settlement substitute, any excess in the settlement would be riba. Others, including Faqih Abu Ja'far al-Hindwani, hold that it is valid because the suspicion is only a 'suspicion of a suspicion' (shubhat al-shubha) and thus not legally effective. The commentary explains that the fear of riba arises from the possibility that the estate contains the same type of item as the settlement substitute and that the share of the settling party might be less than the settlement; however, the opposite possibility (that the estate contains no such item or that his share is less) is equally plausible. To declare invalidity would be to consider a remote suspicion that is itself based on another suspicion. Qadi Khan endorses the view of Abu Ja'far as the correct position. The passage also notes that if the estate is known to be debt-free and its specific items are unknown, the ruling applies. This dispute illustrates the Hanafi principle that mere possibilities (shubah) that do not reach certainty are not sufficient to invalidate transactions.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A transaction aiming to end a dispute by mutual agreement, often involving a compromise on property rights.
تركة جهلت (tarikatun juhilat): An estate whose exact composition or value is unknown, often in the context of inheritance before distribution.
شبهة الربا (shubhat al-riba): A suspicion or possibility that the transaction involves riba (usury).
شبهة الشبهة (shubhat al-shubha): A suspicion that is itself based on another suspicion; i.e., a remote and unlikely possibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Qadi Khan, what is the correct position on a settlement over an unknown estate when the settlement is on a measurable or weighable item?
A) It is invalid due to the suspicion of riba.
B) It is valid because the suspicion is only a 'suspicion of a suspicion'.
C) It is only valid if the estate contains no measurable items.
D) It is valid only if both parties agree explicitly.

Key Takeaway
A settlement over an unknown estate with measurable/weighable items is valid according to the preferred Hanafi view, as the suspicion of riba is too remote to consider.

Settlement Over Unknown Estate of Non-Measurable Items in Heirs' Hands
Ruling on sulh when unknown estate consists of physical items held by remaining heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a settlement over an unknown estate of physical items held by heirs and explain the reasoning based on the principle of jahala leading to dispute.

Core Idea
When ignorance doesn't stir dispute, a sale of unknown a'yan is valid – even if the estate is unknown.

When heirs inherit an estate of physical items whose exact composition is unknown, can they settle among themselves by one giving up his share for known compensation? Hanafi scholars differ: some invalidate it as a sale of an unknown item. This lesson examines the dispute and the preferred view that validates it when the estate is already in the hands of the heirs, because the ignorance does not lead to future conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and the issue
The case involves a sulh (settlement) over an unknown estate of physical items (a'yan). The question: Is it valid?
Sulh is a contract that can be a sale or release.
2
State the opposing view (invalidity)
Some Hanafi scholars argue it is invalid because the settlement is a sale of an unknown item, which is prohibited.
They say it cannot be an ibra' because releasing a physical item is not valid.
3
State the preferred view (validity)
The more correct view (asahh) holds it valid because ignorance (jahala) only prohibits a sale if it leads to dispute (manaza'a). Since the estate is already in the hands of the remaining heirs, no delivery is needed, so no dispute arises.
This is based on a general Hanafi principle.
4
Identify the key principle
The decisive factor is whether the ignorance leads to dispute. This is a Hanafi usul principle: gharar (uncertainty) is tolerated as long as it does not cause future conflict.
This principle appears in many chapters of Hanafi fiqh.
5
Apply the principle to a contrasting case
If part of the estate is not in the heirs' hands, or if it is measurable/weighable, the ruling may differ because delivery is required, leading to potential dispute.
The commentary mentions this exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جهلت وهي غير المكيل والموزون في يد البقية صح في الأصح. وجه عدم الصحة: أن هذا الصلح بيع لا إبراء؛ لأن البراءة عن الأعيان لا يجوز، وإذا كان بيعاً، فأحد البدلين مجهول، فلا يصح. وجه الصحة: أن التركة إذا كانت في يد بقية الورثة، فالجهالة لا تفضى إلى المنازعة فيجوز.
[١] قوله: يكون شبهة الشبهة؛ لأنه ولو علم أعيان التركة، ولكن جهل قدر بدل الصلح من نصيب المصالح يكون شبهة، فإذا لم يعلم أعيان التركة يكون شبهة الشبهة؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يكون في التركة كيلي أو وزني ويحتمل أن لا يكون، كما مر غير مرة.
[٢] قوله: ولو جهلت.... الخ؛ يعني لو كانت التركة مجهولة، وهي ليست مما يكال أو يوزن، بل هي أعيان، فصالحوا على مكيل أو موزون أو غير ذلك، فقيل: لا يصح، والأصح أنه يصح.
[٣] قوله: وجه عدم الصحة أن ... الخ؛ يعني دليل حكم عدم الصحة على ما قال به بعضهم: إن هذا الصلح إما بيع أو إبراء، والشق الثاني باطل، فإن المصالح عين، والإبراء عن العين باطل، وعلى الشق الأول كون التركة مجهولة مانع عن الجواز، فإن بيع المجهول لا يجوز.
[٤] قوله: وجه الصحة أن ... الخ؛ يعني دليل حكم الصحة كما هو المختار أن هذا بيع، وكون التركة مجهولة لا يمنع جواز البيع، فإن الجهالة مطلقة لا تمنعه، بل إذا كانت مفضية إلى المنازعة. والتركة لما كانت في يد بقية الورثة فجهالتها لا تفضي إلى المنازعة، فإنه لا تحتاج حينئذ إلى التسليم؛ ولهذا قالوا: إن بعض التركة لو كان في يد بقية الورثة ولا يعرفون ما مقداره فلا يجوز الصلح؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى التسليم، فجهالته تفضي إلى المنازعة.
And if it (the estate) is unknown and it is not of the measurable or weighable type, and it is in the hands of the remaining (heirs), it is valid according to the more correct view. The aspect of invalidity: this settlement is a sale, not a release, because release from physical items is not permissible; and if it is a sale, one of the two compensations is unknown, so it is not valid. The aspect of validity: when the estate is in the hands of the remaining heirs, the ignorance does not lead to dispute, so it is permissible. [1] His saying: 'it is a suspicion of a suspicion' — because even if the specific items of the estate were known, but the amount of the settlement substitute relative to the share of the settling party was unknown, that would be a suspicion; if the specific items of the estate are also unknown, it becomes a suspicion of a suspicion, because it is possible that the estate contains a measurable or weighable item and possible that it does not, as has repeatedly been mentioned. [2] His saying: 'and if it is unknown...' — meaning if the estate is unknown and it is not the type that is measured or weighed, but rather physical items, and they settle upon a measurable or weighable item or otherwise, it is said: it is not valid, but the more correct view is that it is valid. [3] His saying: 'the aspect of invalidity...' — meaning the evidence for the ruling of invalidity according to some: this settlement is either a sale or a release; the second is invalid because the settling party is a physical item, and release from a physical item is invalid; as for the first, the estate being unknown prevents permissibility because the sale of an unknown item is not valid. [4] His saying: 'the aspect of validity...' — meaning the evidence for the ruling of validity as the chosen view: this is a sale, and the estate being unknown does not prevent the permissibility of the sale, because absolute ignorance does not prevent it, but only if it leads to dispute. And since the estate is in the hands of the remaining heirs, its ignorance does not lead to dispute, because then there is no need for delivery; for this reason they said: if part of the estate is in the hands of the remaining heirs and they do not know its quantity, the settlement is not valid, because it requires delivery, so its ignorance leads to dispute.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on a compromise (sulh) over an unknown estate that consists of physical items (a'yan) not measurable or weighable, when the estate is in the hands of the remaining heirs. The matn states that the more correct view (asahh) is that the settlement is valid. The text then gives two competing opinions: the first says it is invalid because the settlement is a sale (not an ibra'), and selling an unknown item is not permissible. The second, which is chosen, says it is valid because although the estate is unknown, the ignorance does not lead to dispute (manaza'a) since the estate is already in the hands of the heirs—there is no need for delivery (taslim) that would cause conflict. The commentary elaborates that if a portion of the estate is in the hands of some heirs and the quantity is unknown, then the settlement is invalid precisely because delivery is needed and ignorance would cause dispute. This highlights the Hanafi principle that jahala (ignorance) in contracts is tolerated as long as it does not lead to futuristic dispute.

Key Terms
أعيان (a'yan): Physical items, tangible property, as opposed to measurable or weighable commodities.
جهالة (jahala): Ignorance about the subject matter of a contract; in this context, it refers to not knowing the exact composition or quantity of the estate.
المنازعة (al-manaza'a): Dispute or conflict between parties; a key factor in determining validity of a contract with ignorance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the settlement is considered a release (ibra'), not a sale.
B) Because the ignorance does not lead to dispute since no delivery is needed.
C) Because the estate is known to the heirs only.
D) Because the settlement is on a measurable item.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a settlement over an unknown physical estate that is in the heirs' hands because the ignorance does not lead to dispute.

Compromise and Division with Estate Debt: Qiyas vs Istihsan
Ruling on compromise and division with estate debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sulh and qisma when the deceased's debt covers the estate partially or fully, and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in the case of non-covering debt.

Core Idea
Debt covering the entire estate voids compromise; for lesser debt, istihsan permits partial division to avoid hardship.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on compromise and division of an estate when the deceased has unpaid debts. The key distinction is whether the debt covers the entire estate (muhit) or only part of it (ghayr muhit). While strict analogy (qiyas) would require deferring all division until debts are paid, the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to allow partial division in non-covering cases, protecting heirs from unnecessary hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios and Their Rulings
Distinguish between debt that covers the entire estate (muhit) and debt that does not (ghayr muhit). For muhit, sulh and qisma are batil (void). For ghayr muhit, the istihsan position allows them, but the debt amount is withheld.
The passage states 'وبطل الصلح والقسمة مع دين محيط' (they are void with covering debt) and later allows division by istihsan for non-covering debt.
2
Understand the Qiyas Argument
Qiyas holds that every part of the estate is encumbered by debt, so no ownership transfers to heirs until all debts are paid. Thus, both sulh and qisma are invalid regardless of debt size.
The passage says: 'وجه القياس أنّ الدين يمنع تملك الوارث... فصار كالمستغرق' (debt prevents ownership, so it is like covering debt).
3
Recognize the Istihsan Exception
Istihsan departs from qiyas to avoid ṣarar (hardship) on the heirs, as people commonly have small debts. The heirs own the estate subject to the debt, and the debt amount is set aside until paid.
The passage: 'ووجه الاستحسان لزوم ضرر الورثة... فقلنا بأنهم يملكونه دفعاً للضرر' (basis of istihsan is harm to heirs; we say they own it to prevent harm).
4
Apply the Conditions and Exceptions
Note that compromise is makruh before payment due to the priority of the deceased's needs. Also, if a guarantor takes responsibility or heirs set aside specific property for the debt, sulh becomes valid even before payment.
The passage mentions guarantees and setting aside property as valid ways to enable sulh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطل الصلح والقسمة مع دين محيط للتّركة. ولا يصالح قبل القضاء في غير محيط ، ولو فعل قالوا صح، ووُقِفَ قَدْرُ الدين، وقُسِّمَ الباقي استحساناً ، ووَقَفَ الكلِّ قياسا. (قوله : وبطل الصلح ...الخ ؛ يعني إذا كان على الميت دين مستغرق للتركة بطل الصلح مع أحد الورثة ليخرجوه عنها ، وقسمة التركة بينهم، فإنّ الورثة لا يملكون التركة في هذه الحالة، فإنَّ الدين المستغرق يمنع وقوع الملكِ في التركة ؛ لتقدّم قضاء الدين على الإرث.) (وقال الزيلعي في «شرح الكنز » ) : ولو ضمن رجل بشرط أن لا يرجع في التركةِ جاز الصلح ؛ لأنّ هذا كفالة بشرط براءةِ الأصيل، وهو الميت، فتصير حوالة، فيخلو مال الميت عن الدين، فيجوز تصرفهم فيه. انتهى. (وذكر الطحطاوي نقلاً عن «الظهيرية»: ولو لم يضمن الوراث ولكن عزلوا عيناً لدين الميت فيه وفاءً بالدين، ثم صالحوا في الباقي على نحو ما قلنا جاز. انتهى. قال العلامة المقدسي : فلو هلك المعزولُ لا بدَّ من نقض قسمته. انتهـى مـا في «حاشية الطحطاوي). (قوله : ينبغي أن لا يصالح ؛ لتقدّم حاجة الميت، أعني قضاء دينه، فيكره ، وهل هي تنزيهية أو تحريمية، ففيه اختلاف.) (قوله : وجه القياس أنّ الدين...الخ ; حاصله : إنَّ الدين يمنع تملك الوارث، فإنَّ كلَّ جزء من التركةِ مشغول بالدين ؛ لعدم الأولوية بالصرف إلى جزء دون جزء، فصار كالمستغرق، فيمنع من دخوله في ملك الورثة، فلا تجوز القسمة قبل قضائه.) (ووجه الاستحسان لزوم ضرر الورثة؛ حاصله : إنّ الإنسان لا يخلو عن دين قليل، فغير المستغرق من الدين لو منع تملك الوارث أدّى إلى الحرج، أو إلى أن لا يملكوا أصلاً، فقلنا بأنهم يملكونه دفعاً للضرر عنهم إلا أنهم يرجعون من التركةِ قدر الدين، ويترك حتى يقضى به الدين، كيلا يحتاجون إلى نقض القسمة.)
And the compromise and division are void with debt covering the estate. And one should not compromise before payment in non-covering debt, and if he does, they said it is valid, and the amount of the debt is withheld, and the remainder is divided by istihsan, and the whole is withheld by qiyas. (His saying: 'and the compromise and division...' meaning if the deceased had debt covering the estate, compromise with one of the heirs to remove it and division of the estate among them is void, because the heirs do not own the estate in this case, since the covering debt prevents ownership of the estate due to the precedence of debt repayment over inheritance.) (And al-Zayla'i said in Sharh al-Kanz: If a man guarantees with the condition that he not return to the estate, the compromise is valid, because this is a guarantee with the condition of the principal's discharge, which is the deceased, so it becomes a transfer of debt, and the deceased's wealth becomes free of debt, so their disposal of it is permissible. End.) (And al-Tahtawi mentioned narrating from al-Zahiriyya: If the heirs do not guarantee but set aside a specific property for the deceased's debt sufficient to pay it, then they compromise on the remainder as we said, it is permissible. End. al-Maqdisi said: If the set-aside is destroyed, their division must be overturned. End of what is in Hashiyat al-Tahtawi.) (His saying: 'he should not compromise' due to the precedence of the deceased's need, i.e., payment of his debt, so it is makruh, and whether it is tanzihan or tahriman, there is disagreement.) (His saying: 'the basis of qiyas is that debt...' its summary: Debt prevents the heir from ownership, because every part of the estate is occupied by debt, due to no priority of allocation to one part over another, so it becomes like covering debt, preventing entry into the heir's ownership, so division is not permissible before its payment.) (And the basis of istihsan is the necessity of harm to the heirs; its summary: A person is not free from a small debt, so if non-covering debt prevented the heir's ownership, it would lead to hardship or that they never own, so we say they own it to prevent harm to them, except that they set aside from the estate the amount of debt and leave it until the debt is paid, so that they do not need to overturn the division.)
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof from the Book of Compromise. The author states the general rule: if the deceased's debt covers the entire estate (muhit), any compromise (sulh) by the heirs to remove the estate and any division (qisma) of the estate among them are void (batil). This is because the covering debt prevents the heirs from owning the estate; debt repayment takes precedence over inheritance. However, if the debt does not cover the estate (ghayr muhit), the preferred position (istihsan) is that compromise and division are valid before paying the debt, but the amount of the debt is withheld from the division, and the remainder is divided among the heirs. The strict analogy (qiyas) would require withholding the entire estate until payment, but istihsan is applied to avoid hardship to the heirs, as people often have some debt, and postponing all inheritance would cause harm. The passage also notes that compromise is makruh (disliked) before payment due to the priority of the deceased's needs. Exceptions are mentioned: if a guarantor takes responsibility with a condition not to claim against the estate, or if the heirs set aside specific property for the debt, then compromise is valid. This shows the Hanafi preference for istihsan over strict qiyas in muamalat to remove hardship.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): Compromise contract between parties to settle a claim or dispute, especially among heirs regarding estate.
دين محيط (dayn muhit): Debt that covers the entire estate, leaving nothing for heirs.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference departing from strict analogy to avoid hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on compromise (sulh) among heirs when the deceased's debt covers the entire estate?
A) Valid by istihsan
B) Void (batil) because debt prevents ownership
C) Permissible if heirs guarantee payment
D) Valid only after full payment of all debts

Key Takeaway
Debt that covers the entire estate voids compromise and division; for lesser debt, istihsan permits partial division with the debt amount withheld, balancing creditor rights and heir hardship.

Valid Claim Condition for Compromise: Fasid vs Batil
Whether the validity of the claim is a condition for valid compromise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a fasid (corrigible) claim and a batil (incorrigible) claim, and state when a compromise (sulh) is valid or invalid under Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Compromise is valid on a corrigible claim (fasid) but invalid on an incorrigible claim (batil).

This lesson tackles a subtle but crucial nuance in Hanafi contract law: the validity of a compromise (sulh) does not require the underlying claim to be legally perfect. Instead, the key is whether the claim's defect can be corrected. The passage distinguishes between fasid (defective but curable) and batil (incurably invalid) claims, providing concrete examples to guide the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The passage asks: Is the validity of the claim a condition for the validity of the compromise? Determine the author's answer and the reasoning.
2
Understand the fasid/batil distinction
Learn the two categories: fasid (claim can be corrected, e.g., contradiction) vs batil (claim cannot be corrected, e.g., proven freedom or prohibited object).
3
Analyze the examples
Study each example: fasid example (contradictory claim), batil examples (slave proved free, claim for hadd or unlawful wages). Note the ruling for each.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The imams of Khwarazm held that a fasid claim that cannot be corrected invalidates the sulh. Understand the difference from the main position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المسائل المهمة : أنه هل يشترط لصحة الصلح صحة الدعوى أم لا ؟ فبعض الناس يقولون : يشترط ، لكن هذا غير صحيح ؛ لأنَّه إذا ادعى حقاً مجهولاً في دار فصولح على شيءٍ يصح الصلحُ علـى مـا مـر في بـاب الحـقـوق والاستحقاق، ولا شك أنَّ دعوى الحق المجهول دعوى غير صحيحة ، وفي «الذخيرة» مسائل تؤيد ما قلنا. (قوله : هل يشترط لصحة الصلح ... الخ ; ذكر في «التنوير»، وغيره : إنّ الصلح عن الدعوى الفاسدة يصح، وعن الدعوى الباطلة لا يصح، والدعوى الفاسدة ما يمكن تصحيحها، كالدعوى التي وقع فيها التناقض، فيمكن تصحيحها بالتوفيق في التناقض، والدعوى الباطلة لا يمكن تصحيحها. كما إذا ادّعى أنها أمته فقالت : أنا حرّة الأصل، فصالحها عنه، فهو جائز، وإن أقامت بينة على أنها حرّة الأصل فالصلح باطل، إذ لا يمكن تصحيح هذه الدعوى بعد ظهور حرية الأصل، ومن الباطلة الصلح عن دعوى حد، وعن دعوى أجرة نائحة أو مغنية أو تصوير محرم. كما صرحوا به.) (وقال في «الفتاوى البزازيّة»: والذي استقر عليه فتوى أئمة خوارزم أنَّ الصلح عن فاسدة لا يمكن تصحيحها لا يصح، والذي يمكن تصحيحها كما إذا ترك ذكر الحد أو غلط في أحد الحدود يصح. انتهى.)
And among the important issues: Is the validity of the claim a condition for the validity of compromise or not? Some people say it is a condition, but this is not correct, because if one claims an unknown right in a house and is compromised for something, the compromise is valid as previously mentioned in the chapter on rights and claims, and there is no doubt that the claim of an unknown right is an invalid claim. And in al-Dhakhira there are issues supporting what we said. (His saying: 'Is the validity of the claim a condition for the validity of compromise...' It is mentioned in al-Tanwir and others: Compromise based on a defective (fasid) claim is valid, and based on an invalid (batil) claim is not valid. A defective claim is one that can be corrected, such as a claim that contains contradiction, which can be corrected by reconciling the contradiction. An invalid claim cannot be corrected. For example, if he claims she is his slave-girl and she says, 'I am originally free,' and he compromises with her on something, it is permissible. But if she provides evidence that she is originally free, the compromise is invalid, because this claim cannot be corrected after the appearance of original freedom. Among invalid claims are compromise on a claim of hadd (punishment), and on a claim of wages for a wailer, a singer, or prohibited photography, as they explicitly stated.) (And he said in al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya: The settled fatwa of the imams of Khwarazm is that compromise on a defective claim that cannot be corrected is not valid, and on one that can be corrected, such as when he omits mentioning the hadd or errs in one of the limits, it is valid. End.)
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage addresses whether the validity of the claim (sihhat al-da'wa) is a condition for the validity of a compromise (sulh). The author states that some scholars require it, but the correct position is that it is not a condition. The reason is that a person may claim an unknown right (haqq majhul) and then compromise, and such a claim is technically 'invalid' (ghayr sahih), yet the compromise is accepted. To resolve the apparent inconsistency, the text distinguishes between two types of defective claims: fasid (defective) and batil (invalid). A fasid claim is one that can be corrected, e.g., a contradictory claim that can be reconciled. In such a case, compromise is valid because the claim can potentially be made correct. A batil claim is one that cannot be corrected at all, e.g., a claim of ownership over someone who proves to be originally free, or claims for prohibited acts like wages for a wailer or singer. Compromise on a batil claim is invalid. The passage also cites the fatwa of the imams of Khwarazm that the operative ruling is that compromise on a fasid claim that cannot be corrected is not valid. This nuanced view ensures that frivolous claims do not lead to unjust compromises, while allowing flexibility for genuinely uncertain situations.

Key Terms
دعوى فاسدة (da'wa fasida): A claim that contains a defect that can be corrected, e.g., a contradiction resolved by reconciliation.
دعوى باطلة (da'wa batila): A claim that is fundamentally incorrect and cannot be corrected, e.g., a claim contradicted by clear evidence of freedom or a claim for a prohibited act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the mainstream Hanafi position, compromises on which of the following claims are valid?
A) A claim for wages of a wailer (na'iha).
B) A claim based on a contradiction that can be resolved.
C) A claim that the defendant is a slave after she proves free.
D) A claim for a fixed hadd punishment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of a compromise (sulh) depends not on the absolute truth of the claim, but on whether the claim's defect is corrigible (fasid) or incorrigible (batil); only batil claims invalidate the sulh.

Settlement for Invalid Claims is Invalid
Ruling on ṣulḥ when the claim is fundamentally invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a bāṭil claim and a fāsid claim and state the ruling on ṣulḥ for each.

Core Idea
A ṣulḥ over a claim that can never be legally valid is itself invalid.

In this lesson, we explore a key ruling on ṣulḥ (compromise settlement): when the claim at the heart of a dispute is legally invalid, the settlement is also invalid. The text from 'Umda al-Ri'aya clarifies the crucial distinction between a claim that is bāṭil (void, cannot be rectified) and one that is fāsid (defective but potentially curable). Understanding this distinction prevents common errors in applying ṣulḥ contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Nature of the Claim
First, determine what is being claimed. Is it something that Islamic law recognizes as valid property or a legitimate right? For example, wine and pigs are not valid property; a false claim of triple divorce is baseless.
The claim's subject matter is key.
2
Determine if the Claim Can Be Rectified
Consider whether the defect in the claim can be cured. A fāsid claim (e.g., an unspecified right in a house) can be made valid by specifying the right. A bāṭil claim (e.g., claim for wine) can never be made valid.
This is the decisive test.
3
Apply the Ruling: Bāṭil vs Fāsid
If the claim is bāṭil, any ṣulḥ over it is also bāṭil. If the claim is fāsid, ṣulḥ may be valid if the defect is cured at the time of settlement.
The ruling is not absolute; it depends on the claim's category.
4
Work Through the Examples
Review the examples: claim for wine/pig → ṣulḥ invalid; false triple divorce → ṣulḥ invalid; unknown right in a house → ṣulḥ valid because the claim can be rectified.
These illustrate the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الاسبيجابي في «شرح الطحاوي»: الصلحُ الذي . فاسد من قبلهما كما هو إذا ادعى خمراً أو خنزيراً فصالح عن الدعوى على شيء آخر فالصلح فاسد، وكذا إذا ادعت المرأة على زوجها أنها حرمت عليه بالطلاق الثلاث، فصالحها على مال على أن تترك الخصومة، فالصلح فاسد ؛ لأنه غير جائز من قبلهما جميعاً. انتهى.
وإذا عرفت هذا علمت أن الصلح لا يصح عن الدعوى التي لا يمكن تصحيحها بوجه من الوجوه، فقوله : هل يشترط لصحة الصلح صحة الدعوى ؟ لا يوجب كون الدعوى الباطلة كالفاسدة في صحة الصلح عنها ، بل المتبادر أنه أراد الفاسدة لا الباطلة، إذ لا وجه لصحة الصلح عنها ، كالصلح عن دعوى الربا، وأجرة النائحة والمغنية وغيرها.
وما استند إليه الشارح له من أنه إذا ادعى حقاً مجهولاً في دار فصولح على شيء يصح الصلح، لا يفيد الإطلاق، بل إنما صح الصلح فيه ؛ لأن الدعوى يمكن تصحيحها بتعيين الحق المجهول وقت الصلح، فقوله : لكن هذا غير صحيح : أي اشتراط صحة الدعوى لصحة الصلح غير صحيح، ما أراد به؟ إن أراد بعدم الصحة ما يشمل الباطلة، فهو باطل، وإن أراد الفاسد فلم يثبت المخالفة بالدليل، فتأمل.
مي مجاني
And al-Isbijabi said in Sharh al-Tahawi: The settlement that is [dot] invalid from both of them, as when he claims wine or pig and then settles for something else, the settlement is invalid. Likewise, if a woman claims against her husband that she has been made forbidden to him by triple divorce, and he settles with her for money on condition that she abandons the lawsuit, the settlement is invalid; because it is not permissible from both of them. End. And when you know this, you know that settlement is not valid for a claim that cannot be rectified in any way. So his statement: Is the validity of the claim a condition for the validity of settlement? does not necessitate that the invalid claim is like the defective one in the validity of settlement for it. Rather, the apparent meaning is that he intended the defective, not the invalid, since there is no way for the validity of settlement for it, like settlement for a claim of riba, the wage of a wailer, a female singer, and others. And what the commentator relied upon, that if he claims an unknown right in a house and then is settled for something, the settlement is valid, does not imply absolute permissibility. Rather, the settlement is valid therein only because the claim can be rectified by specifying the unknown right at the time of settlement. So his statement: But this is not correct: i.e., conditioning the validity of the claim for the validity of settlement is not correct. What did he intend by it? If he intended by the lack of validity what includes the invalid, then it is false; and if he intended the defective, then the opposition has not been established by evidence. So ponder. Meem yaa meem [unclear].
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement concerning the validity of ṣulḥ (settlement/compromise) when the subject of the claim is itself legally invalid. The author quotes al-Isbijabi from his Sharh al-Tahawi, who gives two examples: 1) claiming wine or a pig and then settling for something else, and 2) a woman falsely claiming triple divorce and settling for money to drop the suit. In both cases, the settlement is invalid because the claim itself is not a valid legal right—wine and pigs are not property, and the false divorce claim has no basis. The commentator then clarifies a crucial distinction: the condition for valid ṣulḥ is that the claim must be 'defective/fasid' (something that could be valid in another context) but not 'invalid/batil' (something that can never be legally sound). For example, a claim for riba (interest) or the wage of a wailer or singer is batil—these are not recognized as valid property rights in Islamic law, so a ṣulḥ over them is void. In contrast, a claim for an unknown right in a house can be rectified by specifying the right at the time of settlement, making the ṣulḥ valid. The commentator refutes the idea that ṣulḥ's validity never depends on the validity of the claim; rather, for batil claims, ṣulḥ is impossible, whereas for fasid claims, it may be possible if the defect can be cured. The passage teaches students to distinguish between different levels of invalidity in claims and their impact on contracts.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-ṣulḥ): A contract of compromise ending a dispute, sometimes functioning like a sale or release.
الدعوى (al-daʿwā): A claim made by one party against another in a dispute.
الباطل (al-bāṭil): A claim that has no legal basis and cannot become valid under any circumstance, e.g., claiming wine or a pig.
الفاسد (al-fāsid): A claim that is defective but could potentially be rectified, e.g., a claim for an unknown right that can be specified later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A claimant says the respondent owes him the value of a pig that was sold. They settle for 200 dirhams. What is the ruling?
A) Valid, because they agreed on a price.
B) Invalid, because the pig is not valid property and the claim is bāṭil.
C) Valid, because the obligation is monetary.
D) Invalid only if the pig was destroyed.

Key Takeaway
A ṣulḥ is only valid if the claim at its core can be legally recognized; a fundamentally void claim makes the settlement void as well.

Owner's Option to Sue Usurper or His Depositee
Intra-Hanafi dispute on owner's right to sue usurper or his depositee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on the owner's right to sue the usurper or his depositee and apply the rule to determine recourse.

Core Idea
The owner may choose whom to sue, but the recourse consequences differ between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

When a usurper (ghasib) deposits the usurped property with another person, the owner has a right to seek compensation. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The core issue is whether suing the first bars recourse against the second, and the reasoning behind it. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze liability in mu'amalat and see how the Hanafi school ultimately settled the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The scenario: a usurper (ghasib) deposits the usurped property with a third party (mustawda'). The owner wants compensation. Who can he sue?
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa says the owner may sue either the usurper or the depositee. But if he sues the usurper first, he cannot later sue the depositee. If he sues the depositee first, the depositee has recourse against the usurper.
The first (usurper) does not become liable until he parts with the property; the second is a trustee with no act of trespass.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say the owner may sue either unconditionally. If he sues the first, no recourse; if he sues the second, the second has recourse. The owner's choice is absolute.
They analogize to the case of a depositee depositing with another, and consider both as having wronged the owner.
4
Analyze the proofs (talil)
Abu Hanifa's proof: the first depositee does not become liable by mere delivery as long as the property remains in his session (majlis). The second is a trustee (amin) and remains so unless he commits an act of trespass. Sahibayn's proof: the first became treacherous by handing over without permission, and the second trespassed by taking possession.
Abu Hanifa uses the analogy of a garment blown by wind into a room; the occupant is not liable as long as he does no act.
5
Note the practical consequence and tarjih
Under Abu Hanifa, the owner must be careful: suing the usurper first waives the right to sue the depositee. Under the Sahibayn, there is no such restriction. Later Hanafi practice prefers the Sahibayn's view, giving the owner full freedom of choice.
The commentary notes 'this is by agreement', indicating the Sahibayn's view became widely accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أودع الغاصبُ ضَمَّن أيهما شاء هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وقالا : يُضَمِّنُ أيهما شاء، فإن ضَمِنَ الأول لم يَرْجِعُ على الثاني ، وإن ضَمِن الثَّاني رجع على الأَوَّل. (ولو أودع الغاصب ضَمَّن أيهما شاء ، هذا بالاتفاق فهما قاسا مُودَعَ المودع على مُودَع الغاصب، فإنّ المودَعَ إذا دَفَعَ إلى الأجنبي صارَ غاصباً المودَعُ الأول فقط، وهذا عند الإمام، وعندها وكذا عند الأئمة الثلاثةِ يخيرُ المالكِ في التضمين، فيضمن أيهما شاء ؛ لأنَّ الأوّل خائن بالتسليم إلى الثاني بغير إذن المالك، والثاني تعدى بالقبض بغير إذنه. فيميلُ المالك إلى أيهما شاء، كالغاصب، وغاصب الغاصب، أو الغاصب و مودع الغاصب، أو الغاصب والمشتري من الغاصب، حيث يُضَمِّنُ المالك أيهما شاء ، فإن ضمن الأول لم يرجع على الثاني ؛ لأنه بالضمان ملكه، فتبين أنه أودع أو باغ ملك نفسه، وإن ضمن المودَعُ أو المشتري رجعَ على الأوّل ؛ لأنه في القبض مغرور من جهة، فيرجع عليه بما ضمن بسببه. ولأبي حنيفة له : إن الأول لا يضمنُ بالدَّفع إلى الثاني ما لم يفارقه ؛ لأن حفظه لا يفوت ما دام في مجلسه، والمالك إنما رضي بحفظه [ورأيه] لا لصورة يده ، فإذا فارق الأول الثاني ضمن بالتضييع بتركُ الحفظ الملتزم له بالعقد، والثاني أمين استمر على الحالة الأولى ولم يوجد منه صنع يجب به الضمان، فلم يضمن، وصار هذا في حقه كثوب ألقته الريح في حجرة فهلك من غير صنعه ، فإنّه لا يضمن باستمراره في حجره ، فكذا هذا، والجامع بينهما أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما لم يكن متعدياً في الابتداء، فلا ينقلب متعدياً من غير إحداث فعل في الانتهاء). [١] قوله لو أودع الغاصب ... الخ ؛ يعني ولو أودع الغاصب المغصوب عند غيره ضَمَّنَ المغصوب منه أياً شاءَ من الغاصب ومودعه إجماعاً ؛ لأنَّ الثاني صار مثل الأول في التلقي منه ابتداءً بعدم إذن المالك، فكذا بقاء. ثم مودع الغاصب إن لم يعلم أنه غاصب يرجعُ على الغاصب قولاً واحداً، وإن
If the usurper deposits (the usurped property), the owner may hold liable whichever he wishes. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He may hold liable whichever he wishes; if he holds the first liable, he does not have recourse against the second; and if he holds the second liable, he has recourse against the first. (If the usurper deposits, he may hold liable whichever he wishes. This is by agreement. They analogized the depositee of the depositee to the depositee of the usurper. According to the Imam, when a depositee hands over to a third party, only the first depositee becomes a usurper. According to them and also according to the three Imams, the owner has a choice of whom to hold liable, so he may hold liable whichever he wishes, because the first became treacherous by handing over without the owner's permission, and the second trespassed by taking possession without his permission. So the owner inclines to whichever he wishes, like the usurper and the usurper's usurper, or the usurper and the usurper's depositee, or the usurper and the purchaser from the usurper, where the owner may hold liable whichever he wishes. If he holds the first liable, he does not have recourse against the second, because by the liability he owns it, so it becomes clear that he deposited or sold his own property. If he holds the depositee or purchaser liable, he has recourse against the first, because he was deceived in taking possession from his side, so he has recourse against him for what he became liable due to him. And Abu Hanifa's proof: The first does not become liable by handing over to the second as long as he has not parted from him, because his (duty of) preservation is not lost as long as he is in his session, and the owner was only satisfied with his preservation and opinion, not for the form of his hand. So when the first parts from the second, he becomes liable due to waste by abandoning the preservation he undertook by contract. The second is a trustee who continues in the initial state and no act requiring liability was committed by him, so he is not liable. This is like a garment that the wind throws into someone's room and it perishes without his act; he is not liable due to its continuing in his keeping. So likewise this. The commonality between them is that neither was a trespasser initially, so he does not become a trespasser without initiating an act in the end.) [1] His statement: 'If the usurper deposits...' means: If the usurper deposits the usurped property with someone else, the owner of the usurped property may hold liable whichever he wishes from the usurper and his depositee, by consensus, because the second became like the first in receiving from him initially without the owner's permission, and likewise in continuance. Then, if the usurper's depositee did not know that he was a usurper, he has recourse against the usurper by one opinion, and if
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the case where a usurper (ghasib) deposits the usurped property with another person (muda'). The owner has the right to seek compensation from either the usurper or the depositee. According to Abu Hanifa, if the owner holds the first (the usurper) liable, he cannot then have recourse against the second (the depositee). However, if he holds the second liable, the second can then have recourse against the first. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they grant the owner an unconditional choice to hold either liable, with the same consequence regarding recourse. The commentary explains that the disciples analogize this case to that of a depositee (muda') who deposits the property with a third person; there, only the first depositee becomes a guarantor. Abu Hanifa's reasoning focuses on the fact that the first depositee does not become liable by mere delivery as long as the property remains in his possession (in his 'majlis'). He emphasizes that the owner's confidence was in the depositee's diligence, not in the physical hand. When the property is handed over and the first depositee parts with it, then he becomes liable for neglect. Meanwhile, the second receives as a trustee (amin) without any wrongful act, so he is not liable. Abu Hanifa supports this with an analogy: a garment blown by wind into someone's home and destroyed without his action does not make him liable. Thus, the depositee of the usurper is not liable if he merely continues holding the property without any fault. The passage then notes that if the depositee of the usurper was unaware of the usurpation, he has recourse against the usurper unanimously.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghāṣib): A person who wrongfully takes property without permission.
مستودع (mustawdaʿ): The person with whom property is deposited (depositee/trustee).
تضمين (taḍmīn): Holding someone liable for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if the owner of a usurped property holds the second (the depositee of the usurper) liable, what happens?
A) The second cannot recover from the first.
B) The second can recover from the first.
C) The owner cannot sue the second at all.
D) The first is automatically freed from liability.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa restricts recourse after suing the first; the Sahibayn give the owner full freedom of choice, and their view is preferred in later Hanafi practice.

Gift with Compensation: Between Gift and Sale
Explains Hanafi view that hibah bi-‘iwad is gift at start and sale at end, rebutting Zufar and Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on hibah bi-‘iwad, identify the thamarah al-khilaf, and evaluate the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i/Zufar objection.

Core Idea
A gift with compensation is a gift at the start and a sale at the end, combining both aspects.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on a gift that is conditional upon the recipient giving something in return (hibah bi-‘iwad). Unlike the Shafi'i school and Imam Zufar, who treat it as a pure sale from beginning to end, the Hanafi school sees it as a hybrid: initially a gift, but upon delivery it transforms into a sale. Understanding this distinction has real consequences for rights like pre-emption and return for defects. We will explore the proofs, the rebuttal, and the practical differences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Explain that hibah bi-‘iwad is a gift at the outset (ibtida’) and a sale at the conclusion (intiha’) after delivery. Before delivery, gift rules apply; after delivery, sale rules apply.
This temporal split is unique to the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opposing view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold it is a pure sale from start to finish because the contract's essence is an exchange for compensation, and contracts are judged by meanings (al-i‘tibar li-l-ma‘ani), not wording.
They cite examples like guarantee becoming hawala when condition is present.
3
Analyze the Hanafi dalil
The contract includes both wording of gift and meaning of sale. The Hanafi principle is to give effect to both aspects (i‘mal al-shabahayn) when possible. Thus the beginning is governed by the wording (gift) and the end by the meaning (sale), analogous to a gift during terminal illness (which is partly gift, partly will).
The text uses qiyas with marad al-mawt to justify the split.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Under the Hanafi view, before delivery the rules of gift apply (no return for defect, no pre-emption, revocable), but after delivery the rules of sale apply (return for defect, option of inspection, pre-emption). Under the opposing view, all sale rules apply from the moment of contract.
This determines when a pre-emptor can claim shuf‘a.
5
Examine intra-Hanafi variation
Note that on the subcase of a father gifting his child's property with compensation, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf deem it invalid (because it starts as a gift, and a father cannot gift from his child's property), while Muhammad deems it valid (because it becomes a sale at the end). The mufta bihi is the majority position.
Also note that using the letter ba‘ instead of ‘ala makes it a pure sale by agreement.

Also, ownership transfer does not allow conditions, so his saying: I gift you this on condition that you gift me that, becomes equivalent to: I make you own this for that. I said: It is carried on two meanings in two situations: like the beginning and the continuation. Ownership transfer does not run the meaning of sale. The principle is that giving effect to two similar aspects, even in one way, is better than neglecting one of them. So inevitably we combine them to the extent possible by acting on both similarities. Thus the beginning of this contract is considered by its wording, so the rulings of gift apply to it, and its end is considered by its meaning, so the rulings of sale apply to it. Second, this contract is conditioned on compensation, and ownership transfer does not allow conditions, so the donor's saying: I gift you this on condition that you gift me that, is in the meaning of: I make you own this for that, so it becomes a sale. Indeed, ownership transfer without compensation and with compensation are considered in what we are discussing in two states, which are the beginning and the end, and this is not impossible. Do you not see that blackness is considered in a body in one state, and whiteness is considered in it in another state.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute explaining the Hanafi position on a gift with compensation (هبة بعوض). The Hanafi school holds that such a contract is a gift at the outset and becomes a sale at its conclusion (after delivery). This means that initially, the rules of gift apply (e.g., requiring possession for completion, no pre-emption, no return due to defect), but after delivery, the rules of sale apply (e.g., the item may be returned due to defect, option of inspection, and pre-emption is established). The opposing view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i is that it is a pure sale from beginning to end, because the essence of the contract is an exchange of property for compensation, and contracts are judged by their meanings, not their wording. The Hanafi response is that the contract includes both the wording of gift and the meaning of sale, so it is possible to consider it as having two aspects: the beginning is governed by the wording (gift) and the end by the meaning (sale). This is analogous to a gift during terminal illness, which is treated as a gift initially (requiring possession) but as a will at the end (limited to one-third of the estate). The Hanafis also argue that the principle of giving effect to both aspects (شبهين) when possible is preferable to neglecting one. They illustrate this with other contracts where wording and meaning diverge, such as a guarantee with a condition of discharge becoming a transfer of debt. The passage also includes a marginal note clarifying that the ruling applies when the compensation is mentioned with the word 'ala; if the letter ba' is used (e.g., 'I gift you this for a thousand'), it is a sale by agreement. Another note discusses the case of a father gifting his child's property with compensation: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf disallow it because it is a gratuitous act at the start, while Muhammad allows it because it becomes a sale at the end. The relied-upon position is the Hanafi one that combines both aspects.

Key Terms
هبة بعوض (hibah bi-‘iwad): A gift that is conditional upon the recipient giving something in return; a conditional gift with compensation.
البيع انتهاءً (al-bay‘ intihā’an): The contract becomes a sale only at its conclusion, i.e., after delivery of the gifted item.
الاعتبار للمعاني (al-i‘tibār li-l-ma‘ānī): Contracts are judged by their intended meanings, not just their literal wording.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a gift with compensation (هبة بعوض) become subject to the rules of sale (e.g., return for defect, pre-emption)?
A) From the moment the contract is concluded
B) After delivery (possession) of the gifted item
C) Only if the compensation is monetary
D) Never, as it remains a gift

Key Takeaway
A gift with compensation (hibah bi-‘iwad) is treated as a gift at the beginning and a sale at the end in the Hanafi school, with corresponding differences in legal consequences.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
The Dual Nature of a Conditional Gift: Gift and Sale Combined
Objection and answer regarding dual nature of conditional gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional gift and explain why it is considered a gift initially and a sale in its continuation.

Core Idea
A gift conditioned on a counter-gift is a gift in inception and a sale in effect.

Can a single contract be both a gift and a sale? At first glance, these two are contradictory — a gift is without compensation, a sale is with compensation. Yet the Hanafi school validates a conditional gift (hibah bi sharṭ) by analyzing it across two temporal stages: its beginning (ibtida') and its end (baqa'). This lesson unpacks the objection, the answer, and the underlying qiyās that allows this dual classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector argues that gift (without compensation) and sale (with compensation) cannot coexist in one contract, and that a condition cannot attach to tamlik (transfer of ownership).
This objection is presented as 'fain qulta...' in the text.
2
Follow the Hanafi response
The commentator answers by splitting the contract into two states: the beginning (ibtida') follows the wording (gift rulings), the end (baqa') follows the meaning (sale rulings). This avoids contradiction by temporal separation.
The principle: giving effect to both aspects (shabahayn) is better than neglecting one.
3
Examine the qiyas analogy
The same dual treatment applies to a gift made during death illness (marad al-mawt): outwardly a gift, but effectively a will limited to one-third. The effective cause (illah) is the combination of a gift aspect (wording) and a testamentary or sale aspect (meaning).
The illah: both contracts involve a conflict between apparent wording and underlying compensation.
4
Understand the condition's validity
The condition of a counter-gift is not a condition of gambling (qimar) or usury (riba), but a valid condition of compensation. It does not negate tamlik; rather, it affects the binding nature after delivery.
The contract is binding only after delivery, like a sale, but revocable before delivery, like a gift.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
Consider a person saying, 'I give you my car on condition that you give me your laptop.' Under Hanafi law, this is valid: initially a gift (revocable before delivery), but after delivery becomes a binding sale (irrevocable).
This distinguishes it from a pure gift (revocable even after delivery) and a pure sale (binding from contract).

If you say: The gift is the transfer of ownership of an asset without compensation, and the sale is transfer with compensation, so how can they be combined? Also, a condition cannot apply to transfer of ownership, so his saying 'I give you this on condition that you give me that' becomes in meaning 'I transfer ownership of this to you in exchange for that.' I say: It carries two meanings in two situations: like the beginning and the continuation, and transfer of ownership does not carry the meaning of sale, and the principle is that giving effect to both aspects, even if partially, is better than neglecting either. So inevitably both are combined as much as possible to give effect to both aspects. Thus the beginning of this contract is considered according to its wording, so the rulings of gift apply to it, and its end is considered according to its meaning, so the rulings of sale apply to it, just as the gift during death illness is apparent as a transfer of ownership immediately as a donation, but its meaning is the meaning of a will, because it entails invalidating the right of heirs attached to it. So its beginning is considered by its wording so that delivery is required, and undivided shares in divisible property are invalidated, and delivery necessitates ownership. And its end is considered by its meaning so that its effectiveness is limited to one-third after paying debts, contrary to the cases cited as evidence, because acting upon both meanings is not possible due to the contradiction between the two rulings, so the wording is disregarded and the meaning is acted upon. Second, this contract is conditioned upon compensation, and condition cannot apply to transfer, so the donor's saying 'I give you this on condition that you give me that' is in the ruling of the meaning 'I transfer ownership of this to you in exchange for that', so it becomes a sale. [2] His saying 'I say': Absolute impossibility of considering contradictory things is denied; yes it is impossible in one place in one state, and here it is not so, for transfer without compensation and with compensation is considered in what we are dealing with in two states, namely the beginning and the end, and this is not impossible. Do you not see that blackness is considered in the body in one state, and whiteness is considered in it in another state. A condition that makes it gambling; as for a condition that makes it a valid compensation in property, transfer does not negate it. So it is a condition initially, considering the wording, so that it does not become a binding sale before delivery, but it is conditioned in the sense of compensation, considering its consequence, so that the rulings of sale are applied to it in the state of continuation, not in the beginning. (1) Its summary: The meaning of its being a transfer without compensation is that it is a transfer without a condition of compensation, not with the condition of no compensation, so it does not negate its being a sale. Also, the condition that negates transfer is a condition containing the meaning of usury or gambling, not any condition. So if he says 'I sold this to you on condition that it becomes your property', the sale is valid. So in our case it is a condition initially, considering the wording, so that it does not become a binding sale before delivery, and a condition in the sense of compensation, considering its consequence, so that the rulings of sale are applied to it in the state of continuation. Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a ruling on conditional gifts (hibah bi sharṭ). The commentator addresses an objection: how can a gift conditioned on a counter-gift be considered both a gift and a sale, when they are contradictory? The answer is that the contract is considered in two temporal states: its beginning (inception) is treated as a gift based on its wording, so delivery (qabḍ) is required and undivided shares are invalidated; its end (continuation) is treated as a sale based on its meaning (the condition of compensation), so the ruling of irrevocability applies after delivery, like a sale. This is analogous to a gift during death illness (marad al-mawt), which is outwardly a gift but effectively a will. The commentator also clarifies that the condition does not violate the nature of transfer of ownership (tamlik) because it is a valid condition of compensation, not a condition of gambling or usury. The principle applied is that giving effect to both aspects (shabahayn) is preferable to neglecting one, even if only partially. This is a refined Hanafi position (mufta bihi) that reconciles the wording and meaning of such contracts.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): Transfer of ownership of an asset without compensation (tamlik al-'ayn bi-la 'iwaḍ).
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; the act of making someone owner.
شرط (shart): A condition attached to a contract; here, the condition of a counter-gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a gift made on condition that the recipient gives a counter-gift?
A) It is invalid because a condition cannot be attached to a gift.
B) It is valid as a gift initially but acquires the binding nature of a sale after delivery.
C) It is considered a pure sale from the moment of the contract.
D) It is a gift only, and the condition is void.

Key Takeaway
A conditional gift (hibah bi sharṭ) is valid as a gift in its inception and as a sale in its continuation after delivery, reconciling the apparent contradiction between gift and sale through temporal separation.
