Gift with Condition of Valid Exchange: Hybrid Rulings
Clarifying that a gift conditioned on a valid exchange is not gambling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a gift conditioned on a valid exchange and explain why it is treated like a sale in its effects after possession.

Core Idea
A gift with a valid exchange condition is not gambling; it is a gift initially but binding like a sale later.

This passage from the Book of Gift clarifies a common confusion: can a gift be conditional on receiving something in return? The Hanafi school answers yes, provided the condition is a valid exchange and not gambling. The ruling is nuanced: the contract is a gift at inception, but its ongoing effects follow the rules of sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditions
The passage distinguishes between a condition that turns the gift into gambling (qimar) and a condition that creates a valid exchange (iwad sahih). Identify which is allowed and which is prohibited.
Only conditions involving riba or gambling are prohibited.
2
Understand the Hanafi definition of gift
A gift is defined as tamlik bi la 'iwad, meaning 'transfer without compensation.' But the text clarifies this means without a condition of compensation, not that compensation is impossible. So a condition for a valid exchange does not contradict the nature of a gift.
The phrase 'without compensation' is about the condition, not the actual receipt of value.
3
Analyze the dual consideration
Initially, the condition is considered only as wording (shart ibtida') so the gift remains revocable before possession. But ultimately, because the condition implies an exchange, the rulings of sale apply after possession (e.g., irrevocability, options like khiyar al-'ayb).
Hence the gift becomes binding (lazim) once possession is taken.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Take a concrete example: Zayd gives his car to Amr on condition that Amr gives Zayd his laptop. After Zayd delivers the car, the gift becomes binding. If Amr fails to give the laptop, Zayd can seek legal remedy as in a sale.
The condition must be a valid exchange of property, not services or uncertain items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الهبة باب الرجوع فيها

فيه شرط يصير به قماراً، فأما الشرط الذي يصيرُ به في المال عوضاً صحيحاً، فالتمليك لا ينافيه، فيكون شرطاً ابتداءً اعتباراً للعبارة حتى لا يصير كالبيع لازماً قبل القبض، لكنهُ شُرط بمعنى العوض اعتباراً لما يؤول إليه حتى يتوفر عليه أحكام البيع حالة البقاء لا في الابتداء.

وعدم جريان الشرط في التمليك مطلقا ممنوع أيضاً، نعم لا يجري فيه شرط يصيرُ به قماراً، وهاهنا ليس كذلك، فإنَّ الشرط الذي يصير به في المال عوضاً صحيحاً لا ينافيه التمليك، فيكون شرط ابتداء حفظاً لكلام العاقل البالغ عن إلغاءه، حتى لا يصير به كالبيع لازماً قبل القبض، لكنه شرط بمعنى العوض اعتباراً لما يؤول إليه، حتى ينفذ عليه أحكام البيع حالة البقاء لا في الابتداء.

(۱) حاصله : إن معنى كونها تمليكاً بلا عوض كونها تمليكاً بلا شرط عوض لا بشرط عدم العوض، فلا ينافي كونه بيعاً، وأيضاً : إن الشرط المنافي للتمليك شرط فيه معنى الربا أو القمار لا مطلق الشرط، حتى لو قال : بعت هذا منك على أن يكون ملكاً لك صح البيع، فيكون ما نحن فيه شرطاً ابتداء نظراً إلى العبارة حتى لا يصير كالبيع لازماً قبل القبض وشرطاً بمعنى العوض نظرا إلى ما يؤول إليه حتى توفر أحكام البيع حالة البقاء. ينظر: «الدرر»(٣: ٢٢٤).
Book of Gift, Chapter on Revocation in It. It contains a condition that makes it gambling. As for the condition that makes it a valid exchange in the property, transfer of ownership does not contradict it; so it is a condition initially, considering the wording, so that it does not become binding like a sale before possession. But it is conditioned in the sense of exchange, considering its ultimate outcome, so that the rulings of sale apply to it in the state of continuance, not in the beginning. And the absolute non-application of conditions in transfer of ownership is also rejected. Yes, a condition that makes it gambling does not apply to it, and here it is not like that. For the condition that makes it a valid exchange in the property does not contradict transfer of ownership; so it is an initial condition to preserve the speech of a sane adult from being nullified, so that it does not become binding like a sale before possession. But it is a condition in the sense of exchange, considering its ultimate outcome, so that the rulings of sale are implemented on it in the state of continuance, not in the beginning. (1) Its gist: The meaning of it being a transfer without compensation is that it is a transfer without a condition of compensation, not with the condition of no compensation. So it does not contradict it being a sale. Also, the condition that contradicts transfer of ownership is a condition involving interest or gambling, not an absolute condition. Thus if someone says: 'I sold this to you on condition that it becomes your property,' the sale is valid. So our case is a condition initially, considering the wording, so that it does not become like a sale binding before possession, and a condition in the sense of exchange, considering its ultimate outcome, so that the rulings of sale apply in the state of continuance. See: al-Durar (3:224).
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Gift, Chapter on Revocation, discusses the effect of a condition in a gift contract. The author begins by distinguishing between two types of conditions: one that turns the gift into gambling (qimar), and one that creates a valid exchange (iwad sahih). The ruling is that a gift with a condition for a valid exchange does not invalidate the gift as a transfer of ownership (tamlik). Rather, the condition is considered initially as a mere statement (shart ibtida'), so the gift does not become binding like a sale before possession (qabd). However, because the condition ultimately implies a return of consideration, the contract is treated like a sale in its ongoing effects (ahkam al-bay') — for example, the gift becomes binding after possession, and options like khiyar al-'ayb may apply. The author then refutes the absolute denial of conditions in gifts, clarifying that only conditions containing riba or gambling are prohibited, not all conditions. The footnote (hasilah) further explains that 'gift without compensation' means without a condition of compensation, not that compensation is impossible. Thus a gift with a valid exchange condition is not a contradiction; it is a hybrid: a gift initially, but a sale in its lasting effects.

Key Terms
تمليك بلا عوض (tamlik bi la 'iwad): A transfer of ownership without any compensation stipulated; the text clarifies this means without a condition of compensation, not that compensation is impossible.
شرط عوض (shart 'iwad): A condition in the gift that requires the recipient to give something in return; this condition is valid and turns the contract into a hybrid.
قمار (qimar): Gambling; a condition that introduces uncertainty and risk into the transaction, making it invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of a condition for a valid exchange (shart 'iwad sahih) in a gift?
A) It renders the gift invalid because a gift must be without any condition.
B) The gift is valid but remains revocable even after possession.
C) The gift is initially a gift but its later consequences follow the rules of sale, including irrevocability after possession.
D) The condition is ignored, and the gift remains a pure gift with no sale-like effects.

Key Takeaway
A gift with a valid exchange condition is a hybrid: it is a gift initially, but binding like a sale after possession.

The Permissibility of Hiring a Cupper
Hanafi response to the hadith against the cupper's earnings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on hiring a cupper and evaluate the evidence used to support it against the Zahiriyya objection.

Core Idea
The Prophet's own payment to a cupper permits the contract, despite the hadith calling it 'khabith'—which means naturally low, not legally forbidden.

This lesson addresses a classical Hanafi dispute over hiring a cupper (hajjam). The Zahiriyya school insists such earnings are prohibited based on a hadith calling them 'khabith'. However, the Hanafi school, relying on stronger evidence and interpretive principles, affirms the contract's validity. Understanding how the Hanafis reconcile these texts reveals key aspects of their methodology.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling on hiring a cupper and the primary evidence cited (hadith of Ibn Abbas).
The Hanafi position is that it is permissible.
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
Recognize the Zahiriyya position and the hadith of Rafi' ibn Khadij that they use to prohibit the hiring.
The Zahiriyya rely on the word 'khabith' and the prohibition of 'asb al-tays'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand the two Hanafi arguments: (1) the prohibition hadith is abrogated, and (2) 'khabith' means natural disgust, not legal prohibition.
The Hanafi response also asserts the superiority of Ibn Abbas's narration over Rafi's.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
Determine why the Hanafi position is preferred (mufta bihi) based on textual strength and scholarly reliability.
Al-Ithqani's statement that Ibn Abbas is superior in precision and jurisprudence.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis permit the contract and consider the earnings halal; Zahiriyya consider it invalid.
This affects the validity of the ijarah contract for cupping.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إجارة الحجام فلما رواه البخاري له مسنداً إلى ابن عباس : «إنه احجتمَ وأعطى الحجام أجرة » ) ، ولو علم كراهة لم يعطه، وفي رواية «السنن»: ولو علمه خبيثاً لم يعطه ؛ لأنه كما لا يحلّ لأحدٍ أكل الحرام لا يحل له دفعه إلى غيره ليأكله. وقالت الظاهرية : لا تجوز هذه الإجارة ؛ لأنه نهـي عـن عـسـب التيس ، وكسب الحجام، وقفيز الطحان ، وروي عن رافع بن خديج له أن رسول الله ، قال : «كسب الحجّام خبيث ، وثمن الكلب خبيث ، ومهر البغي خبيث . والجواب : إن حديث النهي منسوخ بما روي أنه قال له رجل: «إن لي عيالاً وغلاماً حجاماً ، فأطعم عيالي من كسبه ، قال : نعم ، ذكر الزيلعي في «شرح الكنز» هذا الحديث. وحديث الخبث محمول على الكراهة طبعاً من طريق المروءة، لما فيه الخساسة والدناءة، قال الإثقَانِيُّ : على أنا نقول : رواية رافع الله ليس كابن عباس له في الضبط والإتقان والفقه، فيعمل بحديث ابن عباس له دونه. انتهى.
As for hiring the cupper, it is based on what Bukhari narrated from Ibn Abbas that he (the Prophet) had himself cupped and gave the cupper his fee. If he had considered it disliked, he would not have given it. In a narration of Sunan: and if he had considered it filthy, he would not have given it; because just as it is not permissible for anyone to eat the forbidden, it is also not permissible for him to give it to another to eat. The Zahiriyya said: This hiring is not permissible because the Prophet forbade the stud fee of the stallion, the earnings of the cupper, and the measure of the miller. And it is narrated from Rafi' ibn Khadij that the Messenger of God said: The earnings of the cupper are filthy, the price of the dog is filthy, and the wage of the prostitute is filthy. The response: The hadith of prohibition is abrogated by what is narrated that a man said: I have a family and a slave who is a cupper, so may I feed my family from his earnings? He said: Yes. Al-Zayla'i mentioned this hadith in Sharh al-Kanz. And the hadith of filth is interpreted as natural dislike from the standpoint of manliness, because of the baseness and lowliness in it. Al-Ithqani said: We say: the narration of Rafi' is not like that of Ibn Abbas in terms of precision and reliability and jurisprudence, so we act on the hadith of Ibn Abbas rather than his.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi position on hiring a cupper, which is permissible, in contrast to the Zahiriyya school which prohibits it. The Hanafi proof is the hadith of Ibn Abbas that the Prophet himself was cupped and paid the cupper, indicating permission. The Zahiriyya rely on a hadith of Rafi' ibn Khadij calling the cupper's earnings 'khabith' (filthy). The Hanafi response offers two arguments: (1) The prohibition hadith is abrogated by a later hadith where the Prophet approved feeding one's family from a cupper's earnings. (2) The term 'khabith' refers to natural disgust due to the menial nature of the work, not a legal prohibition. Additionally, the narration of Ibn Abbas is preferred due to his superior reliability. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the permissibility of hiring a cupper, and the contract is valid.

Key Terms
حجام (ḥajjām): A person who performs cupping (hijamah), a traditional medical practice.
خبيث (khabīth): Filthy; in this context, it is interpreted as naturally disgusting rather than legally prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis interpret the hadith 'khabith' as natural dislike rather than prohibition?
A) Because the word 'khabith' always means natural disgust
B) Because it conflicts with the explicit action of the Prophet paying the cupper
C) Because the hadith is weak
D) Because there is a consensus on permissibility

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits hiring a cupper, based on the Prophet's own practice, and interprets contrary evidence as abrogated or referring to natural lowliness, not legal prohibition.
